Literary Notes .
Of Translation.
The suggestion that Bulletin readers
should make English versions of a touching
little poem by Maeterlinck lias not met a
quite satisfactory response. Of the dozen or
so copies sent in—some by hands which
should be really competent—hardly one
translates the French accurately, and several
have had to wander far from the pathetic
original in search of rhyme and expression.
Some gave a fair paraphrase of the spirit of
the verse—which is not translation. Some
gave a fair rendering of the letter—which is
not translation. None was able so to mingle
letter and spirit as, allowing for the genius
of the languages, to give the exact force of
the original.
* * * *
Yet the original is not unapt for transla-
tion. Its key-note is simplicity—a simplicity
of which we have the equivalent in many an
old ballad. A verse like—
O where have you been, my long, long love,
This long seven years and inair?
O I m come to seek my former vows
Ye granted me before.
—gets very near to the rhythm and tone of
“Et s’il revenait.” Only the flavour of
modernity is lacking, for Maeterlinck, how-
ever unaffected his art, cannot throw off the
garment of the centuries. But translators
wear this too.
* * * *
The best version received comes from
“Austin South,” and is here printed with
the original :
ET STL REVENAIT.
Efc s’il revenait un jour
Que faut-il lui dire?
—Dites-lui qu’on l’attendib
Jusqu’a s’en mourir. . .
Et s’il demaude oh vous 6tes
Que faut-il repondre?
—Donnez-lui mon anneau d’or
Sans rien lui repondre. . .
Et s’il m’interroge encore
Sans me reconnnltre?
—Parlez-lui emmun line soeur
II souffre peut etre. . .
Et s’il vent savoir pourquoi
La salle est deserte?
Mon rez-lni la laiiipe eteinte
Et la porte ouverte. . .
Et s’il m’interroge alors
Sur la derniere heure?
Dites-lui que j'ai souri
De peur qu’il ne pleure.
This can only be considered a fair attempt.
The note is unforced, and the spirit is well
caught ; but the thought 'is not accurately
given, and the chanson-music of the original
might be more nearly reached. “Till
pale Death remembered the life that
Love forgot” is a pretty phrase, but it is
not the phrase of the original, and is doubt-
fully as strong. “He was looked and
longed for till death came of longing ” .seems
more natural, and it is what Maeterlinck
meant. (By-the-by, with the exception of
“L.M.” and doubtfully of “ R.K.D.,” every
translator slurred the significant en, and
missed the meaning of these two lines.)
“Pale Death” is rather trite; and the “I
waited ” is too explicit—another well-patro-
nised gulf. The original is intentionally
vague : if the enquirer learns that she is dead
in the first stanza the remainder is all
anti-climax. (“Till my dying day” is a
favorite fourth line, and in the fifth the
intelligent stranger is made to ask where
the dead one is.) Passing some verbal
cavils, the unwarranted “too,” and the
expletives in the fourth verse, “Austin
South ” circumvents rather neatly, a miscon-
struction of the last two lines, over which
everybody else but “ R.K.D.” (and possibly
“L.M. ’) tumbled gallantly. The stranger
isn’t to be told that “she smiled lest he
should weep ” —which, in elegant vernacular,
“give - the game away ;” but, lest he should
weep, lie is to be told she smiled. The error
is tempting, certainly; but translators should
have strength to resist temptation.
* * * *
For the rest, tolerable versions come from
R.K.D. (hardly close enough, and alternate
weak ending would have helped melody) ;
L.M. (poetic, and rivalling preferred version,
but metre xxot so good equivalent, and expres-
sions like “apace,” “darkened face,” seem
strained) ; Vie Manquee (sympathetic, rather
stiff): L.B. .(rhythmic, but mistranslated, and
“ lest that,” “ dead lamp,” not permissible);
L. L. mistranslated; “ without rememb-
rance,' and “late ” won’t do) ; B.G. (sympa-
thetic, rhythmic, some stiffness). Bad ver
sions come from J. S, ; T. C. : R. H. ; E. S.
Mach. ; W. M.
* * * »
The art of translation is to reproduce th
precise impression left bythe author—no mar
and no less. As nearly as possible a jus
equivalent should be found for (in order o:
importance) his thought, his tone or colo
or atmosphere, and his mode of expres
sion. A translator-genius like Francis Ma
hony can do this all but completely, work
ing with modern languages ; but in
the turning of ancient to modem, or vie
versa, absolute perfection is visibly unattainec
by the ablest. There will always be room for
the play of idiosyncrasy in debate whether
translator should put author before reader or
reader before author, when to harmonise
their claims proves impossible ; whether, foi
example, Conington’s “iEneid ” is better than
Dryden’s, or whether Fitzgerald’s liberties
with Omar would, on the whole, please or
displease that illustrious tentmaker. Yet
the end is clear, whatever difference there
may be as to comparative value or justice
of means. And people with a little know-
ledge, a little alertness, a little scope, and
little literary knack, may get from transla-
tion an intellectual pleasure which stimulates
without exhausting.
* * * *
It is with a full comprehension of the in-
discretion involved that a rather wooden
application of this commentary is permittee
to follow :
IF HE COME BACK
If some day he come back
How then speak of you ?
—Tell him he was hoped for
Till deferred hope slew.
If he ask Where stay you
What should I reply?
—Give him my gold ring, and
Give it silently.
Yet—he may not know me—
If he question more?
—As a sister soothe him
Lest his heart be sore.
If he seek to learn why
Desolate the hall ?
—Show the lamp extinguished,
Door thrown open all.
And if then he question
llow you fell asleep ?
—Say I parted smiling :
Say this, lest he weep.
The facts in the case of Fisher Unwin—
subject to that publisher’s correction. The
literary and artistic contents of The Bulletin
are covered by copyright in Britain and Aus-
tralia, and where the International Copyrights
Act applies. This copyright is habitually
waived in favor of contributors, on condition
that republication shall be accompanied
with an acknowledgment of original publica-
tion in The Bulletin. There was such a
waiver in the case of Louis Becke’s stories,
and such a condition. In his first book,
“ By Reef and Palm,” a collection of Bulle-
tin stories published by Fisher Unwin, the
condition was complied with. In his second
book, also a collection of short stories, many
of them from The Bulletin, soon to be pub-
lished by Fisher Unwin, the condition (so
The Bulletin is informed) is not complied
with. More—according to the statement
of the editor of the Sketch, he has been
led fo publish without acknowledgment
several of these Bulletin stories, in the
belief that they had never previously
appeared. Probably the editor of the
English Illustrated Magazine, who has
also published some of Becke’s stories
previously printed in Australia, and other
editors, have been in similar ignorance.
The fact, therefore, of previous publication,
in The Bulletin and elsewhere, of Becke’s
stories, seems to have been suppressed (not
by Becke) for the sake of making an extra
profit on the forthcoming volume ; since the
editor of the Sketch writes that he would
not have printed the stories had he been
aware of their previous Bulletin publica-
tion, and probably other editors would
take the same course. Again, Becke
and Walter Jeffery, of Sydney Town
and Country Journal, collaborated in a
long story—“A First Fleet Family’’—sold
to Fisher Unwin as a joint production. This
story has been permitted to appear in the
Illustrated London News as “by Louis
Becke ” (only), without Becke’s knowledge
or consent. Apparently, therefore, the name
of Jeffery, as a comparatively unknown man,
has been suppressed in the idea that it would
be disadvantageous to the sale or appreciation
of the story. Who suppressed it ? Fisher
Unwin ? What does the editor of the
Illustrated London N ews say ?
“ The History of Mankind,” by F. Ratzel,
is being published by Macmillan in monthly
parts. It is well illustrated, but the treat-
ment seems heavy, and the part-purchase
system is little appreciated in Australia.
Fergus Hume’s “Expedition of Captain
Flick ” (Macmillan) is a crudish copy of the
new models in adventurous romance.
THE POET AND THE ANARCHISTS.
I sought the sweetest singer of our day,
Our Chaucer with his fair and golden words,
And tales of old Greek gods, and Vikings brave
(Oh ! City of wild contrasts, meetings strange !)
I found him in a mean, ill-lighted room,
Perched on a platform, dressed in roughest serge,
Prating of Art to alien Anarchists !
Beside him sat a lank and weary girl
Strumming the Marseillaise; whilst noisome lads
Sold broadsheets of Chicago murderers !
In front sat women, frowsy, foul, and loud,
Who mocked at kings, with angry ruffled brows,
And clenched t.heir fists and cursed ; and one
Unwound her swathing neck-wrap like a snake,
Then damned the Queen, and spat upon the floor.
The Poet from his written page, prosed on ;
And when he spoke of Art, a silence fell,
Broken by rustling broad-sheets, but whene’er
He jibed at lords, or dubbed the bourgeois thieves,
The building shook with yells and stamping feet.
Nowhere else does Mr. Martin reach this
pitch of intensity. Here is ore from his
more characteristic vein :—-
WHEN A MAN’S SINGLE.
I ask but little from the Gods—
Good health and friends to cheer;
And, just as one of Fortune’s nods,
Three hundred pounds a year !
I’d let the Politician rave,
The Theologian fight;
I’d let the Sexton dig a “grave
And bury both to-night.
I’d rather sing the Poet's song
Than do the Hero’s deeds,
And gaily go through life along,
Unmindful of the creeds.
And many a pleasant hour I’ll pass
With her so sweet and chaste—
My charming little winsome lass,
My arm about her waist.
And when my time at last has come,
And fled is all my mirth,
Take me as one who seeks his Home,
And lay me i’ the earth.
Not very new or strong, maybe ; but un-
forced and readable.
Phil May gives the volume a clever frontis-
piece, and Hume Nisbet contributes illus-
trations which are better than his stories.
A. B. Walkley contributes to Cosmo polls
some competent criticism on current English
drama :
The Bible episode in “The Notorious Mrs.
Ebbsmith ” is not only not an inevitable, it is not
even a plausible, sequel to the first two acts ; the
very name of “episode” which my pen spon-
taneously found for it is its condemnation. The
incident is not in causal relationship with the rest
of the play ; Agnes’s moral struggle, the crisis
through which she is passing, lies far away from
formal religion. Ibsen, I make no doubt, is partly
to blame. The stove into which Agnes throws
the Bible is the stove into which Hedda threw
Lovborg’s manuscript; but there is this vast
difference, that Hedda’s act is consistent with her
character, an outward and visible sign of her soul
state, while Agnes’s is precisely the reverse. Mr.
Pinero has simply reproduced a stage effect the
externals of the thing, and in doing so has de-
parted utterly from the spirit of his model If
the burning of the Bible is false enough, what is
its rescue? Well, I cannot resist the uncomfort-
able suspicion that Mr. Pinero has here not been
thinking of his play at all, but of his public.
His rescued Bible I feel is a sop to that fetish-
worship of ‘ the book I mean the material,
tangible thing of paper, print, and leather—which
still survives in certain classes of our community.
Now that a man of Mr. Pinero’s calibre should
spoil the crisis of his play in order to make a con-
cession to Ciapham is, indeed, something which
gives us furiously to think.” And this is not
ail. lirom one British fetish we are hurried to
another. Ihe Church intervenes not as the
Church, not as a spiritual comforter, but as an
wlnctl confers “respectability.” In
Ihe Benefit of the Doubt” we have the same
conclusion, though there it is even more flagrantly
inadequate and impertinent. How ludicrous is
the disproportion between the fierce soul-struggles
of the unhappy people in this play, and the short
cut to social salvation provided by a bishop with
a house in town for the season !
FROM A FAR COUNTRY.
What shall I say if he, some day returning 1 ,
Shall seek thee, knowing not?
Tell him I waited—till pale Death remembered
The life that Love forgot.
If he should ask to know thy place of dwelling,
What shall my answer be?
Give him the ring of gold from off my finger,
Give it him—silently !
But if, as with a stranger, he still questions,
Say what then shall I do?
Speak to him very gently, as a sister,
Perchance he suffers, too !
And if he ask why silent and deserted
The halls so bright before?
Answer no word, but show the lamp extin-
guished,
The widely-opened door.
And lest, perchance, he ask of that last hour—
What message must I keep ?
Smile in his face, and say I parted smiling 1
Yes, smile—lest he should weep !
AUSTIN SOUTH.
A. P. Martin’s Verses.
Australians’ knowledge of Arthur Patch
ett Martin consists mostly of an impressioi
that sometime m the dim past he was f
pleasant-natured Melb. telegraph-officia
with a fair literary knack ; that he went to
London and turned his literary knack anc
his knowledge of Australia to account; tha:
he wrote the life of Robert Lowe ; and t-hai
now he occupies an easy chair and slippers
in the ranks of London dilettanti. Mr.
Martin’s book of collected verses sup-
plies opportunity for exact admeasure-
ment of a talent which the new gener-
ation has taken largely on trust. It
proves to be a thin, not unpleasing talent —
an agreeable vin ordinaire rather than a
piquant sherry, a substantial burgundy, or a
sparkling champagne. The Australian verses
—presumably earlier—are decidedly weak ;
the London lines—apparently later—are a
good deal better : a light, occasional verse
deficient in wit and poignancy, but with suffi-
cient fluency and sentiment to be readable.
Some lines have a strong flavor of Locker-
Lampson ; others of O. W. Holmes ; but, on
the whole, the echoes are distant enough to
pass muster. The author seems to have a
good eye for subject-points ; his execution
falls rather short of conception. Here is the
strongest piece in the book :
Reprints.
Itrtj aie scant.
Tread lightly, she is near
Under the snow ;
Speak gently, she can hear
The daisies grow.
All her bright golden hair
Tarnished with rust,
She that was young and fair
Fallen to dust.
Lily-like, white as snow,
She hardly knew
She was a woman, so
Sweetly she grew.
Coffin-board, heavy stone,
Lie on her breast;
I vex my heart alone,
She is at rest.
Peace, peace, she cannot heat
Lyre or sonnet :
All my life’s buried there,
Heap earth upon it.
Oscar Wilde.
Tears for my lady dead,
Heliodore !
Salt tears and strange to shed,
Over and o’er;
Go tears and low lament,
Fare from her tomb,
Wend where my lady went,
Down through the gloom.
Sighs for my lady dead,
Tears do I send,
Love long remembered,
Mistress and friend 1
Sad are the songs we sing,
Tears that we shed ;
Empty the gifts we bring—
Gifts to the dead !
Ah, for my flower, my Love*
Hades hath taken !
Ah, for the dust above,
Scattered and shaken !
Mother of blade and grass,
Earth, in thy breast,
Lull her that gentlest was
Gently to rest.
Andrew Lang, after Meleager.
Strew on her roses, roses,
And never a spray of yew !
In quiet she reposes;
Ah, would that I did too!
Her mirth the world required ;
She bathed it in smiles of glee.
But her heart was tired, tired,
And now they let her be.
Her life was turning, turning,
In mazes of heat and sound;
But for peace her soul was yearning
And now peace laps her round.
Her cabin’d, ample spirit,
It fluttered and fail’d for breath.
To-night it doth inherit
The vasty halls of death.
Matthew Arnold.
It is incorrect to say that the efforts of J.
C. Neild to write the worst verse ever pro-
duced in Australia have been completely
successful. There is an educated blackfellow
at Cooroopajerup (Vic.) some of whose com-
positions are distinctly inferior to Mr
Neild’s.
I think that a story should be conceived and
narrated for the story’s sake, as a disinterested
representation of life, of character, of events. It
should have no more “message,” or ulterior pur-
pose (as of discouraging religion, inciting sedition,
promoting loyalty, persuading us to marry our
deceased wives’ sisters, or to abstain from marriage
as an impure and odious invention), than have the
plays of Shakespeare, or “Old Mortality” or
“ Manon Leseaut. Andrew Lang.

pole the plucky Suffolk girl whose blood still

night ride on the horse which she was transported
tor stealing. Here are the last stanzas

M'Ginty’s Happy Thought .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
M'Ginty’s the fair and O’Ry an the wise,
a l i ley l , set ou^ — 80 they did —for a drink.
And they wanted to drink over head, ears and
eyes,
But they’d not the least taste of the jink—
ihey were sadly in want of the jink.
Said M'Ginty, “ My throat is as dhry as a brick.”
Said O’Ryan, “Faith mine is the same.”
baid M'Ginty, “ But sure we could alter it quick
If we took a deep dhrink at the sthrame—
Sweet bad luck to the tasthe of the sthrame!”
Said O’Ryan, “We’re here at the back of God-
speed,
And the divil a penny we own ;
Faith, ’tis hard wid our tongues out for whisky
indeed,
To be threatened wid wather alone—
Raw wather’s the divil alone.
“ And the doctors all say that ’tis full of disase,
Chock-full of young divils with tails.”
Says O’Ryan, “ I’ve dodged them the most of my
days,
But at last here their father prevails—
Yis, the divil their father prevails.”
But Mac gev a bounce and he shouted “ Hurroo !
Here’s a mighty good thing I’ve discerned •
You mesmerise me and I’ll mesmerise you,
And we’ll think that the wather has turned,
Aye, to best Irish whisky has turned.”
In a minute ’twas done, and the mesmerised pair
At once to the river ran down.
And ever since that hypnotising affair,
They’re the envy of all in the town :
They’re the two drunkest men in the town.
Zef.
The Rhodes-Jameson faction has re-chris-
tened Johannesburg “ Judasburg.”
Cecil Rhodes, no orator, is terse and pithy
in his remarks. For instance, at a meeting of the
De Beers Diamond Co., a shareholder asked what
would be the effect of the large output of dia-
monds, and was it not possible that fashion might
change and run on emeralds and other precious
stones? (Bachelor) Rhodes : “So long as women
are vain and men are foolish, so long will dia-
monds continue to be sought after ” —the case in a
nutshell. By the bye, it is whispered that the
management of the great enterprises with which
Rhodes is connected has caused such a strain upon
him that he flies pretty extensively to—lemonade #
This is the Polynesian garotte, recently
imported from Ambrym (New Hebrides), a
favorite mission vineyard : The garotters
find their victim away from the village,
garotte him, and, while he is unconscious, the
murderer places him on his face on the ground,
and, planting his knee on the victim’s back, twists
his neck and crushes his larynx, yet so as not to
dislocate the cervical vertebrae. When conscious-
ness returns the victim cannot speak, and knows
not the actual murderer. He is only able to crawl
or totter back to bed, and dies in the course of 12
or 24 hours of the effusion of blood from the rup-
tured vessels into the lungs, spine, and cranium.
[But why does the “simple savage ” bother with
these complications? Why not kill the man
right out? —Ed. B.]
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
The paternal Government lately constructed
a well for the use of the inhabitants of a certain
Westralian town. But it didn’t take the trouble
to provide a road to the well: the only access is
via somebody’s private property, and the owner
thereof charges Is. a time for the privilege of
walking across his ground to the water-supply.
An ordinary family finds the bill run into 2s. or
3s. a day for the right to hump its bucket of
water across this Goth’s paddock, and it is neces-
sary to be confoundedly civil to him as well; any-
body who makes remarks about his conduct is
liable to have the price raised to 2s. 6d. The town
is reported to be “ indignant,” but the miserable,
law-abiding place hasn’t enough public spirit to
turn out en masse, and haner this capitalist by the
feet from a tree, and charge him ten guineas for
being let down again.
Cairns Post, reporting a kanaka-grog-
selling case, alleges :
Sam Eromango, a native of the New Hebrides, was then
placed'in the box. In reply to questions by the P.M., he
said he did not swear on the Bible, but that he would go
to hell in the event of his telling a lie.
The P.M.—“ What’s more, you will go to gaol.”
Here is official confirmation of the decadence of
the consoling belief in hell in the torrid North.
Cairns is so hot just now that a place only a little
hotter —if it is hotter —has no terrors. Conse-
quently hell has to take a back seat. The warm
hell was originally invented for cold climates, and
if the clergy want to keep their grip on N.Q. they
should promptly supply another brand guaranteed
15,000 degs. colder than the North Pole. Yet that
would probably seem like Heaven to the Cairns
man. Decidedly, it is a hard matter to keep the
end of the dogmatic log up nowadays.

SATURDAY, MARCH 7. 1396.
Price, 6d.
THE DEMON-FACED CRAB.
IN JAPANESE WATERS.
This interesting creature has a way some-
what less in size than a florin in the lar-
ger specimens, and on the carapace, sculp-
tured in with unmistakable fidelity, is the
face of a Mongolian, a diabolical, leering
Jap., With eyfes, nose, mouth, high cheek
bones, and wrinkled forehead, fully and
clearly defined. Nowhere in the uncon-
scious delineation of nature is there so
finished a picture of the human face as
Stares weirdly from the carapace of this
grotesque sea thing.
fro"j
%-x^k
THE DEMON-PACED LAND-CKAB, AS SKETCHED BY a REPENTANT FREETRADER.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling —with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every iveekfor
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere

to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.

Archibald. .
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. ivhen
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in lilacle and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, MARCH 7, 1896.
The Great Bogus Land.
Westralia is for the present this continent’s
land of promise. On a smaller scale Para-
guay was its land of promise a few brief
years ago, but Paraguay is under a cloud
now from which it seems unlikely to
emerge. Various other places have also
been Australia’s land of promise at pre-
vious stages of its history, and Australia
itself was for many years—and to some
extent is still—regarded by a large number
of people in Europe as a species of Canaan
in the far distance. It is one of the idiosyn-
cracies of man in general that he always
believes there is some blissful spot on this
earth which will furnish him with happiness
and wealth and prosperity if he can only get
there. What kind of place it may be matters
nothing ; any barren, sun-blasted waste in
the land of GoD-forgotten will do to make an
Elysium out of, if it is only a long way off.
Therefore, Westralia, the saddest, poorest,
meanest, most utterly hopeless region to be
found between the Great Australian Bight
and Carpentaria and from the Leeuwin to the
Hauraki Gulf is serving the purpose at pre-
sent. The exodus to Westralia goes on more
or less persistently from every other province
in Australasia, yet hardly anything has
occurred so far to justify a movement on
such a scale, and nearly everything that could
happen to show the utter folly of it has
happened.
* * * *
Westralia is, even in this boom-time, the
least promising province of the seven. It
has very little agriculture, very little in the
way of pastoral industry, and hardly the
semblance of anything that can be called
manufactures. The total area under crops is
about half that of little Tasmania, and about
one-twenty-fourth that of Victoria, yet Vic-
toria is barely one-twelfth the size. The
Swan River province covers one-third the
entire area of Australia, and the whole
annual value of its pastoral, mining, agricul-
tural and manufacturing products is not
much over one-sixtieth of the production
of the rest of the continent. In proportion
to its size, it produces about 200 times less
than Victoria, even with its alleged gold-
yield reckoned in. Westralia, apart, from its
auriferous deposits, has absolutely nothing
to recommend it. And as a gold-producing
province, surprising as it may seem to many in
view of the boom that has been raging for
three years past, it is in point of actual results
the very poorest in all Australasia, South
Australia alone excepted. Considering that
Westralia lives on its reputation as a land of
gold, and on that alone, this seems suffi-
ciently incredible, yet it is fully proved by
the official reports just issued. In its best
year, 1895, it produced less than one-third as
much gold as Victoria, which, taking the
relative sizes of the two provinces into con-
sideration, shows that Victoria, even in its
decadence, is fully 36 times richer a gold
country than is the new province in its
youth, so far as present results go. On the
same basis Queensland is five times, New
South Wales five times, Maoriland eleven
times, and Tasmania nine times richer than
the alleged Golden West. Even South Aus-
tralia is almost as rich, so far as can be
judged by the production up to date, as the
Great Bogus Land of which Forrest is dic-
tator. That land comprises nearly a million
square miles of country, and in no other part
of Australasia can a million square miles of
land be found which do not yield at least
three times as much gold. On the cold logic
of facts and official figures it is by many
times the poorest spot of its size in all this
continent, and the W.A. boom is about the
most outrageous instance of lying on one side
and blank credulity on the other which has
been seen this century.
* * * *
Even the published figures do not show
fully the wretchedness of hungry and
waterless Westralia as a gold producer. Last
year Victoria and Queensland each yielded
three times as much of the yellow metal, and
New South Wales and Maoriland also pro-
duced each many thousands of ounces more.
In Maoriland and Victoria, too, the yield
last year increased faster than in Westralia.
In all the Eastern provinces the gold is widely
diffused and affords an existence to hundreds
of miners, a good living to hundreds more,
and a fortune to a few; while in Wes-
tralia it yields about once a year an immense
pile to one man or one party, and starvation,
or something not much better, to the rest of
the seekers. In all Westralia, which is
nearly as large as Victoria, New South Wales
and Queensland combined, the number of
known dividend-paying mines at the present
time could almost be counted on the fingers
of one hand. Moreover, the gold-return,
miserable as it was last year, shows signs of
falling off. The gold-returns for the last
month of 1895 were much smaller than those
for the first month, and those for January,
1896, were smaller still. Bayley’s Reward
is practically dead and buried. The London-
derry is seemingly ditto. The Wealth of
Nations is almost forgotten. There is hardly
one sensation left save the Great Boulder,
and Victoria alone can show old, partially
worn-out mines that are almost as good even
yet as the Boulder is at its prime. Also,
there are fields almost deserted in Victoria
to-day which look more promising than the
vaunted Ooolgardie, and yet no one could
raise a boom on them by all the lying of all
the Ananias tribe from the first of the name
downwards.
* * * *
The Westralian boom, when analysed, is a
weird affair. First of all, a few large finds
were made, or rather a few moderate finds
which looked large because they were con-
densed in a very small space. When
£50,000 worth of gold was taken out of a
few square yards of ground, the credulous
British investor assumed that the rest of the
ground would yield at the same rate, and
proceeded to float these finds into £700,000
companies. Very little of this nominal
capital ever came to Westralia—it was
almost all annexed by various financiers in
London. But the amounts looked enormous,
and a rush of immigrants, most of them with
more or less money, set in. Westralia has
lived since then to a very great extent on
the money of these immigrants, who brought
much more gold into the province than
they ever took out of its reefs. Their
inrush created Coolgardie, Kalgoorlie,
and many other towns, and gave the land
there an artificial value. These towns
exist, not to get gold by legitimate mining,
but to float companies on the unfortunate
J. Bull ; so long as J. Bull will buy the
business of pegging out leases and floating
companies goes on ; while it goes on the bar-
ren desert on which Coolgardie and the other
towns stand is worth often many pounds per
foot frontage, though its value, measured
by the gold production, is little more than as
many pence. Apart from the industry of
floating companies on J. Bull the people
live by selling leases to each other, not for
working purposes, but for flotation on
J. Bull later on ; by selling food and drink
and tobacco to each other, building houses
for each other, taking each other in as
boarders, doing each other’s washing, bor-
rowing money on mortgage upon the fictitious
value which their presence (for the purpose
of floating mines on J. Bull) has given to
the land, bringing up stores for each other
per team, bringing up timber to sell to each
other or to supply the mines that are living
on British capital, building railways with
borrowed money to take people to and from
places which live on borrowed money,
buying shares from each other, discounting
each other’s bills, and publishing newspapers
which tell each other about the amazing
resources of the country.
So far, in not one single instance, has the
British investor received anything ‘
decent return for his money, and, so t ,
not one single mine has been proved payable
at any respectable depth ; therefore, unless a
wondrous change comes over the spirit of
the dream, the time must come when J. 801 l
will listen to the voice of the charmer no
longer, and the influx of British money will
practically cease. Up to date very few in-
deed of the strangers from the eastern pro-
vinces have realised their visions of wealth
and glory ; therefore, unless a great change
arrives, the influx of capital from the east
must some day die away also. Owing to
want of water, want of fuel, difficulty of
carriage, scarcity of gold, and many other
drawbacks, every £1 extracted from the
earth in the shape of the yellow metal has
cost, on a rough estimate, £<3 to extract, and
only the influx of foreign capital has kept
mining alive under such conditions.
Obviously unless a change conies here also
the time must arrive when the boom will die
for want of dividends, for even the maddest
of prospectors won’t keep working for ever
on these ruinous terms, and the maddest of
British investors won’t keep investing for
ever unless he gets a fair return. The fields
have no agricultural, pastoral or manufactur-
ing industries to fall back upon. They don t
produce enough gold to pay working ex-
penses, and when the foreign capital fails
the population will begin to drift away.
Then property values will fall, and there
will be no more money to be raised by
mortgaging the fictitious prices of land. The
people who live by selling leases and shares
to each other will drop off for want of buyers.
The people who live by building houses, and
taking in washing, and providing accommo-
dation, and selling food and drink and
tobacco, and bringing up stores, and all the
rest, will go the same way ; they all live on
the foreign capitalist, in the first place, and,
in the second, on the artificial land-values
they create by getting together in one spot for
the purpose of living on the foreign capitalist.
The remorseless logic of the gold returns
issued by Government shows that Westralian
mining, even after yeai’3 of hard borrowing,
doesn’t pay its way, and a field that has no
material resources save gold, and whose one
material resource is a dead loss, is doomed.
♦ * * •
Last year — 1895 —was the best year up to
date in Westralia’s history. Also, in a sense,
it was the worst. There was a large influx
of population, the greater part of which was
bound for the goldfields, and the united
efforts of all these new adventurers increased
the gold-yield to the extent of less than one
ounce per man. In other words, the
strangers made, in actual realised wealth, an
average income of something like £4 each
per annum. What else they acquired was
made by selling bogus mines to J. Bull or
to other people ; by getting together in one
place, and thereby giving an artificial value
to a bit of sand, and then buying, selling,
mortgaging, and booming it ; by making
roads and railways, and being paid by the
State with borrowed money for doing so ; by
floating companies to supply, with borrowed
money, water and gas to populations that
will presently fade away, unless something
unexpected happens ; by selling land, or
mines, or shares, or options, or miscellaneous
shadows to each other at enormously inflated
values on bills, and then discounting the bills
with banks which may be very sorry for it by-
and-bye ; by swapping shadows and exchang-
ing hallucinations ; and otherwise by eating
their own and each other’s tails. But the solid
basis of it all was the aforesaid £4 each per
annum only that, and nothing more. And
the fact that man does not live on £4 per
annum is too patent to require demonstration
* * * *

The J. P System,
Sore as are the afflictions imposed upon the corn
munity by individual stipendiary magistrates
the revelations of the Lormer Board of En-
quiry, now jogging along spasmodically in Mel-
bourne, show, it there were any need of showing
the imbecility of the unpaid-magistracy system’
Putting aside the imputation'of bribery and
favoritism, the incapacity, the want of judicial
balance, and the mental puniness of those to
whom are entrusted the characters and security
of thousands, are sufficiently striking to cause
doubts of the existence of their vaunted com-
mon-sense in the peoples who tolerate the
system. Where justices are honest, rule-of-
thumb law is what they “chuck” at you, it
being quite a common experience for a lawyer
who cites a ruling-case in point to save the
expense of an appeal, to have the decision given
against him “on the facts”—for his temerity in
telling the “beak” the first principles of his
business. Astute lawyers never know cases in
the police-courts—they know the justices in-
stead. And it works well. The dogs of Stam-
boul divide the city into cantonments, with
summary justice against poachers : Lhe police-
court lawyers divide the justices, and each is
supreme over his own pack.
* * * *
“ Justices’ justice ” was a byword and a sort
of tolerated reproach when their jurisdiction
was limited to a few pounds’ worth, or to the
preliminary proceedings of a Supreme Couit
trial; but when it plays the jumping clown
over a jurisdiction of hundreds of pounds and
in criminal matters practically beyond appeal,
its existence becomes an intolerable nuisance
and a grave national danger. More laws are
repealed by the ignorance and private passions
of pigheaded or unscrupulous justices than by
the established constitutional machinery.
England for long objected to remove the
consular courts of Japan, because, although the
Japanese laws were good, she was doubtful
about the administration of them. Could a
more apt example of wretched administration
be found in China itself than our unpaid judi-
ciary ? On England’s precedent it should be
quite sufficient to ground a claim for Japanese
consular courts in Australia for the protection
of Japanese subjects. Parliament may pass
hactories Acts as it chooses ; but manufacturers
who are justices, sweaters who are justices,
have to interpret them, and as their decision
is final on questions of fact, they will gladly
interpret their. The “social evil” may be
legislated out of existence, but you have to
reckon with the justice who is the landlord of
the bawd, or the justice who is the “fancy
man” of the nymph, or the justice who is her
wine-merchant, or livery-stable-keeper, or the
one who is always on hand, for a consideration
in money or in kind (frequently kind), to bail
her out on occasions. What system could be
devised more certain to breed injustice, or to
blast a nation’s character for striving to secure
justice, than one which so works in the great
pastoral districts of Australia (where nearly all
offences and disputes are either against or by or
with squatters) that nearly all the Justices of
the Peace are squatters or their hangers-on ?
And in this case one of the offences over which
they have jurisdiction is a class offence, the
committal of which entails imprisonment on
the workman, but only a money penalty on the
squatter.
* * * *
The qualifications of a J.P. are nil, unless
successful flunkeyism at elections be reckoned
one. Whatever brains are necessary are sup-
plied by an underpaid clerk of courts. The
justice usually prides himself on his ignorance
of the law he has sworn to administer justly
and without fear or favor, so that perjury
would seem, in many cases, to be another
essential for a justice. The Bulletin, of
course, is not admiring the law, it merely asks
for fair weight and no adulteration in the
donkey-meat the people pay for. As the
justice is usually a man who has acquired or is
acquiring a competency by rigid adherence to a
sordid ideal of life, is animated mainly by the
ordinary ambitions of putrid Respectability,
and is inaccessible to any of the sweeter in-
fluences of true culture, he carries his limita-
tions in these respects on to the bench—judges
motives from the lowest point of view, and
equips with the sanction of law the knock-
kneed marionettes of shoddy conventionality.
The justice does nob come from the people ; he
is but one of the keener-knifed of the leeches of
the people. He is of the crowd who gorge at
town-halls, who work the oracles of secret-
society boodledom, who boss the “invites” to
mayors’ balls, who blarney the working man at
Sunday-school tea-meetings, who, in a word,
are in the swim and mean to keep there by
hook or crook. His presence is an insult to
Democracy, for he is not selected through
merit, nor elected for fitness, as was his ancient
prototype. Besides, he is unpaid. That is to
say, if he needs payment he must find it himself.
And, needless to say, he can, and often does,
easily manage that.
♦ * * »
There should be no unpaid officers in a demo-
cracy. Whatever may be said against this
from the ideal standpoint (and much may be
said) the experience of ages goes to show that,
in practice, the power of the purse is one of
the principal safeguards of freedom. Econo-
mically, the proposition soon justifies itself.
As a mere insurance premium for sound
justice, a salary would be a mere fleabite,
when the amount of money and of g ao ‘
involved in the case is considered. But,
apart from this, the danger of “justice on the
loose ” is greatly to be apprehended when men,
reduced, perhaps, to impecuniosity by exploded
swindles; are practically invited to help them-
selves out of the pockets of the public, without
check or limit. When our Tammany times
come (if they aren’t here now), they will fi nt *


* “Songs ’Neath the Southern Cross,” by J. C. Neilcl.
George Robertson and Co., Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide,
and Brisbane.
their most suitable “ soil ” in our unpaid muni-
cipal councils, our unpaid Legislative Council,
and our unpaid magistracy.
* * * *
The whole system must be swept away.
It is undemocratic. Election might remedy
this. 1 lie custos pacts of old times was
elected by the people, so that this method
would be consonant with a time preceding that
in which the sordid sway of the country-
squire lay like a nightmare on the chest
of English politics. Again, the system is an
unjust one. A primal “fount of justice” is
the maxim that no man shall be a judge in his
own cause. A system which allows a grocer to
decide for or against a friendly or a rival grocer
in the same neighbourhood, which draws its
judges of the rights and duties of labor from
the employer-class almost solely, which ap-
points a principal shareholder in a brewery to
punish the licensing offences committed in a
hotel the property of the brewery, and which
frequently sees its magistrates alternately
on the bench and on the floor of the
court as suitors, deliberately breaks this
maxim, and must go. And that the system
is a corrupt one, that there are “blind
magistrates ” (thanks, John Chinaman, for once,
for your exquisite irony !), that there are judg-
ments dictated by personal connections witli
lawyers and clients, and by class animosity or
favoritism, that decisions on “questions of
fact ” are unscrupulously given in order
to prevent appeals—these things are so
well known to that universal rumor which,
though rejected as worthless by technical
rules of evjdence, seldom errs, that any
citation of specific instances is unnecessary.
The unpaid magisterial system is rotten in
principle, vicious in its undemocratic spirit, in-
capable of any improvement on account of
its material, immoral in its influence, and
either inane or corrupt in its practical adminis-
tration.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Nicholas Willis on Foreign Capital.
Premier Reid has renewed his promise—the
one he made a year ago and didn’t keep—to
float future loans in the local market. Where-
upon Nicholas Willis, M.L.A., the evil
genius of the N.S.W. Protectionist party,
sends to the daily papers a remonstrance and
an essay on the beauty and loveliness of foreign
borrowing. These are the bones of Nicholas’s
story :
So development should continue by leaps and bounds
until the colony, properly developed,'can boast an edifice
of national resource, which, founded on the rock of
stability, can rest conscious of its own strength. . . .
But will not Mr. Reid’s proposal have a diametrically
opposite effect ? It will open the door to the unthrifty
investor, who, content to take small interest, will throw
his money into the hands of the Government and quietly
await dividend day without putting his hand to the
plough of development. It will close the door to the
flood of English capital, which is so essential towards
cheapening our local money-market. It will, in a word,
absorb all the available local money, tighten the money-
market here, strangle enterprise, stifle development, and
hinder the producer on the soil, who has gone on through
a succession of bad seasons, tight money-markets, and
financial crises, in a hope that when a change did come
the surplus would be on the market for borrowers who
had borne the heat and burden of the day, and not
absorbed by a short-sighted Governmental act which will
make matters really worse than they were in the past.
Every man or woman with a hundred pounds will run to
the Government for stocks instead of placing his or her
money at fixed deposit in the banks ; and thus the capital
usually lent by such corporations to men who would work
with it and endeavor to make more is practically robbed
from the producer by the Government itself, thereby
causing stagnation in trade —a hindrance to the borrower,
the effect of which would, I assert, vibrate throughout
the colony. . . . lam convinced, now that the wave
of prosperity has commenced to set in, that ten millions of
money could be financed by our Government at 3 per
cent.
The average man, who knows about as much of
finance .as Hamlet did of the Schleswig-Holstein
question, has been accepting this view of things
for a good many years now with disastrous
consequences. The only thing that is wrong
about it is that it is all a fallacy from beginning
to end. If the Government borrow's money in
the country and spends it there, it doesn’t
“ absorb the available local money ” nor
“strangle enterprise,” nor “stifle develop-
ment,” nor leave the banks with less money to
lend to the private borrower. If the Reid
Ministry borrow-s, say, £2,000,000 locally, it
will come out of the banks in the first place and
go in again the same day or next day—it will
be transferred from the credit balance of the
lenders to the credit balance of the Government,
and the money in the banks will remain the
same. Then if the Government spends it in
the province it will be taken out by degrees to
pay wages and buy material, and the wage-
earners will promptly dissipate it among store-
keepers and others in the purchase of neces-
saries, and in a few weeks’ time it will have
drifted back into the banks again and be ready
for lending out just as it was before. It will
be at the credit of a different set of men,
but otherwise there will be no change. If
the banks had £10,000,000 in cash, the Govern-
ment could float twenty £5,000,000 loans out of
that sum in succession, provided it spent
one loan before raising the next one—in other
words, it could borrow the money 10 times
over ; and, so long as it spent the cash in the
country, the same old pile of money would be
in the bank again when the last opera-
tion was finished, waiting to be borrowed
for the 21st time. Australia has at present
more gold in proportion to population than any
other country on earth, and the idea of borrow-
ing money to bring gold into a country in that
condition is ludicrous. As to the theory that
money should be borrowed in England because
it can be obtained there at a lower rate, and
brought into Australia to “ cheapen our local
money-market,” that also is a mere superstition.
Cheapness is only a relative term ; 15 per cent,
is cheap if money elsewhere costs 20 per cent.,
and 3 per cent, is dear if money elsewhere costs
only 2 per cent. The only object of bringing
in borrowed money is to make interest as
low here as in England, and if Australia
borrowed a thousand millions from England it
wouldn’t arrive at that destination. Money is
always cheaper in the country which is receiv-
ing interest from abroad than in the one that is
paying interest to foreign creditors, and all the
borrowing in the world wouldn’t alter this fact.
The only way to make interest as low here as
in England is to swear off foreign borrowing, and
gradually pay off the country’s foreign liabili-
ties, and make this country financially inde-
pendent. Borrowing money to make interest
low' is only drinking to get sober. Foreign
loans, however, produce one definite re-
sult which is the one thing Mr. Willis
does not allude to. For a short time
until the interest eats up the money and
takes it back to England—they cause an un-
natural abundance of cash, and an outbreak of
speculation, and a boom in land values, and on
the top of that boom the big squatter— Nicholas
Willis is a squatter if this paper remembers
aright—can sell out his property at three times
its real value and go away in peace. The Melb.
laud-boom, with its natural complement of
awful burst, was caused by the influx of
foreign capital, and the ten millions Mr. Willis
wants to borrow in London at 3 per cent,
would make the same boom and burst in New
South Wales.
Shiels and Speight.
Dear Bulletin, —The nice things said of
Speight by ex-Premier Shiels, in the Vic.
Assembly last month, have been contrasted
with the nasty remarks made by the same
Shiels when lie was accomplishing Speight’s
destruction in 1892. It suited Melb. Age to
point out the awful inconsistency of the politi-
cian, and argue that his latter utterances were
necessarily insincere because they flatly contra-
dicted his former statements. But there were
some much more former statements by Siiiels
to which the organ of David Syme didn’t call
attention. In December, 1890, Mr. Shiels was
acting, under old James Munro’s Premiership,
as Minister of Vic. Railways, and it fell to him
to usher in a new railway policy, or rather an
amendment of the old Act, with a long speech.
He was an admirer of Speight at that time-
-15 months previous to turning him adrift. All
the mischief possible had then been done, and
Parliament was discussing a huge railway deficit
which had come to stay. And Shiels spoke in
this tone :
They might refuse to renew Mr. Speight’s tenure of
office ; they might carry out the hopes raised by Mr.
Giddies’s letter (promising a rise of salary), or they might
reappoint him under the terms of his original engage-
ment. To refuse to renew the appointment would be a
direct reflection on Mr. Speight. That could only be
justifiable if he had proved himself incompetent anil in-
efficient. ... A man would be simply a wonder if he
could manage such a complex railway system as that of
Victoria without complaints or accidents. . . . Mr.
Speight had done nothing to conciliate the public, but
there was another side of the case which every reasonable
and just man should take into account. He practically
stood with his hands tied behind his back. He was called
to preside over a department that was disorganised
and torn by internal jealousies and dissension.
The Commissioner of Railways had to work with
officers that were, in some cases, incompetent,
in others effete, and become responsible for their work.
. . . If he had been more Draconian in discipline, and
more severe in demanding implicit obedience, the public
would have been better served. But what would have
been the consequence in Parliament ? The clamor would
have been intense. . . . Placing in the balance all the
merits or demerits of Mr. Speight, and the difficulty of
getting a better man for the money, the Government felt
it was their duty to reappoint him at the original salary
of £3OOO. He had heard even Mr. Woods say that Mr.
Speight was a man of wonderful resource in every detail
of railway management.
The Shiels of December, 1890, it appears, was
the Shiels of January, 1896, as regards his
opinion of Speight, whereas the intermediate
Premier Shiels —the weakling whose railway-
policy was laid down for him by the Age pro-
prietor—professed to see his duty in Speight’s
peremptory dismissal. Shortly put, the mem-
ber for Normanby has now recanted his previous
recantation, and confirmed his original judgment.
He spoke well, then ill, then well again of
Speight, and the odds are two to one in favor
of the twice-told tale.— Old Newspaper.
Bad Law and Bad Liquor.
The casual, hap-hazard way in which the law
is administered according to the state of the
judicial liver, and the utter want of any visible
connection in its decisions, helps materially to
brighten up an otherwise dreary and painful
subject. For instance : A week or two ago a
shopman in a Sydney grocery store was charged
with selling Australian wine without a license,,
and without his employer having a license, and
in the course of the proceedings it transpired
incidentally that the wine, which was labelled
“old port,” or something to that effect, was
only a loathsome imitation of what it pre-
tended to be. It was not proved that the luckless;
employe knew his master had not the requisite
license, nevertheless he was sentenced to pay
,£3O and costs, or to do three months in gaol.
Then the employer was charged with selling
bogus liquor, and he was fined ,£2O and costs, in
default levy and distress, but so far it seems
that nothing has happened to him over the
license question. So the situation pans out some-
thing like this : When an employer causes his;
servant to sell bogus liquor and to sell it with-
out the employer having a proper license, the
servant is directly responsible for his master
not having a license, and the master is respon-
sible for the liquor. There was no direct evi-
dence to prove that the knew his boss;
hadn’t a proper license, or that he knew his
boss’s liquor was bad, but, by a beautifully fine;
distinction, it is decided that he ought to have
known about the license, but couldn’t reason-
ably be expected to know about the liquor. By
another beautifully fine distinction the boss is
responsible for selling bad liquor by deputy,
but not for selling without a proper license by
the same deputy. The law also holds that the
proper punishment for selling unspeakably bad
liquor on your own account is one-third
less than the proper penalty for another man
not having a license. Further, the Bench said
the conduct of a man who was fined ,£2O was
disgraceful, but it made no severe comments on
the man who was fined ,£3O or three months, so
apparently the disgracefulness and infamy of
the proceeding are deducted from the penalty ;
and it has now been legally affirmed that to
corrode the human stomach with infamous
liquor is <£lo less nefarious than to be employed
by a man whose license isn’t in good order—
which is interesting for the person with the
corroded stomach.
Queensland Govt, has disfranchised 3577 elec-
tors enrolled at the Jan. revision-courts by
dating the elections a month before the usual
time, in the ordinary course of that regular pro- j
vince. This was done after the agents of the
Political Ass had scrutinised the rolls or pumped
the registrars and ascertained that the probable
balance of the new votes would be strongly
against Boodlewraith. So utter is political
demoralisation that even the democratic press
hardly bothers to protest—a long course of
Boodlewraith has seared the public conscience.
And Premier Nelson’s manifesto lays cynical
stress on the favor with which he regards
“such an amendment of the electoral law as
would place every man in a position to acquire
the franchise.” Three thousand and more
electors have just acquired it, and Boodlewraith
prohibits them from using it. The ostensible
reason for the haste is the need for electing
delegates to the Federal Convention, while
everybody knows that the Black-Labor Govern-
ment could only be driven to federate at the
point of the bayonet, and Attorney-General
Byrnes told a Sydney interviewer the other
day that Federation in Queensland was a dead
question ! 0 Lord, how long ?
A case illustrating the necessity of making
the Employers’ Liability Act apply to sailors,
comes on shortly, probably in Victoria. A
sailor engaged in Melbourne for a six months’
trip to YVestralia, &c. When leaving a port
there a number of carpenters were shipped for
the purpose of erecting cattle-barriers, the in-
tention being to convey wild cattle between
certain ports. The barriers were erected under
the captain’s supervision, but, in spite of warn-
ings, the cattle were allowed on board, broke
the barriers, and one of them gored a sailor so
severely that he was laid up for a year. If the
courts follow English decisions they will pro-
bably hold that the captain and sailor are “ fel-
low-servants,” and that accordingly the co. is
not liable, and so it will getoffseot free, although
it has practically admitted its moral liability by
offering a sum which the circumstances make
quite insufficient. The Employers’ Liability
Acts partially restrict the “fellow-servant”
doctrine, but do not apply to sailors in Victoria,
and in the provinces in which they so apply it is
very doubtful whether the provisions are not
ineffectual on the high seas for constitutional
reasons. This, perhaps, is a case where the
machinery of an Imperial Enabling Act might
be justified, if any Imperial-Australian legisla-
tion can be justified.

The S.M. Herald, a couple of days before the
opening of the local Criminal Court, delivered
itself of a “ leader ” extolling the uses of the
gaol lash. “It has been established that the
lash put down garrotting,” it asserted, with
that air of infallibility which only the partially-
informed can successful!}' exhibit. Half-an-
hour’s study of the question will demonstrate
to even a very prejudiced person that no such
fact has been established about the lash. The
evidence on the subject is claimed to prove that
garrotting declined before lash-legislation for
the offence took effect; and, if this be denied,
then most writers hold that the evidence to
hand amply proves that only to an insignificant
extent did the new legislation affect the waning
fashion of the criminal class. Further, on the
list of names to come before this particular
Criminal Court for trial were those of Edward
Crawford and others, accused of robbery with
violence, and the appearance of the Herald
article before the Court began business looked
like a direction to the Judge to apply in such
cases on conviction the lacerating powers of
the Criminal Code. The apparent nicety of
the calculation is creditable to the Herald’s
sense of timeliness, but all else about it is
obtrusively brutal.
♦
It has been enacted in Maoriland that no regis-
try office fee shall exceed 55., half-a-crown
payable by the person who gets the situation,
and half-a-crown by the employer to whom he
or she is supplied. This bursts up a profitable
and old-established swindle. The wild-cat
registry-office keeper, when times are bad and
the towns swarming with men who want work
at any price, often takes all the first week’s
wages as his fee, and goes shares with the
employer. Then at the end of the week,
or the end of the second week, the new man
is sacked, and another engaged on the same
terms, and the boss and the registry-man
divide again. In some large establishments,
when times are very bad, about one-third of
the wages-sheet is thus “whacked” between
the boss or manager and the bowelless ruffian
who runs the registry. When there are more
men available than can be used up even by this
dodge, someregistry-keepersfind them imaginary
billets at the extreme end of the country, wages
good, the men to pay their own fares. The fee
charged in each case is, so far as the registry-man
■can estimate, all the money his victim has over
and above his railway-fare, so that when he finds
out the imposture he won’t be able to come
back and knock the head off the harpy in the
office. This latter swindle is dangerous, how-
ever, and is generally tried on women or small
men who look harmless, but the device of going
shares with the boss is practised every day in
Sydney and Melbourne, and was in full blast in
Maoriland also until the new law came in force.
Now it is practically dead. The half-crown
isn’t worth dividing.
The Criminology Society has now a good oppor-
tunity to secure the release of all the sentenced
murderers, &c., in Victoria who are suffering
in health owing to their incarceration. The
Staples precedent show's that it can be done
easily, quietly and without even the principal
members of the Cabinet knowing anything
about it. The secret release of Staples
emphasises The Bulletin’s old contention that
whenever portion of a prisoner’s sentence is to
be remitted a detailed notification to that effect
should be published in the Government
Gazette. Staples was sentenced to five years
in ’93. The remarks of the Judge were
especially severe, regret being expressed that
the offence, issuing a false balance-sheet, was
only a misdemeanor and did not admit of a
higher penalty. Yet with three years served,
Staples is let loose on society, and is out of
gaol for three months before any of the public
ior even the Ministry, with the exception of
Solicitor-General Cuthbert (M.L C.) hear of it.

Song's ’Neath the Southern Cross,
The latest Australian bard to come out in book-
form is John Cash Neild, the N.S.W. politician
with the unique moustache, who talked the Jen-
nings-Dibbs Ministry to death in a nine-hours’
oration some years ago. The famous Jawbone
publishes his soul-wail in a neat green volume,
with a portrait of himself in a fierce aspect on the
front page, and a beautiful flowing signature
below. Neild, as a poet, is distinctly better than
the dreadful Sladen, but that isn’t saying much.
There is very little more originality about his
book than about the title of it. The Southern
Cross, which is a weak, straggling constellation,
and not much like a cross at the best, is the refuge
of every Australian bard who has no better flower
of imagery at command. It is a figure of speech
peculiarly Australian—at least no European bard
that The Bulletin knows of ever wrote “ Songs
’Neath the Great Bear.” And when one considers
the unspeakable vastness of the Southern Cross,
even if it isn’t much of a constellation to look
at, and the smallness of the bard who sings under
it, and the exceeding smallness of the song he sings,
Mr. Neild’s urgency in pointing out that such avery
small tune is tootled beneath, and so far beneath,
such a very large mass of blazing suns seems
grotesque. It is very like writing a par. about a
municipal meeting at Cow Flat, and heading it
“Prose written beneath the Universe.”
* * * *
J. C. Neild is obviously not a poet. He is a
mechanically accurate person who most of the
time cuts his screed into neat lengths of gum-log,
and piles it up carefully for removal, but there is
no fire in him, no energy, nothing that anyone
will ever want to remember. His first four poems
spread over 103 pages. “ Out West ” tells, in 30
long verses, how three men went forth into the
wilderness, and in the middle of it one of them
saw a vision of an inland sea, and on it an old,
old, weed-covered, barnacled ship of the Flying
Dutchman persuasion, and on the ship an old, old
man who tells, in a digression of 35 verses, a talc
which is a bad copy of Coleridge’s “ Ancient
Mariner.” Neild’s mariner also, with his glitter-
ing eye, holds one of three, and his tale is adorned
with ancient expressions such as “eftsoons ” and
the like. In this painfully new land, old, disused
statements like “eftsoons” seem out of place,
The following is a brief hunk of “ Out West” :
But for the aid of kindly blacks,
Who led us to a tiny spring,
We must have perished. We lost two
Horses that fell dead in their tracks.
The other long poems are large accumulations of
nicely-written commonplace with some common-
place in between which isn’t nicely written but
only thrown together with a shovel. Witness
this verse from “ Two Xmas Days in North Aus-
tralia ” :
Again the boisterous dance proceeds
Till the musicians nod and wink
At one another and the crowd—
They’d had by far too much to drink.
In the course of this poem “George Vivian ” is
made to rhyme with “billycan,” which is rough
on a useful bush implement. Mr. Neild is more
interesting in his sonnets because in them there is
less of him. His poem “ Morning ”is an effusion
in which he urges some unspecified infant “to
grow on, sweet babe,” and “press boldly for the
prize,” and “press onward, youth, be thy goal
noble deeds,” which is about as dull and feeble an
aposti’ophe as could be imagined. The bard who
can only tell an infant to keep on growing is of very
little use, seeing that the infant is growing anyhow,
and will continue growing just the same without
his permission—unless he gives it gin regularly.
The spectacle of J. 0. Neild in book-form
authorising an infant to grow seems unnecessary,
somehow. In his sonnet to “Evening ” the same
authority states that “The sun shall rise,” which
removes a great doubt from the public mind. A
few pages further on J. C. Neild tackles the vast,
comprehensive subject of “ Death ” in such
language as this :
I can hear the deep tread of thy footstep, O ! Death !
On my face I can feel thy lethiferous breath,
And thine arms calmly fold me, magnificent Death.
And again :
Come ! I have called thee in lone hours of midnight,
When woe insupportable hopefulness hid quite.
‘ ‘ When woe insupportable hopefulness hid quite ”
is the worst effort at rhyme this paper remembers.
* * * *
Later on the author plunges into what he calls
“ Bush Rhymes.” The first bush rhyme is
entitled “ Jim.” The second one is “ Bill.” Bush
rhyme No. 3 is “ Joe,” and the next one is “ Fan.”
The following is a sample of “Jim,” which, for
vigor, is far above the bard’s average :
Well, he stayed and stayed, and he pulled poor Jim
Back, steadily back, from the grave’s dark rim,
The doctor? A darned good sort, I call him—
You can call him what you like —
We gave him some nuggets when Jim got through.
Did I ever tell Jim ? What ! About Dru ?
What use ! AVhy, before we left the Paroo
She married a cove called Mike.
Jim was ill in a bark hut at the time, and Dru,
who married a cove called Mike, was the girl who
had promised to marry Jim, and who mashed
somebody else (doubtless Bill) in his absence. Joe,
in another poem further on, was a shepherd’s dog
in 75 verses, who ran 30 miles on three legs to
fetch the boss and four stockmen, at a crisis. In
“ The Bush Fire,” somebody, probably Dick, tells
Bill at some length how Jack carried him, under
difficult circumstances, to rescue Ann. Mr. Neild
is obviously not at home in the Bill and Jack kind
of verse, and tells his story in curt barks like this •
Or cut me a bit of tobacco—hey !
Look at that snake.
Hit him a whack with your whip, and don’t stay
To find a stake.
Harmless ? No ! That be dang—
Brown ! Just look at his fang !
His hole’s there, under that stone.
I was after a mob
Of young steers, on a cob
Out in the ranges alone !
The bard who makes an Australian bushman say
“dang” simply to find a rhyme for “fang” is
capable of making the fearsome Bill of the back-
blocks say almost anything.
* * * *
There is a lot of large “O” in Mr. Neild’s
verses, and his cries of “ O ! Hope ! ” and “ O !
Memory!” and “O! Happiness!” and “O!
Blessed state ! ” and things like that are painful
examples of abstract shriek in long metre. Still,
it may be that the author couldn’t sleep till he got
this volume off his chest, and, if so, he did well to
publish it. Suppressed poetry is bad for the
health at all times.
