That reliable favorite, “ Ma Mie Rosette,”
is doing a week at Sydney Lyceum, and Henriot
Brownlow’s Henry IY. is the same beautifully
finished character that he has always been, and
the people who insist on exhibiting their inferior
French in the d.c. lift up their voices and allude
to him as Angry Cat, just as they did in the happy
bygone days. Miss May Pollard fills the short,
unsophisticated skirt of Rosette once more, and
dreams on the bunch of hay in the farm-yard
about how she fell through carrying refreshment to
a giddy monarch quite as well as any one could
desire. Courtice Pounds is May’s rustic lover this
time, and though he fills that character as capably
as his predecessors he does nothing to explain the
great original conundrum why May refuses to go
off with the King, especially when Brownlow fixes
her with his large, introspective eye and his
beautiful clothes, and her lover has nothing to
offer in lieu thereof save a cottage and a luvin’
’art and a low-comedy face with a pained expres-
sion on it, and a good deal of grass-seed in his
hair. Howard Vernon, Lauri, and Misses
Florence Young and Marietta Nash occupy their
old parts, and the many pleasant things this paper
said about them last time are hereby repeated in
one comprehensive ditto.
* * * •
The new series of Living Pictures is larger
and more varied, and is set in a bigger frame and
a more gorgeous background than the last one.
There is very little of the undress element in these
productions, whereas the other ones were attired
principally in a quantity of dazzling teeth and a
hope that all would yet be well, therefore the
present show is r.ot tempered by a half-formed
expectation that a large married policeman will
rush in, hiding his shocked countenance behind an
umbrella, and arrest the Pictures on the spot.
The new Pictures are also more elaborate than the
first series, and have more character in them, and
in most cases have a tale of human interest to tell,
instead of merely narrating a pair of legs. Long-
staff’s “ Breaking the News,” Kennington’s
“Adversity,” and M'Cubbin’s “Down on his
Luck ” are three out of the nineteen put on by a
lavish management which are alone more than
worth the admission money. On Saturday “ Miss
Decima” is to be produced for the very first time
in Australia, and till then the Pictures and the
bad French king pursuing his mediaeval Flossie
will probably keep the Lyceum full and a little
over.
** * *
George Darrell’s alleged comedy, ‘‘ Wanted
a Wife,” which was staged at Sydney Criterion
last Saturday, is a piece which causes sad reflec-
tions about how man was descended from a monkey
and has been a disgrace to his ancestor ever since.
It belongs to the old, sad school of funnyism
wherein the humor consists of four people being
concealed in as many different rooms, and another
hidden under a table, and one more in a box, and
all these people putting their heads out cautiously
to see if the others are gone, and shrieking dis-
mally when they find they are not gone. There is
no reason worth mentioning why they should play
hide-and-shriek in this inane fashion, save that it
is down in the directions, and they are paid to do
it. When they all discover each other no dreadful
consequence ensues, and, as the situation is quite
harmless from the beginning, their wild sprinting
back and forward is mere superfluous pedestri-
anism. Some more mirth is based on a waiter
with a disgusting sniff in his nose and a tendency
to go into unwholesome particulars about it, and
the waiter’s nose is just as interesting as that of
any plain small boy with a cold and no handker-
chief. Also, his confidential communication—
made between two sniffs —on the subject of
underclothing is not interesting but only
stupid, and the fact that Wilfred Shine hits
him on the head with a ruler whenever he
shows himself from underneath the table is
not comedy but merely assault and battery.
There is no plot in the piece—only a mechanical
gallop of automata, who have no coherent motive
because the author didn’c invent one, and the fun
consists of horse-play and bounds and yells, and
the waiter’s aforesaid nose, and the underclothing,
and the General’s ceaseless complaints about his
gout and his liver, and another funny man’s asser-
tion that he lives on “sheeps’ jimmies” and
that they are “filling.” This kind of mirth isn’t
new, and can be obtained gratis at the boiling-
down-establishment any day. There are some
good people in the piece, and one lady whose
clothes were obviously thrown at her with a
knife and fork, and some other ladies ; and some
of these people accentuate the direful woodenness
of things, and some of them wear their hair hang-
ing down their backs, and some of them go into
unpleasant detail about the waiter’s nose. The
Bulletin doesn’t like that dreadful person or his
nose, but they supply the one gleam of originality
in the show, and if they were taken out there
would be hardly anything save the boiling-down
joke left.
** * *
“ Monte Cristo,” on a scale of much
splendor, is being nightly heaved in five acts to
his doom in the pale-green, hungry sea at Her
Majesty’s. “ Monte Cristo ”is essentially a one-
man piece, and that man is Dampier, who is
the mill that grinds everything exceeding
small. Probably there is no other man in Aus-
tralia who could fill the part so well as this sterling
old actor. Even where he has nothing to do but
pine in a dungeon for 19 years he pines in a highly-
finished manner, and when he gets out and goes
on the trail of his enemies, and takes their scalps
one by one in the slow, patient maimer of a man
who has learned to be tranquil through having
nothing else to do for the best part of a generation,
he is an eloquent example of how the scalp comes
to him who waits. He doesn’t rant much, and he
doesn’t pose much in the limelight, and he doesn’t
vow vengeance to any great extent, but in a
quiet, convincing fashion he arrives (mostly
in evening dress) just the same. Dampier
has three-fourths of the glory, and a dozen
other people divide the rest among them. They
consist of a quantity of assorted bad charac-
ters, one or two good people, and some sundries.
Lesion as the principal crawling villain, and Mrs.
Marshall as the daughter of the reconstructed
banker, and Inman as a brigand in a small way of
business, are the most notable. All these people,
and a lot more, are overwhelmed by Dampier in
the long run, and, when the curtain falls for the
last time, they are only a heap of picturesque
ruins.
** * *
The military spectacle at Sydney Royal
remains as big and smoky and convincing as it
was when last reported upon, and the red glare of
battle, murder, insurrection and sudden death
shines in a lurid haze across the footlights. There
is more good, solid explosion in the “ Enemy’s
Camp ” than in any other piece of recent years,
and the amount of promiscuous free fight on moun-
tain tops and other places where it is needful to
hang on by the hair, is almost unparalleled. One
night last week, after Hazlitt had been executed
in his beautiful uniform, and Scot Inglis had
started off in his beautiful uniform to rescue the
imprisoned girl, and Plimmer, in a gold-laced suit
that must have cost at least <£B cash, went after
him and cut the bridge just as he was standing on
it, the hero went down at too sudden an angle and
bumped himself grievously against the ravine.
This is the only casualty up to date. “ The
Lancashire Lass ” is announced for Saturday.
** * *
After a brief indisposition, executed in his
usual capable fashion, John Coleman has re-
appeared at the Tivoli, and the discrepancy
between the solemnity of his countenance and the
agility of his madly-grotesque legs is on view
every night. La Belle Maie and Miss Alice
Leamar are also on the bill, so that the Tivoli is
particularly strong at present. The Cogills’ show
at Sydney Opera House is also doing well, and the
Music Hall remains closed, and gives no signs of
an early re-opening.
** * *
The new theatre which is being erected by
George Adams, in Pitt-street, Sydney, is getting
on towards completion, and Arthur Garner, who
is to have the sole direction thereof, has sailed for
London by the Warrimoo, on behalf of a bloated
syndicate, with unlimited powers to engage the
best talent to be found in Europe and America.
Mr. Garner’s long experience as a member of “ the
Firm,” and also on his own account, is a sufficient
guarantee that he will get together a first-class
show. The new theatre is intended to be the most
elegant in Australia, and, under the beneficial in-
fluences of a Freetrade tariff, the seating accom-
modation is being specially manufactured in
America, so that the local artisan may have a well-
earned rest. Mr. G. L. Goodman (“Goody”),
who has been manager in Sydney for the Firm for
more years than one can remember, and than
whom no one is more popular with both public and
profession, will be in charge of the front of the
new house.
** * *
“ The Passport,” played in Sydney a few
weeks back, is now raising the harmless, neces-
sary laugh at Melbourne Princess's. It belongs
to a somewhat ancient order of three-act farce, in
which several performers remain carefully ignor-
ant awhile of a fact always known to the audi-
ence. When the truth becomes clear to everybody,
the entertainment goes into liquidation, and the
public who supplied the capital for carrying on
the business are left in doubt as to whether they
got a fair return for their money. There is
nothing to dwell upon when the curtain falls. One
may take away crumbs of food for reflection from
a musical comedy, or a variety show. Sad reflec-
tion is perhaps better than none at all, and a
catchy air can be hung on to. “ The Passport ”
is as unsubstantial as a puff of smoke, or
an elephant’s breath. Fortunately the B. and
B. company appear to advantage in this
vaporous thing. Mrs. Brough contributed a pretty
sketch of a feather-headed lady who mislays her
passport and everything else she handles, except
her husband, and she nearly loses him likewise.
There are many such charming women about, so
wrapped-up in themselves and their nice clothes
that they hardly know when their husbands are
hugging them, and are apt to wonder where the
dear man has got to all the while that they are
sitting on his knee and playfully tapping his bald
head with a fruit-knife. Boucicault goes one
better than this with a clever caricature of the
man addicted to bursts of matrimonial misgiving.
At short intervals the gifted Dion B. leans his head
upon his hand, stares into vacancy, and rumi-
nates as to whether he would have married
his wife if he had been better informed on
the subject ere making the fatal plunge. Cecil
Ward is a walking gentleman husband, who didn’t
bigamise, as supposed, and Dorrington is a long
incoherent Gussie, who wanders aimlessly in and
out of the story. A tradition of Dorrington’s
volubility pervades the scene, but he never gets a
chance to unloose his flow of language. As soon as
he breaks into a preliminary stutter he gets
hustled into an adjoining room, or shut up by
accident, and his conversation remains dead to the
world. Young Majeroni, as a Russian police-
officer, with designs upon Robert Brough’s
stomach, scores a success. He suspects the gentle-
man of carrying a false corporation for the
concealment of stolen goods, and his manner is so
threatening that Brough tries to save his interior
from insult by lying on it. “Fedora” follows
“ The Passport,” probably on Saturday.
** * *
“Ten Nights in a Bar-Room” is still
wafting warm, spirituous odours across the foot-
lights of Melb. Alexandra, and well-filled Houses
appear to hang upon every brealr+Jm
this great temperance drama. The death of little
Eva may also have something to do with the im-
provement in business. The angel child, m this
instance, happens to be a little Mary, who gets
cut down like a flower by a nobbier-glass heaved
at her unsober parent by a scorbutic pub-pro-
prietor, but her real name is Eva nevertheless.
She sings a temperance song on her death-bed,
after which a number of angels call for her
small, white soul, just as though she were
the young person in “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin.
Meanwhile, Augustus Glover, her father, suffers
hbrribl.y from the effects of liquor. A green mon-
key with a scarlet tail gambols rounds his twitch-
ing legs, sjiakes nestle against his bare chest,
and poor father gets the d.ts. again for all they
are worth. A critical audience is not expected at
the Alexandra, therefore our old friend Glover
deserves particular credit for his artistic perfor-
mance. The same thing done under more fashion-
able conditions would bring a considerable amount
of glory to this large, robust, versatile actor.

“ The Land of the Moa, ” at Melb. lloyal,
is not quite the same disjointed panorama that
Sydney saw, although the points of difference are
not worth mentioning. This production, like the
original, consists of substantial scenery and
Maori shrieks, mingled with gall in the form of
dialogue. The plot opens in a sufficiently human
spirit. A nautical uncle, drinking rum hot with
sugar and lemon in it, desires a certain fair
young Mavis to become a barmaid and pour
long beers down the customers’ backs. She
refuses, and is carried aboard the lugger
of a Black Angel, and turned adrift among
the Maoriland mountains. A rising author
—one of The Bulletin’s numerous contributors,
no doubt—spends his time in trying to rescue
Mavis, also venerable James Cathcart, disguised
in thick streaks of paint and pretending to be a
cannibal chief, takes compassion on the maid. '
Finally the chief’s daughter pushes the Black Angel
down a sulphur hole in the Pink Terraces of yore,
and the drama is destroyed by an earthquake.
Many incomprehensible surprises occur in the
meantime. Savages chase white folk, who chase
the savages in their turn, through many changes
of scenery. Miss Maud Williamson hap-
pens freely, sometimes alone, and often
accompanied by a nut-brown lover. Likewise
a half-caste ballet and an unintentionally comic
war-dance are thrown into the expensive farrago,
but why all these incidents occur, and what they
mean when they come to pass, are mysteries
locked- in the deep bosom of the management.
The earthquake and eruption offer atonement for
a good deal. An excellent display of fireworks,
assisted by loud reports from the crater, are not to
be despised. Less imposing spectacles have suf-
ficed to fill the Royal for three or four weeks ;
therefore, “ The Land of the Moa ” is not unlikely
to flourish.

At Poverty Point .
A severe illness accounted for Marie Bister’s retirement
into silence after her one appearance (as Maritana) with
ttie Carl Rosa Co. When she got up from her sick bed
Miss Bister found her voice so much “ off ” that she can-
celled all the concert engagements on her little list"
Another illness and a throat operation put the unlucky
little woman further hack into the shade. Now that she
is strong again (with her voice “ better than ever,” she
says) Marie Bister promises to get a good show in' Eng-
land. Sir Joseph Barnby, shortly before his death, heard
her in “ Sing, Sweet Bird,” and was so delighted that he
got the composer, Ganz, to hear her rattle through the
ballad again next day. Ganz endorsed Barnby’s opinion
by adding a number of Bister’s bird trills to his original
music. Also, he declared her method of voice-production
to be “ perfect,” and offered to coach her gratis whenever
she might feel disposed to seek his advice. At latest
dates Bister had scored a big success in a Lyric Club con-
cert, the audience of somewhat languid fashionables being
so much taken by the lady from Australia, bai Jove! that
she was asked to sing again. Subsequently, at supper
the “ star ” vocalist was the guest of the evening, and
chairman Horace Ledger (of the Era) predicted great
things of her. Bister seems to have got a footing among
the right people.
A competent London musical critic, discussing a ballad
concert at the Queen’s Hall, deals with two or three
artists known to Australia : “Those whose singing pleased
me most were Miss Ada Crossley and Mr. Jack Robertson.
Miss Crossley sang Walthew’s ‘Gleaners’ Slumber Song’
with much charm and refinement. . . . Miss Belle
Cole’s songs were received with applause. . . . Miss
Evangeline Florence sang F. Cowan’s two songs, ‘ Love
Lies Asleep in the Rose ’ and ‘ The Swallows,’ with con-
siderable taste. I, however, would like to point out to
Miss Florence that the fact of her being able to take an P
in alt does not mean that she can sing well. Far from it.
Singing is an art, not a trick; but singing Fin alt is a
trick, and not a sample of the art of vocalisation.” The
men who spread butter on imported concert singers for
the Australian daily press swore that Belle Oole was the
rarest contralto on earth, and Evangeline’s ur!sympathetio
high notes were the perfection of soprano singing.
The rumor current that Florence Young intends shortly
to visit England has been authoritatively denied.
Nellie Farren and her husband are re-united after an
estrangement of 20 years.
Patti says she has Australian offers, but appears to
doubt whether she is yet old enough.
Signor Lardelli, of Sydney, has been appointed a pro-
fessor of singing at Blaekheath Conservatoire of Music.
According to rumor, he owes this success to the inter-
vention of a lady whose sister he had taught in Sydney.
Lardelli is also to compose some pieces for the Prince of
Wales Theatre, where Roland Cunningham is gaining
experience in “ Gentleman Joe.”
Boothby’s operatic dialogue not having proved ideal, an
ordinary hack-writer was at latest providing Gov. Robin-
son’s operetta with a libretto.
Miss Rose Cavendish, who has just been brought before
the London public in the Ella Russell libel-suit, is none
other than Australia’s old friend Rose Albu. Miss Russell
considered her reputation damaged through her name on
the programme being sandwiched in between Rose Albu
and Amy Sherwin. She wanted to be either at the top or
the bottom, and because she was not put there Percy
Norcutt, musical agent and editor, had to pay £lOO.
Ararat (Vic.) Town Hall was formerly a cattle and pig-
market, and a parsimonious council permits it to
endure as a relic of past glory, the cobwebs, in many
parts, not having been removed since the days when the
pigs used to grunt their welcome to the borough-inspector.
The Orangemen have now erected an opposition hall, but
as no R.C. was allowed to work upon it the Pope’s brigade
shun the edifice as a thing unclean. In fact, so far do
they carry their resentment that even Maggie Moore and
Holloway- suffered during their recent season.
Tenor Tapley has only just torn himself away from Aus-
tralia. He left for London per Oruba last week.
Monkhouse and Bradfield are playing together in “ The
Artist’s Model,” despite the fat comedian’s wrathful vow
that he would never be seen in “Braddy’s” company
again. Also the beauteous Ryley, Maud Hobson, and
Decima Moore are in the same piece.
Henry r Irving told an Australian in London the other
day that he had really made up his mind for a tour in this
country before he definitely retired from the stage.
The moral influence of the “ drammer ” is gratifyingly
shown at Melb. Alexandra, where Dan Barry and co. are
putting in “Ten Nights in a Bar-room,” a play which
powerfully illustrates the demoniacal characteristics of
Derrink. The cheap and impressionable audiences groan,
hoot, applaud and perspire with the greatest enthusiasm
during the progress of the acts, and between whiles rush
the adjacent pubs., and flood themselves with liquor more
or less accursed. The temperance drama should have no
intervals, and should close one minute after the pubs,
shut.
Melbourne gallery-goers patronised “The Notorious
Mrs. Ebbsmith,” but the d.c. people studiously absented
themselves, probably not liking the public attention drawn
to their exposed wish-bones and shoulder-blades by the
reflections of “ Mad Agnes ” on low-neck dressing.
A most realistic touch is lent to Duke Boucicault’s
gouty limp in “ Mrs. Ebbsmith,” at Melb., by reason of an
attack of sciatica which is at present troubling the come-
dian.
Pro. : There are not at present half-a-dozen shows “on
the road ”in N.S. \V. The “ depression ”in the profession
has never been so terrible as at present. Another gigantic
benefit in aid of worthy out-of-shop “pros.” would be a
heaven-sent blessing.
One of VV. and M.’s fixtures “ahead” is “The Trip to
Chinatown,” New York’s burlesque comedy success of
last year. The entire American co. which spread itself
over the original production is to be imported also. An
Australian co. is not equal to a prolonged wrestle with
the subtleties of Amurkan humor.
J. C. Bain, the Q. “ comic vocalist,” appears for a short
season with Harry Rickards, before leaving for England
to try his luck on the music-hall stage. Bain used to keep
a tobacconist shop in Barcaldine a few years ago, but
gave it up when he began to get kudos as an amateur
comique.
A Capuchin priest named Bambini died suddenly in
Sydney the other day. Memories of “ Tambour Major ”
and Johnny Wallace—Bambini, are you there ?
T. Mandeno Jackson, who graduated from Allan’s Melb.
music-shop to the concert platform, and who toured Aus-
tralia with the Belle Cole Concert Co., is getting on
admirably in London. Stockwell has not been so much
heard of lately. Both these singers, with Lennnone and
George Clutsam, owe much of their English success to Amy
Sherwin, who has become a most noted London hostess.
“ The Gateway,” a poetical song written by H. C. Cogh-
lan and composed by organist Turner, goes very well in
Melbourne, a first consignment of 500 copies from the
London publishers having been sold right away from
Glen’s. Ida Osborne tried “The Gateway” at a Melb.
Exhibition concert last winter, with an encore result. It
travels on the “Holy City” ticket and has a pretty,
haunting refrain.
Melb. Aok gives 17in. of space to a dull story of the plot
of “ Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith,” but the yarn isn’t even a
plain statement of facts. According to this long-winded
critic, “The fourth act brings Sybil Cleeve, the wife of
Lucas, and Agnes together, the former coming to appeal
to the latter to relinquish her husband.” The appeal, as
it happens, is just the other way round. Agnes has
already renounced Lucas Cleeve, and the wife comes to
ask her to reassert her influence to bring the man back to
his blessed career.
Prof. Heller, writing from South Africa, says the only
things which catch on there are circuses and semi-religious
shows.

The Wild-Cat Column,
The Bank of North Queensland has made a new
departure. Instead of laying itself out for short period
fixed deposits on the old system under which the banks
took money for terms varying from three months to three
years, and invested it where they often couldn’t get at it
for seven years, and trusted to Providence that there
might be no rush of withdrawals, it now sells 4J/> per cent,
debentures fixed for ten years, the money to be repayable
thereafter solely at the bank's convenience. This means
a great economy in one direction; as the debenture-holders
can’t rush the bank at a sudden crisis there is no need to
keep any reserve lying idle to meet withdrawals. There-
fore, money borrowed at 4} per cent, on interminable
debentures is much cheaper than ordinary deposits at 4
per cent. But in these days of falling interest anv bank
which pledges itself to pay 4$ per cent, for ten years ties
a big and dangerous millstone round its own neck. The
value of money may go up to 5 per cent., in which case it
will have made a good bargain, and it may fall to 2), which
will mean a heavy loss. And, seeing that the B.N.Q. earns
only an uncertain £1 4s. per cent, for its shareholders, and
keeps that on hand to cover depreciation, and that it could
easily call up more capital at the same cheap rate, the idea
of offering 4£ per cent, certain on £50,000 of debenture-
money seems grotesque. If the bank can offer that rate
of interest to anybody, why not call up £50,000 more capi-
tal, and pay the 4£ per cent, thereon ? It is a long
time since the shareholders have seen anything like that
dividend, and they would probably rush for it. As matters
stand, the B.N.Q. offers the public £2250 a year for the use
of £50,000, while at present its shareholders get nothing a
year for the use of £200,000, which is a one-sided situation
somehow. Moreover, the bank pawns its uncalled capital
as security, so it will probably find that while the deben-
tures last it can’t call up any of the said capital, except
for the purpose of paying the debenture-holders, and, as
it can’t pay them for 10 years, it is quite likely that the
B.N.Q.’s £250,000 of uncalled capital has been put beyond
its own reach for all that time for the sake of borrowing
£50,000 at exorbitant interest.
* * * *
The “ Flood Estate trouble ” is now reported to
be about settled. Said trouble arose through the National
Building Society (Sydney) buying a piece of land with a
£OOOO mortgage on it, selling it at a profit to small buyers
on time-payment, and taking their purchase-money with-
out making any provision for paying off its own purchase-
money. Therefore, when the buyers of allotments had
paid off their liability, or part of it, to the society, they
found that the society didn't really possess the land, and
couldn’t give a title, and that the previous owners were
about to sell it by auction for their unsatisfied mortgage,
in which case the buyers would have lost their land, their
houses and their money, all in one act. The difficulty,
however, as already mentioned, is being “ settled,”
though the National Society’s idea of a settlement is
rather primitive. It has borrowed £5OOO on the estate
from another society, therefore it still hasn’t got the pro-
perty, and has merely got extra time and a differ-
ent creditor. The buyers presumably will go on paying
their instalments as before, and if the National has no
better luck at lifting this mortgage than it had with the
last one the only change is that the people who have spent
their life's savings in making homes on the luckless estate
will be sold up a little later on by the new mortgagee in-
stead of being sold up now by the old one. The difficulty
is postponed, certainly, but the postponement throws no
new light on the morality of selling land without any
certainty about being able to deliver it, and taking money
for a property which the seller doesn’t actually possess,
and may never possess if things happen to go wrong with
him.
• • * •
The Standard Bank reports that it is still pulling
nard against the stream. Last half-year showed a loss of
£1513 against a loss for the previous half of £4908.
Against this, however, it takes credit for £10,161 interest
which was previously written off, or carried to suspense
account, or something, and which is now carried back
again, and thereby reduces the total balance on the wrong
side of the sheet to £9330. The co. doesn’t state that it
has actually collected this £lO,OOO odd, or that it feels
sure the amount will be collected ; it is merely stated that
it is credited on the strength of “ counsel’s opinion,” so
apparently it is passed as an abstract point of law rather
than because the money is actually there. As abstract
p lints of law are unsaleable this item isn’t so com-
forting as it might be. The last £4OOO of the
reserve seems to have been written off to cover
losses, so further depreciation must come out of the
shareholders’ funds. The Standard has reduced
the interest on its fixed deposits to 2£ per cent., and
thereby saves nearly £13,000 a year, and even that saving
and much economy in the management doesn’t keep its
head above water. Its total investments are set down at
£1,300,000, and they yield all round a shade over 2£ per
cent, gross. Assuming that they are lent out at a nominal
5 per cent, all round, this means that £656,000 worth are
d ad—temporarily or permanently. If they are supposed
to be lent out at an average of 7} per cent., which is
much more probable, that means that over £860,000 of
the alleged funds are so utterly stranded that the interest
is unobtainable and the principal more or less ditto. And
that would mean that all the paid-up capital and £300,000
of the deposits are gone aground.
* * * *
A correspondent draws The Bulletin’s atten-
tion (good old phrase) to the advt. in the S.M. Herald of
a firm called W. F. Coxon and Co., Limited. Said firm
seems to be in the time-payment, cheap macintosh, con-
certina, and all-sorts business ; it takes slow instalments,
and promises quarterly dividends at the rate of 6 per cent,
per annum. Also, it advertises for travellers with the sig-
nificant addition, “all employes must be shareholders.”
Which moves Tub Bulletin to ask, without insinuating
anything, the following few questions, which Messrs.
Coxon and Cc. are at liberty to answer (at not
too great length) in this column (1) How many
shares at how much a share has a man to take in
order to get a billet worth how much a week ? (2) How
long does he keep his billet on an average after he has
paid for his shares? Does he ever go out and thereby
make room for another man who will take up more
shares ? Doef, he generally stay long enough to get back
in salary what he put in as share capital, or does he gene-
ra! ly stay longer, or does he generally stay not so long?
(3) Does he get back his invested capital when he leaves ;
if not, when does he get it back ? (4) What sort of a voice
has he, as a shareholder, in the management of the con-
cern. (5) How many shares are there in the co. ; what is
the paid-up capital; what price are the shares selling at,
or are they selling at any price in particular ; and who
manages the co. ? In these hard times a lot of men will
risk their little savings in almost anything in order to get
a billet, and a concern which shoves its shares into circu-
lation in this semi-compulsory fashion naturally excites
interest.
* * * *
The Equity Trustees and Executors Co. (Melb.)
nas made a profit for the half-year of £483, and, as the
dividend at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum amounts to
£625, and £lB is written off furniture, the result is that
£l6O had to be taken from the previous balance to make
up these amounts. Last half-year the profits were £5BO,
and the dividend of £625 was made up then also by taking
the difference out of the cash on hand. The Equity
Trustees Co. has been in existence for eight years, and in
that period it has accumulated the lamentable reserve of
exactly £750. In the present half-year the net profit
doesn t seem to equal the amount received as interest on
its own invested capital, and the trustee business appears
to be wholly unprofitable. Meanwhile, the desire to pay
5 per cent, dividends under all circumstances is a thing
which the shareholders may be very sorry for by-and-bye.
* * * *
Re the charge brought against the Citizens’ Life
Assurance 00. that, in its industrial branch, it makes no
provision for liabilities upon policies on the lives of
children under 10 years, the actuary, in a letter to
Sydney Telegraph, admits that this is the case. The re-
port also states that no provision is made for liabilities
under the new endowment policies (table F.). The
actuary says that the point re children’s policies is unim-
portant because “ from any point of view the liability
could not be large.” Probably it isn’t large, but it exists,
and it isn’t provided for. And as the assets are only
£152,949, and the liabilities, even after making no pro-
vision for the policies above-mentioned, are set down at
£148,095, said liability only need equal £4900, which isn’t
so very much, to make the assurance fund of the Citizens’
industrial department insolvent, unless money is taken
out of the shareholders’ capital to make good the
deficiency. Under these circumstances it would be much
better to state exactly what this unprovided-for liability
really amounts to, and show the exact degree of its un-
importance.
* * « *
The Victoria Insurance Co. has made a profit
for the half-year of £7020, and it expends £8987 in paying
a div. at the rate of about 36 percent, per annum. There-
fore to keep up its gigantic div. it takes over £l9OO out of
the funds. The Victoria is a large, solid, and prosperous
concern. But even a big and solid concern shouldn’t
hunger after 36 per cent. divs. quite so violently as this,
especially as the business is practically stationary, the
reserve hasn’t been added to for a number of years, and a
possibility is ahead of heavy depreciation in the freehold
assets which may mop up a lot of the contingency account.
* * * *
Apropos the rejection by one vote of the Wages
Attachment Bill in Vic. Assembly (Yes, Assembly) and
the cry that the poor man’s credit would be stopped if
the Bill became law, it is notable that even now the Vic.
civil servant can’t be garnisheed, yet he gets as much
credit as he wants, and a goorl deal more than is good for
him. Besides, the fraud-summons procedure is an effec-
tive check on systematically-dishonest debtors. Gar-
nisheeing wages is a petty, but intolerable, form of
traders’ tyranny. Even the British conscience was
shocked at its existence, and stopped it"; but then Vic.
Assembly doesn't pretend to be as studious of the interests
of the people as the British House of Lords.
* * * *
Thu Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank
reports, in a glad tone of voice, that it) loss for the past
half-year is only £2616, which makes about £15,000 gone
in 18 months. This institution recently got a reduction
of deposit interest to 2.J per cent., and the new rate was
in operation for three months of the half-year, otherwise
the loss would have been £7500. If things remain the
same next half there will, on the 2J4 per cent, interest
basis, be a profit of 2200 or so, and as the bank’s affairs
don’t seem to be getting worse just at present this
glad result may be arrived at. The A.D. and M. has paid
off one-tenth of its deposits (about ' 68,000) in one lump,
which is something in its favor. Against this its reserve
fund has gone bung, its assets are admittedly, even on
paper, nearly £19,000 less than its liabilities, and it is only
earning £1 10s. and some odd coppers per cent, on its
alleged funds. Therefore the amount of its dead or
comatose assets must be something enormous. The bank
itself admitted lately that £73o,ooo—equal to more than
half its advances—were paying no interest, so what the
real worth of that £730,000 of paper values may be just
now is a thrilling conundrum. Also, the rest of the
advances are clearly not yielding 4 per cent, all round, so
what their present value may be is another conundrum.
Still, if the institution begins, as anticipated, to realise
even a small profit, it will doubtless overtake its losses
some time or other.

The Mortgage Guarantee and Mercantile Finance
Corporation of N.S.4V. has made a loss of £479 for the
half-year. The previous half it lost £B9O, and the one
before it lost £576. Roughly speaking, this concern
makes nothing a year on its own capital of £34,030, and,
apparently, its deposits and other moneys are so carefully
invested that they are a dead loss of about 3 per cent, per
annum.
* * * •
The chairman of the Balmain New Ferry Co.
writes, at much too great length for publication, to point
out that the apparent discrepancies in the balance-sheets
and other matters to which this paper drew attention
were all satisfactorily explained at the annual meetings.
This is quite possible, but when, to quote a single
example, a co. declares a dividend of £342, and the next
balance-sheet shows a dividend paid of £4lO, it should at
least account for the discrepancy. A sheet which is unin-
telligible in many particulars, unless the reader can
remember all the lengthy explanations given at a series
of meetings, is of very little use.
» * * »
About the saddest and most candid boom-tale
yet told was that narrated the other day by the Perpetual
Land and finance Co. (Melb.). The co., some years back,
bought a "property in Moray-street, Emerald Hill, for
£45,000, payable by instalments. When it had paid
£32,125 it offered to sacrifice this amount and give the
property back free if the sellers would only accept it and
call things square ! But as it wasn’t then worth anything
like the £13,000 still owing the offer was refused with
contumely. Then the Perpetual Co. offered to throw in
£7700 worth of city property, which meant that it was
willing to give £39,825 and the £45.000 property to be rid
of the £45,000 liability, but the sellers didn’t think the
said property even worth £6175, and again they refused.
Next, the Perpetual threatened to go into liquidation, and
the vendors were so terrified at the idea of having to take
tie land back at any price that they knocked off £1875 of
their claim, and reduced the interest, and gave more time
for payment. Said the Perpetual Land Co\chairman :
“This company’s losses would touch the heart of anyone
but a vendor of Moray-street property. I can only say
now that the shareholders and directors were idiots, and
1 was one of them.”
* * * *
J. B. Davies, brother of the acquitted and certi-
ficated Matthew, was absolved, the ot her day, by Vic. Full
Court from the statutory obligation to pay 7s. in the £,
and is duly whitewashed,of £593,600 of liabilities. J. B.
Davies never was a rich man except on paper. He was
one of the crowd which owned the Freehold Investment
Co., and that awful concern, according to the liquidators’
report, during the last 2}4 years of -its life made a loss of
£50,000, yet paid £llO,OOO in dividends and professed to
carry large sums to an alleged reserve. Its alleged profits
were simply paper, and its dividends were, in effect, paid
out of the deposits. Yet the law has apparently decided
th ’ there was nothing about the Freehold Investment
Co which it could complain of. Judge Molesworth rb- :
tused J. B. Davies’s certificate, but the Full Court over-
rules Molesworth and grants the boomer an uncoj*<Htional
release on the ground that his fearful company
wasn’t wrecked by paying £llO,OOO in dividends
when there were no profits to pay it out of, but
was simply overtaken bv misfortune or the act of God or
something like that. Also, the same Full Court went out
of its way to state that J. B. Davies acted as “ any
prudent man might have done ” when he incurred a call
liability of £453,000 with nothing like that sum to pay it
out of. Considering that the uncalled capital was the
principal security of the creditors, and that it was allowed
to appear to the public as a solid, reliable asset, and that
the public was invited to put its money into the Freehold
Investment curse partly on the ground that it was
secured by all this mass of uncalled capital, and consider-
ing that J. B. Davies must, as a sane man, have known
this and must have also known that his share of the
alleged security (about one-third of the whole) was mostly
bcrns -considering these things, how does the morality
of "the business stand in the Full Court’s idea, saying
absolutely nothing about the morality of the Freehold
Co itself ? The Full Cou t deliberately records its opinion
that a man who undertook to pay £453,606 if called upon,
when he couldn’t do it or anything like it, was a
“ prudent ” and careful person. Will it also state that he
was a perfectly honest and honorable person, seeing that
other people were led to risk their money on his promise
to pay, and that he must have known they were doing so ?

The Eleanora mine, at Hillgrove, is now run by
a man capable of handling it. Jolly, formerly of the
Junction and once manager at Nymagee, has put in the
finest concentrating plant in N.S.W., and is sending away
large quantities of calcined concentrates worth ±36 a ton.
So far, he finds the 30-heads quite enough for the mine,
on which there are 60 new heads that have never been
used and are not likely to be for a long time to come.
Whose mistake ?

Ructions are promised at a coming B. H. mine-

• * * *
The Leader has spread itself to boom Sheep
Station, and, consequently, David Syme is the best-cursed
man in Gippsland just now. There are two decent reefs
in the district, but the rest are nothing but squibs, and
most of the magnificent yields—6oz. and upwards—are
worked up on the edifying principle of averages : “ If a
bucketful of stone goes lOdwt., what will a ton go?”
Practically there is no telling what the ton will go,
because it is unprocurable. Tucker is very dear at Sheep
Station bread Is. a loaf. Carriers take a man through
from Bairnsdale for ±l, but will cart hi-m back for 2s. 6d.
Numbers of men are busy in the surrounding creeks,
trying to earn the half-crown.
An old and experienced digger, back from Sheep
Station, and much disgusted, replied to a Bulletin man
in Melbourne ; “ Oh, yes, I had some blanky luck. I got
three quid for pulling a dashed dead Chow out of a shaft.”

An excited individual charged into the Mining
Department, Melb., the other day, with his hands full of
specimens that he had picked up in a Little Bourke-street
sewage-trench. He wanted a lease on the spot, and evi-
dentfy expected to float his mine during the afternoon.
The geological authorities pointed out that the quartz
“ appeared*to be a broken-off portion of an original driftal
deposit accidentally caught up by the older volcanic flow.”
But the disgusted discoverer didn’t understand, and has
been dropping in every day since, with fresh specimens
and wilder appeals to be allowed to mine a great hole in
the centre of Smellville.

lords (with another 30 years “ life ”) extort 1) p.c. tribute.

up by his persistency.

Zeb. Lane (loq.): “The subscribed capital of
the Boulder is something like £135,000.” An admission
that a great number of shares were not taken up in
the float. It has paid 6s. a share in divs., and has£2o,ooo
in the bank; he expects to return the subscribed capital
within two years of the formation of the co. He speaks
well of Brown Hill mine, the Perseverance, and the Lake
View, but is quiet about many others. “ I have also been
commissioned to enquire into the statements of some of
the experts upon whose recommendations big prices have
been paid for some of the Hannan’s mines. I have
affidavits to prove that properties on which developments
have been described have never had a pick in them.
Others have reported thousands of tons of rich stone at
grass, when the whole of the available stuff for crushing
could be carried off in a dray.”
* * * *
Wired to the East that the Great Boulder did
not crush any stone last tinm at the Leviathan battery
because it was unable to get teams to cart the stone. Lane
says it was on account of the larger percentage of gold
left in the Leviathan tailings. Anyhow, the fact tran-
spires that teamsters refuse £1 a ton to cart quartz a very
short distance, which shows how cheap mining is in W.A.

Second clean-up from the Premier mine, 25-Mile
(Coolgardie): 75 tons, 1660 z. This time the excuse is that

been left in the tailings. The yield is good enough. Why
grease the fat pig ?
* * » *
Cue wire : —“Exceedingly encouraging news to
hand from all parts of the field,” and the wire proceeds
to show the richness of many mines. No crushings, no
yields and no divs, from which it may be inferred that
none have taken place. The Murchison has too many
batteries and too much water to play this game any
longer. The public are tired of it, and the continued
publication of such news in papers like the Argus and
S.M.H. is a sad commentary on their intelligence.
* * * *
Specimens half gold have been got at a mine at
the Norseman (W.A.) and have been sent on to Adelaide.
What for? The half-gold stone, from W.A. especially,
should be given a rest. It has lost its charm, and the
new crop of fools is not up yet. May the time soon come
when the man who displays half-gold stone will be put to
death!

The Anchor Tin-mine (Tas), bought for a big
price, is now at least temporarily shut down by order of
its London directors. Premier Brad don, whose name ap-
peared on the prospectus as a promoter and Premier of
Tas., says he holds no interest in the mine, but that he
gave his name in the public interest, at the suggestion of
the Mines Department.
The Tasmania Co. has stumbled on another
likely-looking reef in the bottom levels. Next to no
prospecting is intentionally done on the mine, although
the last annual Govt, survey showed that the co. was
crosscutting for stone, that it was actually driving on—
the co’s plans being grossly in error. This crosscut is not
being continued. Shares have fallen 25% since Bravo
Jones’s attempt at flotation.

The Racecourse Mill (Central), Mackay, has just
published its balance-sheet for 1895. On the average 7J
tons of cane yielded a ton of 88 per cent, sugar—a profit
for the mill of Bs. 4d. a ton. The Central Mill actually
paid 14s. Bd. a ton for cane, and : av 5 its shareholders an
extra Is. a ton into the bargain. Besides this, £1035 was
paid for improvements to machinery, and the concern
also paid off £4135 of capital lent by Govt.—so that the
state of things in view of the low price of sugar is highly
satisfactoi y.

CAMEO
A sudden change has come over the spirit
of the Melbourne magistracy scandal. Judge
Williams, who adjourned the Board for an indefi-
nite period because he had to go on circuit, and
his colleague, Professor Kernot, would have private
business to attend to presently, is not going on
circuit after all. Chief Justice Madden will take
the tour, leaving learned brother Williams to
grapple with further Board revelations. Also, the
Professor’s private business has lost its urgency,
and there is nothing to prevent Lormer’s allega-
tions of beak corruption from being heard and re-
ported upon with decent promptness and despatch.
Already there is talk in the street, not among the
tail-hat brigade, but among uninfluential citizens,
of a testimonial to Lormer, the man who opened
his mouth when a scandalous silence was the order
of the day. Lormer ran a great risk of getting
solemnly squelched by the powers that be, and but
for the hearing being so long delayed, and the
gradual arrival of evidence from unexpected
quarters, he would have got squelched for a cer-
tainty.

The story goes that, when Chairman
Wilson, of the N.S.W. Public Service Board, first
heard of some of the salaries enjoyed by certain
head-mistresses of public-schools—particularly of
the Infants’ Department—he' was simply flabber-
gasted. “Why,” he exclaimed, “it’s ever so
much more than many first-class bank-managers
receive to keep up‘ expensive positions and rear
large families on ! ”

THE DAY AFTER THE WEDDING.
HAPPY MAN : “ What was my pet thinking about?”
ADMIRING BRIDE: "I was thinking that I couldn’t hare really loved a man who wasn’t
handsome.”
The “chain” letter-curse device for col-
lecting money has reached Australia. Said device
consists in sending a letter to some individual ask-
ing for a subscription, and also requesting that he
make three copies of the precious document, sign
them with his own name, thereby taking the
responsibility of what may be a gross swindle, and
pass them along to three separate friends. The
three friends are thus invited, not only to subscribe
themselves, but to harry, on their own responsi-
bility, nine more friends, and the nine are re-
quested to subscribe and bring their personal
influence to bear on 27 more, and the latter lot are
mvited to cash up themselves and worry 81 more,
and these latter are to rouse up 243 of their per l
sonal acquaintances, who, in their turn, appeal to
oi berS ’ ai *d they are earnestly solicited to stir
up 2137 people they know, who are all to subscribe
themselves and pass the appeal on to 6561 others,
in U P an( l send it further along to
19,683 more, and these send it on to 59,049 —and sO
on ad libitum. In other words, the chain device
is a begging scheme whereby you make people beg
tor you and subscribe as well. What is wanted
in the present case is stamps. A children’s ward
is needed, so it is alleged, for a suburban hospital,
mid an apparently benevolent lady whom The
Bulletin doesn’t know states that if 1,000,0 >0
used stamps, not the common Id. and 2d. kind,
are forthcoming, some person not named will give
for them a sum not specified wherewith to build
the said ward. Which is about as vague as any-
thing can be. Who gets the stamps ? How much
is he willing to give for them ? What kind of a
ward is he going to build ? Who holds the stamps
m the meantime ! And who holds the person who
holds the stamps ? Also, is the “ chain ” begging-
letter curse coining to stay in Australia ?
Melb. Zoo. authorities have lately been
accused of cruelty for allowing dogs to be at
times caged with lions. As young lions in cap-
tivity are inclined to be hideously lazy, the half-
grown pup or small dog is put in as a playmate to
liven them up while the cubs are still too young
to harm the dog. They always become great pals
and play about merrily. Even when almost full-
grown the lions still hanker for their foster-
brother—and the dog reciprocates.

There are rumors of a vigorous anti-vivi-
section crusade in Melbourne, arising out of the
recent interesting snake-poison experiments ”
an f ro £"dissections recommended to future
candidates for matriculation in physiology. It
seems that, in sbme obscure regulations, a license
is required by medical men to escape the provi-
sions of the local Animals Protection Act, and
that, even then, anaesthetics must be used on at
subjects of experiment.
A keen medical controversy re snake-bite
antidotes has been carried on for some time past.
Some swear by strychnine and chloride of lime ;
others hold that more people are killed by strych-
nine than by snake-poison.—
These arguments do well denote
New remedies are risky ;
Also that people should
Hold fast by what is good :
To wit, the grand old antidote—
Whisky.

WANTED, AN ASBESTOS GAMP.
UP-TO-DATE STREET-PREACHER (during the late heat-wave): “ Yus, my dear friends, it’s ’ot,
an yer glad o them umbrellys-but it’s ’otter in ’ell, and there’s no umbrellys there."
Fever-accommodation at the Melbourne
hospitals is overcrowded owing to typhoid, and
many cases have been refused admission. Apropos,
it is stated that the stench at the Yarra steamer
berths “ turns the passengers ill and blackens the
ships paint inside and out.” The water is dirty-
black, thick and slimy, and the smell of it makes
people inclined to fall down dead. There is talk
of dredging the place, but in that event the
Southern hemisphere will have to hold its nose.
And Dr. Gresswell, Chairman of the local Board
of Health, says right out that the Yan Yean
water in daily use by the citizens is “loaded with
8 per cent by weight of putrid organic matter.”
There is in N.S.W. no legal obligation on
doctors to report typhoid cases. So much for the
long reign of the vaunted Parkes.
The Bulletin prints a pic-
ture of Maoriland’s champion
yacht, the Waitangi, and owned
by a triumvirate, Messrs Jamie-
son, Georgeson and Kirker, who
sail her for all she is worth—
which has meant first past the
post in every race she has entered
for, bar two. Her latest win was
the first-class yacht-race at the
Wellington regatta on 22nd
Jan., this year, beating the ex-
champion Maritana by 55min.
over a 32-knot course. At Lyt-
telton the Waitangi did the
course in record time, beating
the crack IMascotte on her own
water. Already in the ’95-6
season she has come home first
in the five races entered for, but
big time allowances have taken
three out of the five trophies
away. Logan, of Auckland, is
the builder. She was launched

A gruesome hot-weather story. A private
come b, the heat, the 2,5
aside and proceeded to camp underneath while
the passenger took a spell under a neighboring
tree. When the latter had rested sufficiently he
turned to rouse the driver, but found him dead
Lifting the corpse into the buggy, he started for
Windorah, but, overcome in his turn, fell out of
the buggy unconscious, and lay on the road until
rain fell and revived him. Again he made "or
Windorah with his gruesome load, and finally
arrived there to find the landlord of the only hotel
in the place also dead of heat-apoplexy.
* * * *
More women doctors. Already Melb.
University has turned out 13 women-doctors, and
this year’s entries show that there are 10 girls
more studying medicine, or one woman medical
student to every 20 men ditto.
* * * *
The much-gilt Miss Nellie Aitken, one of
Melb. ’s prettiest girls, is to be married next week
to Mr. Lionel Buckland with great pomp and cir-
cumstance.

Wail from New Australia: “If this is
Socialism, God defend me from it. The trouble
is, we all mistrust the laziness of the other fellow.
I had an awful grudge against one man I suspected
M.E. Champion Yacht WAITANGI.
Photo, by T. Pringle, Wellington.
of loafing until I found somebody else suspected
°f the same. We have no candles or kerosene
vf iV however, the fireflies here are big about
half-an-inch long—so we catch them, and secure
them m the cleft of a forked stick, and make them
serve as substitutes.”
thus a married man now in New Aus-
tralia: “ We have no light to light us to bed ; no
soap for washing ; we never taste tea, flour or
sugar. Our tools and utensils are wearing out,
and we cannot get fresh ones. Our principal food
is maize, and even that is failing. We cannot
possibly get back unaided, for we threw all we
nad into the movement,”
THE J.P.
“Justice is mine,” saith the Lord.
But the powers of darkness get level,
Grinning like one who has scored,
“ And the J.P. is mine !” saith the Devil.
S. S.
THE FATE OF FATE.
I riend Danton,”poet of the puzzled pate,
Permit thou me, a pundit poor, to state
that Tate, of Fate, is first and final date,
And thus the only Fate of Fate is—Fate.
P.M.
Find first where a circle ends,
And fix where it begins :
Mark where Space no bound
O puny mannikins !
Space pegg’d out, Eternity,
With dials regulate :
Then, Child of Humanity,
Fix thou the Fate of Fate.
C.

NAMDIK SEMAJ, Oxford Street, and George and Market Streets, SYDNEY.
Another voice from Paraguay is heard in
the columns of the Barrier Miner: “Gilbert
Casey is back, says the voice, “ and is whipping
up the scattered flock, but its members are riven
by conflicting opinions. It is a dull, hard, spirit-
less grind, and no ray of light can I see ahead. So
the most cheerful and the bravest have left in sad-
ness. H. G. Watson, whose early letters were so
full of hope and courage, has silently gone down
to Asuncion. Only the bushmen’s dread of being
laughed at by the tender-handed but tough-
tongued city scoffer restrains those who could
raise the money from silently stealing back to
Australia and fighting once more with the world
in the old way at Coolgardie. The education of
the children is neglected ; the young women are
already old, and mere mechanical drudges; and
altogether the community, which started out with
such high hopes and ideals, is getting surely down
to a plane no higher than that of the poor native
people whom less than three years ago they all so
heartily despised. It is one of the social catas-
trophes of our time. ”
* * *
The Victoria Regia, the great African water-
lily, flowered, for the first time in N.S.W., last
week at the residence of Mr. Hugh Dixson. Summer
Hill, Sydney. In Victoria, for 30 years past, the
annual flowerings of this plant have been a great
attraction at Melb. Botanic Gardens, where old
Baron von Mueller had a special house built for
its accommodation.
* * * »
Who says that the yellow aster is not to
be ? A green dahlia has been grown in the public
gardens at Oamaru (M.L.). The blooms of this
curio are small, perfectly double, and of a brilliant
green.
* * * *
Boss Chow laundrymen in Melb. are, it
would appear from a petition re the Factories
Bill presented to the Leg. Council by the
Tailoresses’ Union, gradually “ sacking ” their
yellow employes and taking on white girls to do
the work. They find it cheaper, they exp’ain.
And several vegetable-carts now traversing Melb.
suburbs are driven by Europeans, though bearing
the names of Ah Tip, One Lung, &c. The alleged
white of Cairns (N,Q.), identifiable by his habit of
taking off his hat whenever he meets a Chinaman,
will soon have a Southern prototype.
* * * *
Judges’-associates have a very soft time.
As a rule, they are near relations to the Judge,
and are in training as barristers. All their duties,
which are well-salaried, could be easily performed
by a court-messenger. Also, their pickings often
exceed their salaries. Any court-documents which
are to be copied are put in the care of the associate.
He has the work done by a miserable typewriter
at Id. or 2d. per folio, and charges Is., which the
unfortunate parties to a suit have to pay. In this
way, out of a very ordinary case, .£2O or £3O can
be reckoned on. In the course of law-reform,
attention should be given to this cold-blooded
swindle.
* * * *
“ Women are more imaginative than men.”
Our colonial word ! A Ballarat servant-girl the
other day drew upon herself the attention of half
the town by hanging out of a window in a
state of undress, and shrieking for the police, and
the fire-brigade, and the benefit of clergy. The
brave girl had found burglars in the house, and
attacked them with great ferocity, whereupon
they fled down a ladder. The ladder was there
to corroborate the story, and moreover, the rooms
were turned upside-down, and a gold watch and
other valuables belonging to a fellow servant were
missing. After imaginary burglars had been
chased for seven days and seven nights, and
shrewd detectives had gathered up half-a-ton of
footprints and other clues, the girl artlessly con-
fessed that she got the whole idea out of a penny
dreadful, and the missing jewellery was hidden in
her chest-protector, and she wanted to go home
to ma.
* * * *
Professional burying-parsons in Australia
will read with much pain that their work is being
done in Long Island, U.S.A., by phonograph.
The mechanical parson is brought along by the
undertaker and set up at the head of the grave,
where it reels off the service with much greater
impressiveness than the average black-coated
minister of grace. After all, it is merely the
change of one automaton for another. The par-
son who gabbles over a dozen coffins a day in any
Australian boneyard displays just about as much
sentiment as a hurdygurdy.
* * * * *
“Look here,” said a detective, lately
transferred from country to metropolis, accosting
a leading reporter on a morning paper, a man
whose name is known as a journalist of excep-
tional ability throughout Australia, “ I want you
to mention my name whenever there’s a show.
My arrangement with the press boys is 2s. fid. for
each mention, and that’s what I’ll give you.” And
the man of clues sincerely believes that the sub-
editors are leagued in a conspiracy to suppress his
name, and often does he commiserate with the
reporter on the loss of his prospective half-crowns.
The story of the N.S.W. one-legged man
who, the other day. jumped on his big brother,
locked him in a room, and then dashed himself
through the side of a house in the upper storey,
and tried to bore a hole to Tophet with his own
upper storey, after a 20-feet dive, makes the writer
want to tell a lot of stories about wooden-legged
men. He has often seen a timber-shanked party
back against a wall, unscrew his loose limb, and
defy “ the foorce ” to “ come on now, ye murdher-
ing ruffians.” There was a noted Brisbane ex-
man-o’-warsman who could sprint like a ped. on

♦ * * *
Recent obituaries record the death of Mrs.
Dight, a member of the historic N.S.W. family,
at a great age. It was her husband who took up
an early grant at Yarra Falls, near Studley Park,
Melbourne, and established the big mills there.
Twenty years ago, the Dights’ Saturday and
Sunday boating parties were one of the innocent
glories of social Melbourne. Many a young couple
took a plunge into matrimony close by the eddying
A FRAGMENTARY LOVE-PASSAGE.
THE GENT: “Ah! had I but windows to my soul"-
THE LADY: “ Whereas you, only have one, poor chap!"
currents of Dight’s Falls, and one man drowned
himself there on being rejected by a girl who
afterwards became one of the stoutest and most
haughty boom-matrons.
* » * *
Princess Helena is preparing a book of
etiquette for occasional visitors to royal and im-
perial courts, which will be full of “ hints on dig-
nified behaviour in august presence. ”, The ideas
of a Queen’s daughter as to how “ subjects ” should
comport themselves in an august presence ought to
be deeply interesting.
* * * *
Madame Blank, of London, advertises that
she is eorsetiere to all the leading members of the
dramatic profession, and to the officers of many of
Queen Victoria’s crack regiments.
A hint for women who love to languish
long over their little interesting events. The
Duchess of York drove out to her parents’house
to dinner 11 days after the birth of her latest
baby. It was certainly a special occasion—Xmas
Day—but that doesn’t alter the fact that the con-
valescent pulled herself together and performed a
feat which Mrs. Brown de Jones would faint at
the thought of. If Mrs. B. de J. wants to be
really fashionable she must make less fuss next
time she adds to the population.
* * * #
Melb. society staggers under the momentous
intelligence that Gov. Brassey has changed his
“trike ” for a “ bike.” He found that the three-
wheeler was too great a handicap when Mrs. Free-
man Thomas started to “scorch,” and now,
mounted on two wheels, an elderly Governor
bruises himself for an hour every day for the sake
of lightness and suite. Imagine Augustus Loftus’s
horror at the degradation of Australian vice-regal
traditions !
An Adelaide
lady who arrived in
Melbourne the other
day on a visit met
with a collection of
picturesque bad luck.
Before she left the
steamer her pocket
was picked of every
cent she had with
her —the average
woman goes into a
strange country with
all her assets in a
pocket in the back
part of her skirt ex-
pressly contrived so
that she can’t see the
man who is pillaging
her. She thus found
herself, with two
small children, a
cleaned-out stranger
in a new city. A
fellow - passenger
with four small chil-
dren was moved by
her troubles and
asked her to break-
fast in a restaurant,
and after breakfast
stepped out for a
moment to buy some-
thing. She never
returned, though a
distracted lady with
six small children
waited for hours in
hopes that she might
turn up, while an
aggravated landlord
besoug'A her to pay
for the breakfast —which she couldn’t do because
she bad no money—and get out —which was im-
possible, for she had no place to go to or to take
the other four children to. Finally, the devas-
tated proprietor gave the other four children into
custody as neglected infants, and the lady with
the other two children went away and got out of
their troubles somehow. And the moral of it all
is that the back part of a lady’s frock is an insane
place for a pocket, anyhow.
ROUGH DIAMONDS.
Far from the noise and smoke of towns
Dwelt her adorer ;
Upon the Western Queensland downs
He was a borer,
Who always, slightly through his nose,
Struck fire and water ;
And she, a fair and adipose
Bush public’s daughter.
A wild (grog) blossom, innocent
And self-reliant,
Of evil thought or evil meant
She was defiant.
A wide experience had she,
And nothing shocked her ;
She cured more cases of D.T.
Than any doctor.
His cheque was done. She coldly said
She loved another;
But he, no longer fancy led,
Married her mother.
And, when his case is diagnosed,
He’s not so far out :
He serves the drinks, while she, deposed
She scrubs the bar out.
Likewise he swears, when partly drunk
And ripe for slaughter,
- No darned bananer-eatin’ skunk
Q-
Will git his daughter.
R. A. F.
AN IMPRISONED SOUL: TO ANGELA.
The medical evidence showed that deceased, a young
girl of 19, died from a weak heart. —News item.
“ Weak heart,” the doctors said ;
Sweet soul, they did you wrong !
Though hearts with heavenly longing fed
Beat fast, they still beat strong.
Yours, caged, felt no doubt
That when against the bars
It beat its earthly being out,
Its soul would join the stars.
R. K. DEE.
Melbourne.

Perhaps it is—in itself—but you can’t get over hereditary

name of Ebenezer usually does it as an advertising move,
and the ostentatiously-holy are mostly addicted to sneak-
thievery and the more contemptible vices. No stalwart
bushranger was ever named Ebenezer. So, when you
meet Ebenezer, and Reuben, and Jedediah, and the rest
of ’em, you have to consider whether figs grow on thistles.

Sydney, March 3, 1896.
My Dear Moorabinda—
This is indeed a frosty time for old Adam, what
with revolting wives, new-womany talk, absence
of rudimentary decency, and all the rest of it.
And the other day a lecture (the hall was crowded
to the doors) was given in a Sydney suburb for
“ married women only,” “ on mental and physical
inadaptability,” by a female medicine-woman, Dr.
Ida Somebody. I can imagine the result—how
the sheep came home after the enlightenment
more determined than ever to strike ill-used atti-
tudes and burn the gravy, for what women see,
hear and read has a lasting effect on them—their
emotional adaptability insists on it, and all these
serio-comic problem plays and sex lectures, which
try to rub out women as women and substitute I
don’t exactly know what, are making domestic
joys very uncertain things. And what rubbish
women' are striking attitudes about nowadays !
Take the utter hysterical tommyrot of Mrs. Ebb-
smith’s shuddering moan and staring eyes of
horror on discovering that she—a woman —was
fundamentally womanly! By the same token,
every woman who suddenly finds that she wants
her lunch ought to grab hold of something and,
with eyes starting out of her head, shriek in a
dramatic voice, “Bring me a pot of tea and a
scone in the common, hungry way of my unfor-
tunate, contemptible sex! ’
Talking about sex, Miss Alice Muskett, in her
breezy and interesting description in Sydney D.T.
of some phases of life in the Paris Latin quarter,
discovers the fourth sex. “ The room,” said she,
“was full of men, women, and models.” This
will be comforting news to Mrs. Grundy, to whom
“ model ” and “ hussy ” have heretofore been syno-
nymous terms. How is it, by the way, that you
never, by any chance, read in an Australian news-
paper an article, a sketch, or a story from the pen
of any of the numerous gilded young Australian
women whose riches privilege them to travel
among the older civilisations? What delightful
letters are sent from Europe to San Francisco
Argonaut by wealthy, cultured Californian
women abroad ! And how is it that only once in
a. blue moon “ A Victorian Girl ” or an Alice
Muskett arises on our newspaper horizon ? The
rich Australians all seem to be either destitute of
brains or ashamed of their country.
Rev. C. H. Yatman, the hysterical Yankee
revivalist who has been turning up the oft-tilled
spiritual soil of Sydney, has now departed for
other shores, but I cannot let him go without com-
plimenting him on the symmetry of the order of
his discourses. For example, one day he gave a
“ Woman only ” afternoon on the subject of
“ Woman’s Special Work,” while “ Hell ” was his
starting-point the very same evening. It almost
looked as if the dear brother was illustrating cause
and effect.
Apropos of ghostly things, I am reminded
that a new convent lately blessed has its
looal habitation in three amalgamated terrace-
houses, one of which is the identical dove-cote in
which a much-married politician went successfully
courting bride No. 2 some time before No. l’s
shoes were quite ready for her. Evidently the
morals of a house are solely the existing tenant’s
affair, for now a rev. mother occupies the most
thrilling apartment in that historic domicile.
Which is interesting in connection with the theo-
sophic theory that evil lurks in houses like typhoid-
germs in a drain, and that the microbe of—er—
unconventionalism is as easily absorbed as the
others.
The N.S.W. military made the farewelling of
Major-General and Mrs. Hutton a prolonged
agony—numberless dinners and a conversazione in
the big Town Hall, which was rather too large for
the attendance and presented the appearance of
more flags than folks, though, to be sure, a man in
lobster fixings takes up the space of six black-
coated civilians as far as filling the eye is con-
cerned. Captain Gingah & Co. may congratulate
themselves on the fact that they did their level
best to make “ Uncle ” Hutton’s last moments in
office as much like the termination of a kangaroo-
hunt as possible; and though they pursued him
with corkscrews instead of stirrup-irons, the
result to the quarry was practically the same. The
most trying part of officialdom must be that you
oan’t get into or out of your collar but the com-
munity comes down on you in sections with corn
and oil and long-winded until the
bored recipients feel a wild longing to cut away
i nbeknownst per sheep-truck—if they happen to
te outward bound.
The blonde Mrs. Hutton took part in almost all
the farewell functions. Sydney will honestly re-
gret the Major-Generaless, who proved such a
tactful leader of society at a time when Guvment-
ouse appeared to be hardly answering to its helm,
and showed much practical sympathy for the
Sydney charities. Mrs. Hutton was generally the
best-gowned woman in the room, too, and she had
the art of wearing her gorgeous diamonds as
calmly as if they were strings of beads. If only
as a valuable object-lesson she will be missed by
many diamond-wearing and consequently worried
Australiennes. It isn’t at all unusual to see Lady
Marrowfat grab at her new diamond necklace with
an anxious expression every ten minutes or so.
A company of well-intentioned musical amateurs
under the inspiration of -Mrs. J. E. Scantlebury
(Miss Josephine Deakin), gave a pleasant enter-
tainment to their friends at the Masonic Hall,
Summer Hill, last Wednesday. “In Honor
Bound ” opened the programme, but the piece de
resistance was a new operetta “ Extremes,” by
Odvardo Barri. “ Extremes ” is a unique operetta
in that there is absolutely no male interest—it is
played altogether by women, which rather gives it
the air of a girls’ boarding-school break-up. It
was very well dressed and staged, but “Mrs.
Crabtree,” “Nora,” and “Clara” were the only
convincing members of a cast seven strong.
Nothing else —except that the clever pianiste ac-
companying the opera was the very speaking
image of Juliet Flegeltaub Wray.
N.S.W. Royal Agricultural Society means to
make a far-reaching splash this autumn with its
annual show, which opens on Ist prox. Most of
the Australian Governors have signified their in-
tention of being present, and, as all the leading
horses have also promised to attend, the show
should be particularly brilliant this year. There
is to be a banquet in the Town Hall on the evening
of opening day, With ladies looking on from the
galleries ; on the 7th prox., a grand ball in the
Town Hall winds up the show-week. And the
Governors are to be in evidence all the time. Ido
hope there won’t be any contretemps like that at
Christchurch (Maoriland) Agricultural Show, the
other day, when, after the toast of the “ Queen ”
had been solemnly proposed, the band struck up
“Daisy Fell.”

“Joe ” Darling and Clement Hill.

Ma Mie Rosette had a signally-successful revival
at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday. Memories of
Nellie in the name-part grew faint, and certainly
May Pollard’s warble is very fetching—she has a
dove-like coo in her voice that soothes like a cat
purring in your ear. But she wore too many
petticoats—as usual. The public resents even one
skirt on the shapely Miss P., and, when she
bunches ’em on in threes, there is a rankling sense
of injury “in front.” Mr. Pounds, as Rosette’s
well-meaning lover, looked like Walter Howe at a
fancy-ball, and wore a pair of mauve tights which
gave me a pain in the eye every time he stood
near Rosette’s rose-pink garment. But he played
and sang Vincent as that -ill-used gardener—who
has such a bad time of it in his best girl’s night-
mare—has never been interpreted here before, and
one heard for the first time what Vonsong was
howling about. A great difference was made in
the duel-scene. There was no clash of property-
swords as heretofore. “My lords will not fight,”
says the author plainly.
Wallace Brownlow played and warbled the
gallant “ Ongri ” even more picturesquely than
before, and, as a fatal consequence, the encore
fiend got his thumb on each of the bold, bad
monarch’s solos while the red-hot scene in the
second act got an enthusiastic hand. Mr. Brown-
low’s big voice has a clear field in “Rosette,” and
he can let himself £o without any danger of
drowning the other principals, for Royalty has the
orchestra all to itself when it elects to pour out its
soul in song. Only one thing I don’t understand
in Brownlow’s King, and that is when Jie alludes
to himself as “I.” Aren’t kings—and editors —
always “We”?
Miss Hetty Muret (with the speaking-voice of a
horse-marine) was new to Sydney in the flesh-
colored tights of Segur, and the new—l won’t say
fresh —faces in the chorus lent an air of unfami-
liarity to the guests in those awful garments of
state. Chorus-girls are the greatest of all argu-
ments against petticoats. They are so trim and
alluring in tights, and so suggestive of third-rate
milliner’s dummies when the plot requires them
to veil their principal beauty. Seeing the chorus
in long gowns proves that the woman who isn’t
Venus has all to gain and nothing (to speak of) to
lose in bifurcating herself. Even the back row of
the ballet have a certain opera-glass interest when
they demonstrate in pink tights that men and
women walk on two legs.
