Premier Reid, in his speech at the Hutton con-
versazione, enunciated a great truth when he said
Mrs. Hutton was the better general of the two.
Of course; the blonde and smiling Mrs. Hutton
had only to present the silky side ; it wasn’t her
task to clean out the Augean stables of the service,
so consequently she had a greater vogue than
hubby. Somebody said the other day that General
Hutton was all right enough, but he was a lot too
sudden. It was a real shame to have a great, big,
strong, active potentate, who simply ravened for
exercise and hard work, coming along in the early
morning and bustling men, some of whom had for
years never ventured to get out of bed in the
morning until they had taken six whiskies to drive
the snakes out of their boots.
The Medical Congress which has just subsided
in Dunedin certainly has a peculiar appearance in
the hotel-lists published in Otago Daily Times.
“ Wain’s hotel, Doctor MacParritch and one lady ;
Watson’s hotel, Doctor Burgoo and two ladies ;
Smith’s hotel, Doctor Mac Haggis and three
ladies.” But perhaps the public of Duhedin,
where they wear kilts on their morals, wildly
speaking, is too Scotch to grin at such things.
Writes my Melb. correspondent
Polo has been the local diversion during the
past week : sassiety ” thronged to Richmond
racecourse for the three days to watch the youths
of the smart set half kill their ponies in this reck-
less but high-toned sport. On Wednesday after-
noon, when the two Melb. teams were badly beaten
by Camperdown and Caramut, the gathering was
particularly fashionable, and the dressing of the
maids and matrons “Cupesque,” if I may coin a
word. The wife of the president, Mrs. Geo.
Chirnside, wore a serpentine-dancer’s gown of
blue pmk and green opalescent silk, with pink
silk sleeves. Mrs. Everard Browne, looking pale
but pretty, affected demure black and white
tweed, but let herself go somewhat as to the hat
which was vast and black and pink. Vice-royaltv
made the affair a kind of family picnic and went
out on bicycles, the babies and nurses follow-
ing m a capacious carriage. The bicvcle
costume affected by Lady Brassey and Mrs.
Freeman Thomas is graceful and pretty when
the wearer is riding, and it has been slavishly
copied locally to a button. In promenading in
the breeze, however, there is an obviously un-
wrinkled or wet-bath mg-gown look about the
lower part of it. The costume is made of grey or
dark-blue alpaca with full skirt and 8 jacket
worn over a white blouse with goffered frills and
j e iiT ai^0 Ti C S? ar a^SO mu °h befrilled. Lady B
and Mrs. F. T. who make a point of displaying
on all occasions how very much the maternal in-
stinct is in vogue just now, hugged their respective
infants half the afternoon. The three Govment-
ouse men played with the Melb. teams; Lord
Shaftesbury looked particularly dashing in his
polo dress. He played with much vigor, and
rolled his sleeves up to the shoulder, thereby dis-
playing elaborate devices of dragons, serpents, & c .,
m Japanese tattoo, mementoes of his recent visit
to the East. One Melb. player rode a miniature
cream pony during the first “quarter” of the
game, and emerged from the fray with his horse’s
flanks and sides dripping with blood. Even
sassiety, which makes a point of concealing its
feelings, was moved to horrified exclamation when
the quivering little horse was led off the ground.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
SPOUTING NOTIONS.
The Leger at Flemington on Saturday was a
ludicrous exhibition of bad jockeyship— i.e., bad judg-
ment of pace—on Gough's part. Nobody supposed that
James Gough had the head of a Tom Hales, but he was at
least given credit for knowing what to do with a first-class
horse in a 1J mile race when there is but one dangerous
opponent going. A wise rider under these conditions
never allows himself to be lured into the rear by his
dangerous opponent, whilst the d.o.’s stable companion is
plainly running away with the race. Whether W
Delaney (on Waterfall) fooled Gough into “waiting” too
long, or Waterfall couldn’t go any faster, is not a
question to the point. Wallace’s jockey failed to
overhaul Cabin Hoy because he couldn’t realise
the slowness of the race he travelled at for the first
94-mile, when engaged in watching Waterfall ; he dis-
tressed his mount by driving him along for the next mile
at something like record-time, with no more practical
result than a bad second to Cabin Boy’s easy win. The
horse that made the running from end to end was never
in trouble, whereas W’allace and others struggled under
the whip to make up the ground they had lost.
Kallara’s jockey was the only one who seemed to guess
how things were going.
Cabin Boy’s exercise gallop for the V.R.C.
Leger was a very genuine performance. J. Delaneyj
following instructions from owner Oxenham, went away
at a good sound pace, and improved his lead along the
back of the course. Before coming to the turn for home
he took a pull at Cabin Boy, bringing him several lengths
back to the field. Rounding the turn he gave the then
second-winded animal his head and let him “ win if he
could.” Cabin Boy, as it happened, oould win in the
respectable time of Smin. lSjsecs. And didn’t Ikey Mo
cheer !
When The Bulletin’s Melbourne prophet
selected Maluma as the best of three Newmarket Handi-
cap guesses the filly was still a 20 to 1 chance. She started
first favorite at S’s, and won nearly all the way. Indeed,
it looked as though young Redfearn had brought Mal-
volio's sister too soon to the front and would be “ pipped"
at the finish. However, nothing got near enough to
bustle the leader. Within eighty yards of the post the
boy glanced nervously over his shou.der, an amateurish
weakness which fetched a cry from the Lawn of •* Keep
your head straight! ” but otherwise he rode in business-
like style.
Auraria’s bad failure in the Essendon Stakes
didn t quite prove that the Cup-winner has gone amiss,
although it brought the hopes of her Champion backers
down to freezing point. What’s the matter with Auraria,
anyway ? She gallops freely, and looks very muscular.
In the paddock, she walked without showing any sign of
lameness, or even soreness worth mentioning. Yet, after
a rousing preliminary canter, the pretty filly seemed a
trifle tender on first one, then the other of her fore-legs.
And again, she appeared to be as right as possible. All
the same, Ikey Mo and the touts were right in the assump-
tion that Auraria couldn’t win last Saturday. They knew
more than the stable.
With Auraria reckoned a hopeless case, punters,
for an inexplicable reason., laid 2 to 1 on Hova for the
Essendon Stakes. They deserved to lose their money.
Quiver, an 8 to 1 outsider, met him on 4lb. better than
their Melb. Cup handicap terms, and now is the time for
mares to show their best form. On paper, there wasn’t a
point to choose between the chances of Hova and Quiver.
It was characteristic of public opinion re turf
“ certainties” that Coil could be backed at 2 to 1 on for
the Sires Produce Stakes. On latest form it was 10 to 1
against the good thing getting beaten. Coil had proved
too fast for Maluma at Caulfield a fortnight earlier, and
Maluma had just run away from five ana thirty horses,
most of them triers in the' Newmarket Handicap. But
backers know to their cost that nothing is certain in Aus-
tralian racing.
With few exceptions, the leading Victorian
jockeys are up to their ears in debt to some “ book ” or
other. Instructions from the trainer, or owners, are the
merest farce in these cases. Ikey Mo gives the all-impor-
tant whisper which decides the policy of the horse.
The absolutely worst exhibition of riding ever
given at Flemington, within the writer’s recollection, was
achieved in the run off for the Hopeful Stakes. The boy
on Aroha, who was winning easily, sprawled himself along
the colt, on the near side, and walloped it as though he
were mad. An ironical Hebrew offered even money that
the youthful artist wouldn’t fall off.
Oxenham, who didn’t notice anything wrong
with Waterfall after the Leger, got his first intimation
that the horse was lame from Alf. Joseph. Alf’s placid
eye had spotted a screw loose.
Homespun and Budgeree were clean out of the
Newmarket Handicap race when the starting-machine
rose. Moorite—who couldn’t have won, in any case—was
also left at the post. Foliage got first away.
» » * *
Owner Oxenham, though he started two, never
expected’to win the Leger. Of the pair 1000 to 1 wouldn’t
have tempted him to back Cabin Boy. Yet the public
always fancy they can beat Ikey Mo at the game !
* * * *
The moral of the St. Leger is obvious. Any
crock can beat a champion if he gets a sufficiently long
start. The Delaney brothers, on Oxenham’s pair, com-
pletely nonplussed Gough, Wallace’s pilot. It’s the story
of Wycombe, Australian Peer, and Abercorn, in Randwick
Plate, re-told. Gough waited for Waterfall to move, but
the jock, didn’t want to move so long as Cabin Boy was
going his hardest in front. When Wallace was set at top,
Pursuit was hopeless, and once more the public fell in.
he favorite started at 2to 1 on. In the race it was 2to 1
against Gough.
* * * *
Wallace must indeed be a great colt to run the
last mile of a If and 132yds. race in 1.40, and the con-
cluding six furlongs in 1.14. It was wasted energy, too.
* « * *
It’s generally good biz to back a mare for the
Newmarket if you back the right one. The sex has scored
10 times in 23 years.
* # * *
The Newmarket field certainly lacked sprinting
brilliancy, but it was large and contained good animals of
all ages and reputations. Class asserted itself, and
Maluma’s flutter was reminiscent of Bungebah’s year,
only her victory was even more hollow. Courallie, giving
a stone, scored second for N.S.W., as Vanitas did
last year, and the unfancied Pilatus and Disparity
formed the tail of the van. The winner was
highly fancied by trainer Redfearn, whose son had a
picnic ride. As she has been more or less fancied
throughout, and was first choice, Ikey Mo was badly
Struck.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Graham Berry’s pension may come on for
discussion before Parkes’s.
The N.S.W solidarity Labor party says
straight out that it will never consent to vote a
pension to Parkes.
How W.A. is being overdone may be
gauged by the fact that there are 2000 applicants
for billets in the Education Department
The coming S.A. election will probably pro-
duce more candidates than any previous contest.
Already 100 candidates are out for the 54 seats.
Settled that Duffy is to be the new Vic-
torian Speaker. Which will be the first Austral-
asian instance of son following father in the office.
One Yic. female telegraph-operator is re-
sponsible for the safety of £23,000 passing through
her hands every year. Her salary is just £1 os.
9|d. a week.
The Wellington (M.L.) Knights of
Labor have elected a woman as Master Work-
man for the ’96 term. Miss Lee runs a circulating
library in the windy city.
N.S.W. Premier Reid, as Solicitor-General,
is merely emphasising the iniquity of a political
Attorney-General. Law and Justice (so-called)
should be divorced from politics.
It now appears that a recent Ministerial
resignation in another province was not due to
Cabinet dissensions, but was entirely owing to
private financial troubles, as on a former Occasion.
Member of the Boodlewraith Cabinet :
“Yes, we’ve got a strong crowd, and a strong
platform. But our right-hand man is Thomas
Glassey, and our trump-card is ‘ Socialism in our
Time. ’ ”
Dr. Magarey, M. L.C., has declined the
S.A. Chief Secretaryship. If Gordon definitely
refuses to return it will be offered to Arthur
Addison, a country miller, or J. V. O’Loghlin,
editor of the S.A. Catholic paper. The fact that
the latter is of the religion is against him, as
Premier Kingston, Lands Minister Gillen, and
Govt, whip O’Loughlin are all more or less sons of
the Church.
Again the cheap and reli-
able. Recently six boys were
“recruited” at Tucopia. In
the latter part of ’95 they landed
on the Burnett, and four
promptly showed their appre-
ciation of plantation diet and
conditions by dying of dysen-
tery. The fifth gazed mourn-
fully at the majestic suite of
apartments allotted him in the
Kanaka barracks, and then (says
a local paper) “ deliberately
hanged himself. ” Hereupon the
sixtli appears to have gone mad,
for he was found plunging into
the river and swimming aim-
lessly seawards—probably mak-
ing for home again. This is the
sort of thing that is gradually
settling the Kanaka question,
and bringing Bananaland
planters to the conclusion that
perhaps sugar can be grown
with white labor, after all. You
can’t import a Kanaka slave
much under £3O, cash on de-
livery, and no man cares to fork
out 30 lovely sovereigns for a
bit of cold meat dangling at the
end of a plantation rope.
Coutts, one of the Fat Men
of Vic. Leg. Council: “We
never really go before our con-
stituents. T have been a mem-
ber of this Cnamber for 14 years
and scarcely once had an election
contest.” That is the secret of
all the trouble. A few of the
Fat Men go out every two years.
There is seldom any excitement
on at the time, so the people
don’t notice their exit, and so
few of them retire at once that
it doesn’t seem worth while
trying to shift them and to put
better men in their places ; thus
things are allowed to slide. As
often as not half the community
doesn’t even know that an
election is in progress, or
that the chance, which comes
just once in six years, for cast-
ing Sargood out on his ridiculous
head, has c-ome.
The Free trade proclivities of many parsons
are sadly undermined by the discovery that they
will be personally liable under the N.b.W. Land
and Income-tax measures. The softer sex, both
the clerical and the strictly female, adore cheap-
ness, and are Freetraders to a woman.
Q. Govt, has neatly scattered its elections
over a month, and put all the Govt, supporters
constituencies in the first batches, so as to provide
plenty of back-doors m case of defeat. In the
more advanced provinces the people have risen
against politicians who perpetrate this trick, and
thrown them out in their dishonesty.
Melb Democratic Club will probably soon
be reconstructed. The objectionable boxing ele-
ment is to be tabooed, a feasible debenture scheme
is mooted, and democratic work is at last to be
recognised as the proper object of the club, it
those now at work succeed, the club will probably
become a trysting place of Melb. sansculottes,
instead of being a target for justifiable jeers from
the enemies of democracy.
Quaint relics of last N.S.W. general elec-
tion are the remnants of two posters on South
Head road, evidently referring to the Woollahra
and Paddington contests. They now read “Neild
for Padding ” and “ Knox for Wool.”
The N.S.W. National Defence Guard, to
be founded at G. Republican Dibbs’s instigation,
will receive a large accession from Wollongong.
All the retired military men in that district have
applied for enrolment. They are —One. Newcastle
enrolls a unit also.

Yic. female telegraph-operators get no
increments, but the male operators do. Male in-
crements were to have been stopped some years
back, but agitation and organisation of males
(voters) put political pressure on, and they were
safe from the pruning. “ The age of chivalry ! ”
The Q. Premier “does not approve of a
treaty with Japan,” and “ notes with, satisfaction
that the number of Kanakas engaged in the sugar
industry is decreasing, whilst the area under cane
is rapidly increasing, thus showing that the white
farmers are superseding the Pacific islanders.”
Also, he states that he “favors a slight increase in
the payment of members, with a view to attract
and retain the services of competent men as repre-
sentatives.” ‘‘ When the Devil was sick,” &c.
What a wonderful political tonic is a general
election !
The curse of foreign capital is falling more
and more heavily on Mildura (Yic.) irrigation
settlement. The settlers bought their land at a
high price from Chaffey Bros., and gave mortgages
for the amounts due, the brothers contracting to
put the irrigation works in order and supply
water. The Chaffeys transferred their assets to a
company. Now it transpires that the settlers o: ■
Photo, by Noudin.
Mr. F. WEBSTER, Secretary N. S. W. Agricultural Society.
their money to the co., but that the obligation to
supply the water vests with the Chaffeys. lne
Chaffeys can’t supply the water. The co. threatens
to sell up the settlers unless they pay for the land,
which, without the water-supply, isn t worth any-
thing like the money still due, and if they are sold
UP they lose what money they have paid and
several years’hard work. Therefore they curse
the day when British capital first came to Mildura,
“We cannot do away with Party Govern-
ment. The only way is that men shall be elected
who will not make unscrupulous use of their
powers,” says Mr. Lewis, new Tory member for
Christchurch (M.L.). The gentleman may give
this subject a rest. There were never more than
two men of the kind he is looking for, and they
are both dead.
Herewith a sketch of chief-officer Stein,
who, seven years ago, created the Melbourne Met.
Fire Brigade out of chaos. Originally imported
from England to Melbourne, to take charge of the
old Insurance Companies’ Brigade, new-chum
Stein found himself in a hot and uncomfortable
place. Amateur arrangements for the alleged
purpose of suppressing conflagrations were at that
time rampant in almost every suburb. Smell-
bourne abounded in brigades. Once a year the
city witnessed a ridiculous
march round of second-hand
engines and vain - glorious
boozers, known as a demonstra-
tion, which m -rely demonstra-
ted the necessity for sweeping
reforms. When a fire broke
out, the great object of Mr.
Stein was to rush his own well-
trained body of men upon the
scene before the irresponsible
amateurs could aid the devour-
ing element by doing the things
they oughtn’t to have done,
and baulking his efforts to get
fairly on the job. Sydney has
had experience of the sort of
sufferings that Melbourne en-
dured at the hands of volunteer
fire-brigades. They mostly used
to set forth with a fixed inten-
tion of safeguarding the beer-
cellar at the expense of the
balance of the burning pre-
mises, and they came home
speechlessly drunk, in many
cases, if they met with good
luck. Superintendent Stein,
on behalf of the insurance com-
panies, fought hard against this
bad state of things for some
years. The nominal boss of all
the brigades, he, being a strong,
ready fellow with his fists,
brought main force to back
up his moral superiority. If
the salvation of a top storey
depended upon the overthrow
of a few blasphemous obstruc-
tors in brass helmets Stein
didn’t hesitate to lay ’em out,
so the public soon recognised
him as a useful man. The old
order of things came to a
climax at the famous Bijou
Theatre fire. It happened that
Stein was away from Mel-
oourne on that Easter Monday night, and in
his absence the volunteers were perhaps rather
more thirsty and uncontrollable than usual. J_ne
fixe raged in the Bijou, but refreshments abided
in the Palace Hotel adjoining the burning play-
house. The situation was faithfully depicted in
some Bulletin verses of the period —- The Mel-
bourne Fireman’s Story.”—
Besides, poor Brough and Boucicore ’ad got no beer and
So itlrops my ’ose when David shouts We d better save
the pub. !”
David was summed up in a previous verse
There’s my mate, David Feeder—ah ! a ’ero, sir, is Dave—
He’d go through fire and water solid h’eatables to save ~
And I make bold to say, sir, knowin’ well old Davey s
worth, " , ,
That at savin’ beef and pickles he’s the smartest man on
earth.
The Bijou calamity was Mr. Stein’s opportunity.
Anon he got instructions from Duncan (ililies, or
Fitz Gibbon, or some such potentate, to organise a
Metropolitan Fire Brigade, in place of his Insur-
ance Companies’ Brigade, the cost to be divided
between the Government, the various Melbourne
municipalities, and the companies aforesaid.
Whereupon he posted a circular to the volunteer
stations, requesting all enthusiasts desirous of
earning a comfortable living wage in the new con-
cern to send along their names. They all sent
along their names, and thus these amateur brigades
practically disbanded themselves. Stein, having
selected some of the most likely men started to
furnish Melbourne and suburbs with a really
efficient fire-extinguishing service. A master
of his craft and a disciplinarian, chiel-
officer Stein is clearly the right man m
the right place, and he seems to enjoy the
fullest confidence of the Board appointed to take
his reports. His telephone system of communi-
cating with suburban fire stations and public
buildings is perfect, and he shows quite a genius
in electric cont: winces for saving time when time
is most vjihri Withal, St6in is a modost public
servant, cia.nung only to be up to the mark, and
always ready to assimilate the latest improve-
ments.
The Vic. Opposition papers profess an
unprintable contempt for the Turner Administra-
tion because it has yielded to the weight of
opinion in the House m connection with matters
on which the privilege party would have liked to
see it stand hard and fast; but they are just as
mad with Turner because he is adamant in his
determination to put the unemployed on to en-
large Flinders-street station this winter, instead
of casting more new wild-cat lines in out-of-the-
way places—as the Fat interest demands, lhe
trouble is not that Turner vacillates, but that he
does not vacillate at the nod of “ Proputty.
The excellent bromide photo, of Carrie
Moore and Ivy Scott, published in a recent
Bulletin, was from the Melbourne studio of
Charlemont and Co. Through an oversight the
name of the photographer was not published with
the picture.

MELB. FIRE-BRIGADE SUPERINTENDENT STEIN.
I The Mr. Cuthbert whose name cropped up
in a spicy portion of the Lormer Board inquiry,
and who was called as a witness but didn t ap-
pear, is in no way connected with Vic. Solicitor-
General Cuthbert. Unfounded rumors to the
contrary merely indicate the tone of Melbourne
public opinion re the Lormer Board scandal. The
Crown has shown no eagerness to do its duty,
wherefore any little coincidence that looks like a
possible reason for this official torpor arouses sus-
picion.
Mr. George Black, M.P., writes :
The N.S.W. Labor-party, assembled for the purpose,
decided to run a candidate for election to the Public
Works Committee ; then, almost without dissent, they
decided that their select, if also the select of Parliament,,
should donate half his fees to propaganda work. This
condition being made absolute, four nominations were re_
eeived, including the names of Messrs. M'Gowan and
Cann. Together they did not poll more than half the
votes that I received individually, but neither of the two
can honestly attribute defeat to prior declarations that
they would not, if elected, abide by the Party’s decision
re fees. Such matters concern the Party only, but so
many garbled statements have been made by the jealous
and still further distorted by the malicious that I think
self-defence justifies me in making these facts public.
N.S.W. “Education” Garrard: “The
Ministers are modest men. I will content myself
by saying, ‘ By their works ye shall know them. ’
Q. Attorney-General Byrnes has done his
best to put Boodlewraith’s fat in the fire over the
North Brisbane election. He was member for the
Chinese constituency of Cairns, which he pro-
mised in so many words “ never to desert until it
deserted him,” but he threw it over without a
blush and told “the Party” that he must stand
for North Brisbane. The Orange section of the
Political Ass, knowing the constant endeavors of
the R.C. hierarchy to upset undenominational
State education, didn’t relish Byrnes one bit. No
more did Tozer, scenting a struggle for the Pre-
miership. But Byrnes has had his way ; and he
and Kingsbury—a Govt, lay figure with a plausible
tongue —are accredited candidates for the chief
metropolitan constituency.
One of the concessions demanded by the
Outlanders at Johannesburg, it will be remem-
bered, was that the teaching of English should lie
permitted in the public-schools. The following
snuffle, from a Dutch paper, is the chastened wail
of a local farmer who had been eaten out by
locusts :
God was very just in my punishment, for, had my
children not learnt English and been to a dance, the
Omnipotent would not have punished me in this way.
This epitomises exactly the views held by some
of the Boers on the subject of allowing any lan-
guage or ideas other than Dutch to take root in
the Transvaal.

Henry Lawson’s book of collected verses,
containing all his best Bulletin poems, may be
had post-free from this office for ss. sd. in stamps
or postal-notes of any province.
Eno’s “Fruit Salt” possesses the power of
reparation when digestion has been disturbed or
lost through alcoholic drinks, fatty substances or
want of exercise. If its great value in keeping
the body in health were universally known, no
family would be without it. Sold by all chemists
and stores.*
Personal Items.
Mr. Frank Wrench, late of Sydney, now
holds a commission in the Chinese army.
Of Melb. Stock Exchange’s first roll of 32
only Gavin Brown and J. S. Butters remain.
Mountmorganaire Tom Hall has now
settled in London and lives in Berkeley Square.
Gov. Brassey, during his recent Hobart
visit, fell out with Gov. Gormanston. Nobody
knows how they fell out, or why ; but they did
fall out.
From Adelaide Evening Journal
The Recognition op Women’s Rights —Mrs. Mary Lee,
who has been one of the foremost workers in the cause of
women’s rights, has been appointed a visitor to the lunatic
asylums.
The ever-dignified A’ic. Chief Justice
Stawell would have stared to see his favorite
pupil, Judge Hodges, as now visible daily in
Collins-street, on a bicycle.
Photo, by Beavis Bros
The Late Mr. J. B. GRAHAM , P.M.
The alleged portrait of Higinbotham
after being stowed away for months, has now been
hung in Melb. Supreme Court library, and
should be viewed by all who wish to see what the
revered Democrat wasn’t like.
Buckley, K.C.M.G., was allowed at Wel-
lington (M.L.) to take his seat on the Supreme
Court Bench for the first time in an ominous dead
silence, though the Bar was there in force. Which
looks bad for both Bench and Bar.
The numerously-signed petitions for N.S.
Wales Justice Windeyer’s removal, which were
never presented through the illogical reaction of
the popular mind when Dean confessed, are locked
up in the Sydney Safe Deposit vaults.
Gov. Brassey sends along a cheque for £25
to the Trenwith fund. The aristocracy in politics
has also come down pretty liberally, it being
understood that the Vic. Labor-leader will have
his eyes opened in “ Urup,” don’t you know.
Mr. John Cooper, brother of the baronet,
and principal owner of at least one of the divisions
of the Cooper estate, is again in Sydney. A new
policy of dealing with some of the vast Cooper
interests is expected to be introduced —and not
before it is needed.
Journalist Whitaker, formerly sub-editor
of the defunct Melb. Standard, now on Wes-
tralian Hansard, has signed a contract with that
smart young man Calvert (and valet) for a four
months’ trip to England to write up the goldfields,
the sum to be paid being £5OO.
Laureate Austin, the penny-in-the-slot
poet, is entitled, since his election to the position
of kept-bard, to draw all the back-salary since
Tennyson’s death, also the arrears of sack. There
is a precedent for this in the case of Dryden. Pre-
sumably Austin wrote “Jameson’s Ride” after
drinking all the accumulated sack.
It was said that Princess Beatrice brought
about the appointment of Alfred Austin as Poet
Laureate, in order that there might be an official
bard to glorify the valor of her Battenberg when
that luckless warrior came back from Africa. Poor
Battenberg returned in a wooden ulster, and Austin
was required to chant a requiem.
What became of the privately-printed
pamphlet of the great Geelong scandal, owned by
J. Bracebridge Wilson, late headmaster of the
Geelong Grammar School ? It was not seen
amongst his books as catalogued for sale ; and
scores of pounds would not buy it. It is be-
lieved, if Wilson’s copy has disappeared, that not
one now exists.
Humored that Gov. Brassey will take a
trip to England next year ; also, that about the
same time he will ask the Colonial Office to relieve
him of the rest of his term at Melb. Guvmentouse.
The. bazaars, and agricultural shows, and tea-
meetings are said to be damping the Brassey
ardor, an 1 it will only be the delay of that much-
desired earldom which will make the serious old
gentleman continue his course in Vic.
Mrs. Hannah Hall, relict of James Hawkes-
bury Hall, formerly of Macquarie Retreat,
Hawkesbury River, who died on 19th Feb., and
had reached her 90th year, is believed to have
been the oldest N.S.W. native. She was born at
Portland Head, Hawkesbury River, in Nov., 1806.
Brokenhillionaire George M‘Culloch will
shortly take possession of the palace he has built
in Queen’s Gate, London. It is estimated that the
value (or at any rate the cost) of the works of art
he has bought runs into £ 120,000. But it is not
often that art dealers get a chance of shearing a
Broken Hill fleece.
It is now known that the donor of the
splendid dome to Brompton Oratory, which
must have cost over £5OOO, is Mrs. Bellasis
(late Campsie-Dalglish) nee de Lauret, of
N.S.W. It is one of the most conspicuous land-
marks in London. Mrs. Dalglish’s former husband
got into Broken Hills early and made a big pile.
The death is announced of Vice-Admiral
Robinson, brother of Sir Hercules of that ilk.
Deceased was on the Australian station in the late
60’s or early 70’s as commander of H.M.S.
Pioneer, and married a daughter of the late Mr.
William Bradley, of Sydney and elsewhere in
N.S.W., for which slice of fortune he was indebted
to Lady Robinson, who was a great match-maker.
Habitue : “ Assiduous Gagliardi would be
impossible as Melb. public librarian only because
he is too foreign, and not mobile like another
Italian, Panizzi, the London man. The librarian
ought to be a youngish but mature Australian, sent
round just to see the British Museum, Parisian
Bibliotheque Nationale Boston Public Library,
and the Chicago one. ”
Ex- Premier Parkes, who has lately had to
contend with many rebuffs, took his gruel at
Waverley very badly. Although he spoke up
sp'ritedly from the hustings, and said he was just
as pie sed he had not been elected —his usual gag
—tad old man broke down completely immediately
after, and has since been in a weak and uncertain
state of health.
J ustice Holroyd is more than respected by
the Victorian lawyers. The other day the follow-
ing scene took place :
Ooldham : “ But your Honor’s all wrong-”—(then after
a pause)—“with respect.”
Holroyd (anxious to get at the real facts): “ Never
mind the respect! ”
Coldham : “ But I can’t help it, your Honor.”
Whereupon those in court almost said “ Hooray!”
“ T. M. Sheridan, the abortionist,” writes
a Bulletin correspondent from Queensland, “ was
at one time a chemist on the Roma-Dalby line,
and was favorably known to hundreds, and gener-
ally esteemed as one < f the kindest of men.
Fever was raging there during Sheridan’s time —a
man died for almost every sleeper on the line—
and the ministering angel to many a poor, parched
and dying navvy was the man who lately paid the
last penalty of the law in the city of Sydney.
Night and day, wet and sunshine, Sheridan
tramped from tent to tent; money, or no money,
he always supplied medicine ; till, weary of it all,
he left the West—wandered South and died—a
felon 1 ”
Photo, by Brand
“ bob ” FITZSIMMONS, Champion Pugilist of the World.
Bird, J.P., confessing his weakness for “re-
forming ” soiled doves to the Lormer Enquiry
Board, was humorous, but not as humorous as Bird
subsequent to the event. When the giddy old man
was loosed from his examination, after having
been fairly “roasted” by witnesses and counsel
alike, he rejoined his friends, quite proud and
happy, saying, “Didn’t I come through flying,
eh? Didn't I come through flying?” He was
fully persuaded that his irrelevant ravings con-
cerning his ancestors, and so forth, had distracted
the Board’s attention from his magisterial eccen-
tricities.
Phil. Greenwood, over eight years secre-
tary to the Firm, is almost knocked out by illness,
and early this month takes a trip to England to
recruit his health. Phil, has always been a good
fellow, and an earnest worker for charities,
especially for distressed actors’ fetes. Now that
his dark day is on him, his friends are rallying up
and filling a purse with sovereigns. Clifford
Ferley is hon. sec. and Geo. Tallis hon. treasurer,
and both will be glad to send their autographs in
return for a shekel.
By the recent rather sudden death of Mr.
J. B. Graham, P.M., of Bathurst, Suffield, con-
victed of criminal assault before Judge V indeyer
and sentenced to ten vears’ imprisonment, will lose
a good friend ; for the deceased magistrate made
no secret of his emphatic belief in Suffield s
innocence. Mr. Graham, a bachelor of 54 years,
and a nephew of “ Jack ” Robertson, had many of
the best traits of that old warrior, and was
esteemed by all who knew him as an able, humane
and fearless magistrate.
Once again N.S.W. litigants are suffering
from the indifferent health of the Judges. Judge
Windeyer is at present away on a health-c-wm-
holiday tour; Judge Manning is rusticating at
Bowral, and not likely to be in harness before
April ; Judge Innes, who has just concluded a
spell off, is still in weak health, and may have to
leave at any moment; and Judge Stephen is just
back from a year’s holiday. About the only really
robust occupant of the Bench is the Chief-J ustice.
Since Parkes’s third marriage, so goes the
story, Edmund Barton and the old man have been
rather less friendly. It was to Barton that
Barkes communicated, in writing, an emphatic
denial of the rumor that he was going to marry
for the third time ; and the former, with charac-
teristic justice and good nature, nailed the rumor
whenever it cropped up, only to discover after-
wards that the gay old boy had been “ pulling his
leg.”
Ballington Booth, who, it is cabled, has
now caved in, took the stand of his predecessor,
“ Major ” Moore, in the U.S. When “ General ”
Booth recalled Moore, the latter flatly refused to
come and tried to form an American Army, but
ignominiously failed. “ Colonel ” Barker had such
a pull in Australia that he refused, when first called
on, to shift, and had to be humored awhile.
Booth’s sons appear to be shaping like those of
the Indian or Afghan potentates —the Abdur
Rahmans and Yakoob Begs.
Mr. F. AVebster, the energetic secretary of
N.S.W. Agricultural Society, is the first white
man born in Victoria Barracks, Sydney; his
father was Captain Webster, of the 11th Regi-
ment. When first he was secretary —13 years ago
—the society’s annual “ gate ” totalled but £9oo—
it is £2400 ; the sole catering right cost but
£2o—now £450 wouldn’t buy it; the show’s
entrance fees reached only £70 —now they total
£5OO. Mr. Webster ’cutely seized the cycling
boom the moment it presented its forelock; he
laid down a modern fast banked track, and
invested the N.S.W. wheelmen’s money in a new
grand-stand on the Webster estate. He has
arranged for all the Australasian Governors to
appear at the big April show—at the expense of
the N.S.W. Governor. He is now working up a
magnificent military tournament for “ the show,’
and expects contingents from all the provinces.
On opening day all the Governors will arrive with
a gorgeous guard of honor, and the sec. will
receive them with his hat off, as shown in the
photo, printed in this issue.
Amongst the Jameson filibusters
captured by the Boers is Gordon
Grimes, the younger son of “Jemmy”
Grimes, formerly of Toowoomba, Q.
He was in the Bechuanaland Police, a
corps lately disbanded, and joined in
Jameson’s unsuccessful raid.
G. P. Doolette, the somewhat
pious erstwhile Adelaide man who is
making such a splash in London, and
is reputedly a millionaire, drew his
initial success out of Broken Hills.
He was a humble trader in King Wil-
liam-street, Adelaide, when “ Sammy”
Beddome, then Adelaide P.M. and now
a wealthy man himself, put him at a
low price into original Brokens, which
he sold out at the very top of the
boom. Doolette has purchased a big
cattle-station in 8.A., and intends to
eventually settle down as a horse-
breeder.
Carping critics profess to be
mystified by John Haynes’s published
correspondence on Japanese and other
leathers. But John, M.P., was not
the first of his name to print senti-
ments of occult significance. “John
Haynes informs the public that he
possesses the true means of making
leather breeches sit easily,” is said to
be the cryptograph which led Gan-
ganelli to suppress the Jesuits. John,
M.P.’s letter on the boot-trade may
cover a plot for the deportation of
Cardinal Moran.
Henniker Heaton’s proposal,
reported to have been accepted, that a
banner emblazoned with the arms of
the Metropolitan See of Australia
should be hung near the cenotaph of
Bishop Broughton, in Canterbury
Cathedral, recalls the fact that that
bishop, when in Sydney, initiated a
movement in public protest against
the Roman Catholics of N.S. Wales
being allowed to form a See, or possess
a ruler with the title of Archbishop.
Polding was at that time setting up
house, and the modest Broughton, who
was also a bit of a politician, and sat
in the Legislative Council, claimed to
be the sole head of Christian Australia.
“Does ‘the Banjo’ smoke ?” enquires a
lady correspondent of The Bulletin. “It is
noted as a curious thing that throughout his book,
‘ The Man from Snowy River,’ he does not men-
tion tobacco or a pipe. The BulleHn (Feb. 9,
’95) mentioned, in a note on Gordon, that ‘ the
pipe is closest to his heart of all,’ and said:
‘ Every bush poet puts his pipe—and his horse
and a woman—into his poetry.’ ‘The Banjo’
puts a whole stud of horses into his book, and not
a few women, but where is the pipe?” “The
Banjo ” does smoke.
The story goes in London, says a Bulletin
correspondent, that the action brought by Mrs.
Langtry for the recovery of her diamonds from
the Union Bank of London will be a cause celebre,
if not squared, as the brilliants are alleged to be
the family jewels of a certain nobleman, and are
said to have been obtained from Mrs. Langtry’s
bank by a ruse on the part of that nobleman’s
countess —a very beautiful woman, who years ago
ran away from her former husband, was divorced
by him, and then she married her friend the noble-
man. If the rumors current are all true, the lady
is now reaping the whirlwind. ,
Wullie M’Millan, M.P., presided at the
t’other-day Sydney Town Hall meeting, at which
the Irish corps of volunteers was enrolled. In
manners, canniness, looks, and name, the perky,
brainy little Mac is as Scawtcn as Scawtch can be,
but he has a claim to ca’ himsel an Irisher, and he
kens how to use it. M’Millan’s Irish tie is of
“the black North,” and his father, Gibson
M‘Millan was a meenister at Londonderry—the
town that was never captured ! Con^^ u h entl 7’
Wullie was well-placed, presiding over the broths
of boys whose memories cling to the South
the Slatterys, Freehills, Daltons, Tooheys, and
O’Hooligans.
Mr. PHIL GREENWOOD.
John Deasy, formerly M.P. for West
Mayo, who visited Australia some years ago with
Baronet Esmonde and John Dillon, is dead. He
is best remembered in N.S.W. by a love-affair in
Maitland district, in the course of which he
charged the local postmaster with tampering with
his letters. The official was acquitted by a Royal
Commission which elicited, amongst other things,
that a lady concerned had, per aid of steam, peeped
into the contents of the love-missives, and quietly
squelched the intended match. At ’Ome Mr.
Deasy fell foul of a “ slavey ” who charged him
with assault, for which he was duly fined, and, as
a consequence, he resigned his seat in Parliament
and retired into private life.
It is not regarded as complimentary (writes
“J. Drayton ” to Sydney Truth) to refer to any
Westralian gentleman as a descendant of one of
the first families. The founder of one of the first
families came here to stay, because it was not
clearly proved that it was he who cut the head off
the captain of the ship when the crew mutinied.
But someone must have done it. Because when
the mate dived into the flesh-pot to pick out a
nice fat piece of beef for his mid-day meal, it was
the captain’s head which grinned at him on the
hook of the searching-fork. The mate was of
opinion someone killed the captain, and was
strengthened in this opinion by the reflection that
he was not the kind of man who would have cut
his head off and put it on to cook for a lark.”
Concerning Peacock, of the Yic. Ministry,
and his laugh. That undisciplined burst of merri-
ment has long been a luncheon institution at
Melb. Stock Exchange Club, where other mem-
bers, by way of protecting against disturbances
during meal-time, would rap upon the table with
their knife-handles every time Peacock gave
tongue. Apparently Peacock’s hide is thinner
than might be supposed. The table-rapping
practice touched his dignity, and the order, “ Let
no dog bark when a Crown Minister shrieks,”
was served privately upon the club’s manager. A
few days ago Peacock laughed as usual, but only
two members retaliated with their knife-handles,
all the other lunchers sitting wrapped in thought
as though they had heard nothing dreadful
going on. Presently, when the companion
of these two delinquents was hurrying
away to keep an appointment, the manager
stuck him up with a request “ not to rap upon the
table every time the Chief Secretary laughs.”
The member thus appealed to happens to be a
solemn-eyed city man, for whom Vic. politicians
possess very little glamour. He slowly placed the
manager under observation and said: “ I unfor-
tunately forgot to knock just now, but I am glad
of the present opportunity to mention that Mr.
Peacock’s noise is a nuisance. Kindly tell Mr.
Peacock that, for the future, I will never permit
his scandalous tumult to pass without administer-
ing a counter-irritant with a knife, fork, or spoon.
Kindly put my resolution in writing and give it to
brother member Peacock before he can further
annoy the peaceable supporters of this club.”
Whereupon the manager gasped in horror, and the
injured protestor retired gracefully.

St. John’s Works, W.P., LONDON,
Government Notices.
** Land and Income Tax Assessment
Act of
NOTICE is hereby given, under section 30 of
the above Act, that Returns of Raced
and Income for the purposes of the assessment
of Land and Income Tax are required to be fur-
nished to the Commissioners of Taxation, at their
Office, on or before the 27th day of Mar h,
1896; and all persons who are owners of
land, or in receipt of income, and all persons
liable under the provisions of the Act in
any representative capacity to furnish re-
turns for the purposes, aforesaid, are hereby
informed that the prescribed forms of returns, ex-
cept those for Companies, are now available, and
may be obtained on application at any Post
Office, Police Office, Custom House, Rail-
way Station, and the Office of any Clerk of
Petty Sessions in New South Wales, Stamp
Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at
the Taxation Office. Custom House, Sydney.
Forms for Companies can be obtained at the
Stamp Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at
the Taxation Office, Custom House, Sydney.
All owners of land are required to furnish
Returns of the whole of the lands owned by
them, notwithstanding that the value of such
lands, or any portion thereof, is below the ex-
empted amount of <£24o.
All persons carrying on any business, trade, or
profession on their own account are required to
furnish Income Returns irrespective of the amount
of their income; but no Return of Income is
required from any person in receipt of
Salary or Wages only, if the gross annual
amount of such salary or wages does not exceed
<£2oo.
Public attention is called to the fact that a
penalty of Twenty Pounds is imposed on any
person neglecting to furnish the return required of
him within the time above mentioned, and that
persons failing to furnish such return are not
relieved from liability to the penalty by reason
only that they may have received no notice to fur-
nish the return, or that the prescribed form may
not have been delivered to them.
JAMES POWELL,‘'I
R. N. JOHNSON,
L. S. SPILLER,
Commissioners
of Taxation.
Land and Income Tax Office,
Custom House, Sydney,
20th February, 1896.
Particular attention is called to the observa-
tions and directions on the Forms of Return.

Dear Bulletin, —Prof. Morris, in his
derivation of “gone-bung,” is even farther astray
than you assume. “ Billabong ” does not signify
a pond of dead or motionless water. It is the
aboriginal equivalent of “ana-branch a natural
by-wash or secondary channel, running, as a rule,
only in flood-time. The Billabong Creek is a
billabong of the Murray ; so are the Edwards, the
Gulpa, the Wakool, and a host of minor intermit-
tent streams. A billabong may form the flood-
connection of two separate rivers ; the Willandra
Billabong is an example. But there is as much
difference between a billabong and a lagoon as
between a State-bank and a Mercantile ; and the
integrity of the aboriginal term should be pre-
served. Of course, “billabong” may suggest
“gone bung” when the faithless stream is dry,
not otherwise.—Tom Collins.
The steadily growing demand for Parker’s
Australian Hair Preserver not only marks its
growing popularity, but undoubtedly stamps the
efficacy of the preparation. The many testi-
monials from well-known residents show that,
in the near future, no dressing-table will be
complete without it. #
At one meeting of the Liberator Building
Society committee, Jabez Balfour placed the
following resolution on the agenda paper :
That, on the occasion of taking possession this day of
our new premises, the directors desire to record their own
sense of thankfulness for the prosperity with which, as
they believe, God has hitherto blessed their efforts in the
establishment of this business.
Says a London paper, commenting on the cant of
the greasy Pharisee : “ Certain it is that a very
large number of the Liberator victims were simple-
minded, God-fearing, chapel folk, ensnared actu-
ally by their own pastors and office-bearers in
temperance, religious, and thrift organisations,
all of whom were hired and paid by commission
to tout for the Balfour group of companies.” How
closely the tactics of Jabez were imitated in
Melbourne !
The eviction of Tenetahi, an ancient
Maori, whom the Seddon Ministry recently cast
out by force of arms from his ancestral home on
Little Barrier island, is being worked for all it
is worth by the Tory press of Maori land. The
pathetic old man, dragged in his declining years
from his humble home and brutally “ chucked ”
into a cold world, has been dished up in many
different shapes, and Seddon, as the base
oppressor, has become a bye-word. Tenetahi
and his tribe sold their ancestral home to the
State, and religiously collected the money. Then
they all gave up possession, except this one
pathetic old wreck, who simply stayed on, and
shook his hoary locks at the purchaser, and talked
pathos about the resting-place of his fathers, and
his declining years, and so forth. And, now that
he has been evicted, he is going up and down
the country quoting poetry of the 1 ‘ Old Dog Tray ”
description and smoking the strongest plug-
tobacco, and the Tory papers want to know if
Maoriland will endure this brutally-oppressive
Ministry any longer.

Apropos of the present denunciation of the
State-school system by the R.C. hierarchy, it may
be remembered that the R.C. and Anglican pre-
lates made a similar onslaught on the English
Education Act of 1870, which they attacked
as “destructive of religion and morality.” In
1870, when the population was under 23,000,000,
the number of prisons was 113. In 1890,
when the population had reached 26,000,000, the
number of prisons had fallen to less than 60. At
that time (1870) the number of children in schools
was 1,400,000. It is now over 5,000,000. “And
in that period,” says Lubbock, “ crime has de-
creased by at least a' half.” Drunkenness shows a
similar and even more marked decline. Full
State-schools, in the long run, make empty prisons
and a sober people.

THIS Syndicate is being formed to acquire 120 acres of Freehold Land, situated immediately north
of the celebrated Nymagee Copper Mine.
It will be seen on reference to the plan that the workings of the Nymagee Copper Mine have been
extended half-way across the road which separates the two properties. The recent valuable discoveries,
which have so materially enhanced the price of Nymagee shares in the market, are in the extreme
northern end of that Company’s property, and are dipping rapidly into the North Nymagee Mine.
The report of Mr. W. blakemore, General Manager of the Nymagee Copper Mine, is appended
hereto, and from which it will be seen that the property now being offered is an exceptionally valuable
one, and in his opinion the various rich lodes now being so remuneratively worked by the Nymagee
Company, will no doubt be proved to exist in this Company’s property ; but, in addition to this, large
deposits of Oxidized Copper Ores can at once be raised and profitably disposed of, and a valuable
Silver Lead Lode, the existence of which is proved, only requires developing to form a lasting source
of profit.
The Nymagee Company, since its formation, has distributed the handsome sum of .£94,000 in
dividends to its Shareholders.
Attention is drawn to the great revival which has already taken place in copper-mining, and in
the values of copper shares. A short time ago, Nymagee shares were practically unsaleable, now their
,£1 shares paid up to ss. are readily dealt in at 565. ; and in the same period Great Cobar shares have
increased in value from Is. 6d. to over 30s.

When Abercorn won Vic. Sires Produce Stakes
It Was only by a head from the Musket Maxim. On Satur-
day Goil had a much easier task than his dad, having no
finishing opposition. Fucile, who beat The Officer, is a
green Carbine who wants time.
• * * *
Old Whakawatea isn’t apparently his once
junior self. Even Hales, who can’t be much this side of
50, couldn't bang him to the front. Hales’ last New-
market was on Hova, when he landed a swell stake.
* * * *
Courallie seems to be a second-place horse. His
first notable second was to Hopscotch, in Epsom Handi-
cap, and his last (the fifth) in the Newmarket.
♦ » * *
Adelaide people have been smitten with horse
fever since Auraria brought Melb. Cup there. Foliage
and Thunderer carried a heap of S.A. money in the New-
market, but weren't class. The little syndicate that fore-
stalled the Foliage stable got a lesson in turf ethics.
Follow a stable lead ; never lead a stable.
* * * *
Oakleigh Plate is a fair Newmarket tip fur-
nisher ; Hova, second in ’94, won the six furlongs after-
wards ; Vanitas, second last year, ran second at Fleming-
ton ; and Maluma, second this year, won. Her sire—
Malua, William Tell, and Wild' Rose, Oakleigh Plate
winners, scored in the V.R.C. dash in their respective
years.
* * * *
Maluma only won one race before the New-
market. Her brother had little to his credit before he
scooped Melb. Cup. Yet both started favorites.
* * * *
Maluma’s two last performances stamp Coil as
a slashing youngster. After having bad luck, he beat her
at Caulfield. Then she donkey-licks 36 of a Newmarket
field, and he leisurely appropriates the Sires Produce
Stakes.
* * * *
Lawyer O’Halloran’s libel-action against jour-
nalist Champion was nipped by the Vic. Attorney-General,
who declined to file a bill. The Attorney-General's
opinion about O’Halloran’s case can only be gauged by his
action. A conviction was improbable/ Which reflection
is a poor salve for O’Halloran’s injured feelings.
* * * *
Had Maluma not been outheaded by Coil in
Oakleigh Plate, she would have been penalised for the
Newmarket,, and, after her. Saturday’s unextended achiev-
ment, curiosity is expressed as to how. she would have
shaped with a penalty.
* * * *
Fully a dozen neddies were ridden by Holdhard
at Caulfield, but the potato-eyed stewards said nothing.
Strange to say, they heard nothing either. The idea of
dropping on a real trier, who won, was ludicrous, but
quite in keeping with the traditions of Caulfield heath.
* * * *
The best two-year-old, Sydney side, is un-
doubtedly Tird, the M. L.-bred youngster (half-brother to
Tirailleur, killed in Glenloth’s Melb. Cup). If looks and
pace go for anything he’s another Carbine.
* * * «
It was' not only the anticipated stiff running
that kept the public away from Caulfield autumn meeting,
which resulted in loss. Oakleigh Plate and Purse aren’t
sufficiently attractive from a spectacular point of view.
The distances are too short for big handicaps. People
like sitting back in suspense and gazing at something
longer—a cross between, say, a mile-and-a-quarter and a
Chinese opera.
* * * *
St. Alban’s Wilson is going ’Ome to recuperate,
see the Derby, and secure a couple of new stud strains on
which he has long had an eye.
* * * *
Some of the ring were quite prepared for
Preston’s defeat of the odds-on Harvester in last year’s
Leger. Bookies are generally ready for surprises. The
public aren’t. When Cabin Boy cantered home on Satur-
day even his leviathan owner was flabbergasted. He never
lays his own horses, but no one fancied his hack this time,
anyhow.
Twenty to one was Cabin Boy’s quoted starting-
price. What would a tote have paid ?
A hundred yards is an awful start when the
leader’s nearest opponent has to round a turn at top.
How rider Gough must have kicked himself for his St.
Leger error 1 Look at the great ovation he missed, too,
riding in an easy winner on the idolised Wallace 1
Riding according to instructions isn’t always
the game, unless a jock’s instructions are to ride stiff. A
pilot should follow instructions and go “ on his own ” as
well.
So far as Adelaide is concerned the lesson of the
Newmarket is that Foliage is always green in autumn.
« * ’» *
The N.S.W. v. S. Aus. match will be memor-
able for Turner’s reversion to brilliant form with the ball.
He bowled nearly all day for 35 runs and got six wickets.
The climax came when oniy one S. Aus. wicket had to
fall, and Giffen, having cut a ball, ran only one instead of
two in order that he might face Turner himself.
Nemesis ! Giffen’s wicket was scattered second ball, and
the Sydney crowd roared itself hoarse for Turner. At
time of going to press the match seemed a very good
thing for N.S.W. S. Aus. had made4oo and 200; N.S.W.
first innings, 428, an 1 103 in the second innings for one
wicket (Mackenzie’s).
* * * *
Lyons has one chance of being included in the
English trip, and that is—Buckley’s.
* * * *
TheTas. giant Eady has kept his hand in during
the last few weeks. His club batting-average has been
considerably over the century.
* * * «
The S.A. bowling last Monday was generally
weak stuff. Nearly 30 full-pitchers were bowled before
luncheon ; no wonder Iredale made 187 ! Iredale’s big
score is chiefly valuable in proving that he has plenty of
pluck to fight an uphill game—if proof is necessary after
his 133 and 140 against Stoddart’s team last year. This 137
follows directly on two successive innings for 140 and 124.
» • • »
Several members of the present Australian
Eleven are against the inclusion of a fourteenth. “ Three
men always to watch a match,” remarks veteran A. Ban-
nerman, “ three men to always grumble, and get ill drink-
ing lemonade.”
* * * *
After Kelly’s grand wicket-keeping against S.
Aus.—not a sundry against him In a score of 400—several
of the certainties for England are asking whether it isn’t
a mistake to take Johns and leave Kelly.
Percy M‘Donnell, who captains the No. 2
eleven in Australia v. Australia, thinks his side ought to
win if it wins the toss.
This week’s contest between the first and
second Australian elevens gives rise to reflection. Sup-
pose, as is possible, the English team is beaten, that
Turner takes 9 wickets for 20 runs, M'Kibbin 3 for 100,
and Jones gets 20) scored off him for nix. That Kelly, at
his best, takes four wickets by lightning stumping, ami
Johns, off his nerve, mulls his chances ; while Mackenzie,
with the bat, scores more than all the first eleven put
together, how shall we feel ? What sort of “conquering
hero-don’t-want-to fight ” tune can the picked ones sing
when they board the steamer; and, more vital still,
what sort of draw will a defeated Australian eleven be in
England? The match will be very interesting from a
sporting standpoint ; but, after all, what is sport ? What
is anything, in comparison to Gate ?

Bob Fitzsimmons, the Maoriland pug., is a game
fighter who learned much in Sydney froir Peter Jackson.
Corbett, who waited sixtv rounds for Jackson, selected
Maher as his proGgd, and'handed him the championship ;
Fitzsimmons, Jackson’s pupil, only needed sixty seconds
to take that honor from Mr. Maher, who was said to have
greatly improved since he was beaten by Fitzsimmons in
a dozen rounds three or four years ago —a nasty jar for
“Gentleman Jim.” Maher is a man who, when called on
by his friends in several bitter goes, always shone brilli-
antly. But in Fitz. he met a scientist who “ waits.”’ And,
seemingly, the waiting champion is the fighting champion,
and as Fitz. is now an excellently-dressed personage, with
a good little hoard, it is time to assume the title of “ Gen-
tleman Bob,” Champion of the World. Yet Fitzsimmons,
as a world-beater, is a mystery to Sydneyites. Of his
cleverness there was never any doubt, but his ability to
stand punishment, and come round after being dazed,
puzzles people. The writer recollects seeing the red-
haired blacksmith set-to one afternoon with Hick Sandall.
Of course the long man got all over the stocky weight-lifter
for a few rounds, and then Fitz. suggested “a willing one”
for a finish. Towards the end Sandall landed a heavy
right on Fitz’s ribs, when the latter turned green and
looked as if another punch would have knocked him clean
out. Maher and Fitzsimmons are of one height, sft.
lljin., but the former is 101 b. heavier, and the champion
seven years older. Fitz. has a bigger chest and waist
measurement, but in thighs, biceps, calves, forearm and
wrists, the Irishman has the advantage.
* * *
Ever since the new fistic champion Fitzsimmons
knocked four men out in one night at Timaru (M.L.), and
five the next, he has had a great career—has never been
beaten, unless a “ doubtful ” with Jim Hall can be counted.
It can’t. Once, too, he drew with Choynski, but played a
veritable tattoo on the clever Hebrew, and should have got
the verdict.
Three years ago, Fitz. walked the stone-broke
plank having lost his thousands through bad-management
and speculation. He swore then he’d never employ an-
other manager, but found he couldn’t do without. It re-
quires something more than a wife to keep a boxer straight.
Had Slavin set up as a temperance-model before
he left Australia, he might have been champion to-day.
Bragging Corbett, when he first received Fitz.’s
challenge, tried to bluff by offering to back Creedon
against him for £lOOO. The blacksmith had beaten Cree-
don, and has now twice downed Corbett’s second nomina-
tion - ,
During the recent cricket-match between a
Southern °Tasmanian team and Mr. Wood’s Eleven, the
Southern Tasmanian Cricket Association achieved some
sort of curious distinction. Mr. Wood (the husband of
Miss Essie Jenyns) is a decent fellow, quite apart from the
indirect celebrity his wife brought him. He pays all the
expenses of his team, and takes no percentage of the gate-
money, the fees, &c. The S.T.C.A. collected the ordin-
ary fees of admission to the ground from the wives of
such of the visiting players as are married, and in other
directions failed in hospitality and courtesy.
The Year-Book of Australia, containing a
vast mass of information about almost every-
thing Australian, has just come to hand. One
excellent feature about this publication is its
exceeding up-to-dateness. It presents a very large
amount of valuable detail about Australian in-
dustries, finances, Governments, railways, public
departments ; mining, banking, insurance, sport-
ing, commercial, legal, military, educational and
ecclesiastical matters ; and almost everything else
from the averages of Australian elevens to the
amount of dog-fees collected in Melbourne ; and
all these facts, corrected up to the end of ’95, are
published by the end of February : 9G. If the pro-
prietors would only extend this valuable work to
cover Tasmania and Maoriland as well as Aus-
tralia, it would be practically perfect. Tasmania
especially, as a province of which wonderfully
little is known on this continent, should be
included.


A Useful Hint for Gentlemen.
Custom is second nature—it has a mechanical
effect upon the body, an extraordinary influence
upon the mind. We grow fond of doing what we
are accustomed to, and our employments become
our diversions. Our taste is never better pleased
than with things which at first were objectionable
to us. Did you enjoy the first glass of claret or
beer or whisky you ever tasted ? What about
your first smoke? Yet habit has made you an
inveterate smoker, and you couldn’t while away
your time without your pipe or your cigar. The
great point in habits is not to give way to them
injudiciously. Be particular about the quality of
your wine and the brand of your weeds. You
may depend upon one thing—that you will not go
wrong if you smoke Frossard’s Cavour Cigars—
mild and aromatic, sold in packets of 8 for Is.
Let not outward appearances deceive you. They
look rough, the shape is unusual, but the quality
is there, and that is the essential.*
DEATH.
From mixing phosphorised pollard at Inver-
cargill, New Zealand, last April, three men were
seized with illness ; one was prostrated for a week,
another recovered in three or four days, and the
third man died. At the inquest the jury returned
a verdict “ Death was caused by phosphorous fumes
whilst phosphorisina pollard for rabbit-poisoning ; ”
and a rider was added urging the Government to
supervise the manufacture of phosphorised pre-
parations.*
Messrs. W. H. Glen and Co., the well-
known musical warehousemen, of Melbourne,
have established a branch of their business at 3)9
George-street, Sydney, an enterprise to be con-
ducted with the same spirit of liberality and
straightforwardness which has won the firm such
prosperity and respect in the Southern province.
Of course, the new branch has been primarily
established to supply the wants of the considerable
clientele already existent in N.S.W.*
THE RABBIT PEST IN VICTORIA.
The Waranga Shire Council at last meeting
instructed the engineer to procure three “ Ringer”
poison-distributing machines. They are known to
be the most practical and economic means of des-
troying rabbits yet out, or likely to come out.
The machine works in the roughest country ; the
size of the bait laid is under the control of the
driver ; one thousand acres can be scarified with
furrows and seventy thousand baits dropped in a
day, at seventy-five per cent, less cost than any
other means. *
Warner’s Safe Cure.—The annual re-
port of the Warner’s Safe Cure Co. for the year
ending July 31st, 1895, shows .£32,078 Is. 3d.
available for dividends. This report is certainly
satisfactory, and shows that the old-time pros-
perity of the Safe Cure business is rapidly return-
ing under the management of the English Board
of directors. No doubt both the Australasian trade
and the publishers will be pleased to learn that
the Australian branch will now receive special
attention under the management of Mr. H. W.
Calkins, who was previously associated, for nearly
four years, with the introduction of this business
in Australia, and whose experience has been
ripened since that time by having charge of the
advertising department of this company in Great
Britain. Mr. Calkins is expected to arrive in
Sydney about 1st March.*

E3^

This
Very
Carefully.
An important change is pend-
ing in the well-known Drapery
business of Messrs. Manning
and Co., Collins-st., Melbourne.
For some time past Mr. R.
Manning has been Sole Pro-
prietor, and quite recently has
had very tempting proposals of
partnership from a friend of his
in Scotland. These are likely
to result in a combination of
talent and capital which will be
calculated to produce a large and
rapid expansion of business.
While negotiations are proceed-
ing, opportunities of reducing
stock and collecting book-debts
are being made the most of, and
(Messrs. (Manning & <Qo.
are making a most determined
effort to do both in the most
practical way possible. Their
warehouse has been closed for a
whole day, and every article in
their choice Stock has been RE-
MARKED and heavily dis-
counted. Each Department
must reduce its Stock by a cer-
tain sum, and one or two depart-
ments (notably the Boot Depart-
ment) are to be closed altogether,
in order to make room for the
extension of the COSTUME,
MANTLE and MILLINERY
DEPARTMENTS. Ladies
would do well to drop in here
during the progress of this
genuine
Partnership Sale.
MANNING & CO.,
OH THE BLOCK,
Collins Street, Melbourne.

The Equitable Life-Assurance Society of
the United States has been under examination by
the Insurance Department of the State of New
York, and the result of the investigation is
summed up by the superintendent, in his report to
the Legislature, in the following words: —“ The
examination has been of the most searching and
exhaustive character. The officers and directors
have given every facility to the department during
the progress of the examination. The securities
owned and held as collateral were examined and
counted. The cash was balanced and verified.
The same diligence and exacting care was applied
to all the categories of assets. The valuation of
the liabilities has been made in the most careful
way, nothing being taken for granted, and abso-
lutely independent computations being made in
every case., It affords me profound satisfaction to
find that all the affairs of this vast insurance cor-
poration are in a sound and prosperous conditi< m.
The policy-holders of the Equitable may be satis-
fied that their interests are guarded by conscien-
tious and skilled officers and directors, whose
integrity is a guarantee of fidelity, and whose long
experience qualifies them to conduct the business
in a manner best calculated to conserve the true
interests of the policy-holders.” Mr. C. Carlisle
Taylor, for some years a member of the head-office
(Staff in New York, has recently been appointed
general manager in Australasia for the society.

Mr. Charles Stephenson, a well-known
Railway Employe at Kaiapoi,
New Zealand, writes:
“About ten years ago, while engaged in
shunting, my foot caught between the rails,
and my leg was fractured below the knee.
It healed in time, but I have been troubled
ever since with swollen veins, and have been
obliged, at times, to wear a bandage. About
a year ago it became much worse, and I
feared I should be obliged to give up my
work. A friend advised me to try Ayer’s
Sarsaparilla. I did so, and after taking four
bottles the swelling disappeared, and I have
not been troubled with it since.”

The other day, James Healey was picked
up in Swanston-street, Melb., much damaged, and
was wheeled away to the hospital. There he was
reported to be suffering from superficial bruises,
and ordered to gaol. In gaol he died from a badly
fractured skull. He was only a ship’s cook, and—
well, everybody knows who sends cooks.
Trial Bay, writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent, is probably the nearest approach to a
pashalik that can be found in Australia. Save
for a nominal and perfunctory supervision exer-
cised from Sydney, at a distance of about 200
miles, the superintendent is as absolute as, say
President Kruger, and enjoys many interesting
privileges. The Comptroller-General of Prisons
(qy., why not Inspector-General ?) pays an official
visit once or twice every three years ; he managed,
for the first time in 18 months, to put in an ap-
pearance seven weeks ago, the Thetis being pro-
vided for the special accommodation of himself and
his friends. He walked around the establishment,
brought up to an abnormal state of cleanliness for
the occasion, heard requests from prisoners for
leave to petition, mostly returned the stereotyped
answer, “ No grounds,” and vanished—for another
18 months or two years ? The prison, a strong
structure of granite, the cost of which made a
big hole in <£loo,ooo, stands on the narrow mole
or peninsula from which the breakwater starts.
It is 25 miles from Kempsey, and is completely
isolated from the outer world by the ocean on one
side, and mountain, impenetrable thicket, and
swamp on the other. Parson Meekin, of “His
Natural Life,” would piously extol Trial Bay as a
place designed by Providence for a penal settle-
ment. M’sP. never come there, unless involun-
tarily ; educated men naturally shrink from collect-
ing experiences in such a place ; and the occasional
travelling reporter is easily hocussed by ex parte
official statements re the administration of the
prison and the blissful contentment of the inmates.
Apart from every other consideration, and irre-
spective altogether of individual grievances, it
would seem that an enquiry by the Public Service
Board is necessary in the interests of economy.
Trial Bay has escaped the knife of retrenchment
because of its inaccessibility. The public of
N.S.W. would be appalled at the extravagant
expenditure involved in the maintenance of the
establishment on its present footing. A superin-
tendent, a deputy-superintendent, clerk and
schoolmaster, a chief warder, and 18 subordinate
warders, are paid to keep the average of less
than 100 men in order. These men—all, be it
remembered, doing the last portion of their
sentences under conditions of relaxed discipline—
are, with few exceptions, employed outside under
the Harbors and Rivers Department in quarrying,
loading and discharging trucks of stone, stripping,
&c., for eight hours daily, and are, of course, more
orderly than any similar number of free men who
have access to liquor. They are so tired, so
divided in interest, that mutinous conspiracies are
naturally out of the question. The time they
have to serve is so insignificant, and the objections
and obstacles to escape are so obvious, that the
outer gate might with safety be left open all
night. As it is, only three warders are on duty
each night, and the doors of cells and huts are
never locked. Three or four escapes have been re-
corded in eight years, and by consensus of opinion
the recaptured ones were dubbed fools, and went
back to gaol for fresh terms without having even
the barren sympathy of their quondam associates.
Muster, muster, muster is the principal business
within doors ; and for the performance of
this important function the country pays
the superintendent <£425 p.a., deputy-superin-
tendent, <£22o ; clerk and schoolmaster (with
no pupils to teach), <£lso ; senior warder
<£lBs ; and the 18 other warders the full rate
which they would be entitled to receive in Dar-
linghurst according to their grade. In addition to
their pay, the officials, including the resident sur-
geon (<£3so) draw full rations of bread and meat
for themselves and wives, and of bread for each
child. Each little angel in transit from the stars
to this world of sin and struggle is as sure of a
square meal while pa wears the blue as if he or
she were a Guelph. Why this extraordinary dis-
tinction is made between the city warder and the
T.B. warder is an inscrutable mystery. Perhaps
the ration concession is a solatium for living in
such an ultima thule. But existence at the Bay
carries for warders its own advantages. There are
no shops and no pubs. There are even no churches.
There is no opportunity for spending money. The
boundary-rider, a gate-keeper, and one other
“ screw ” are on police duty during the time the
establishment is empty, and for the rest the duties
are of the most trifling description. Consequently,
each warder has taken reverentially to himself
the command given unto Abraham. The fresh
sea air, the freedom from care, the absence of
stays and of the accursed drink, all combine to
assist his devout resolution, and the obstetrical
branch of the doctor’s business flourishes while
disease itself decays. This Arcadian condition of
things long ago arrested the attention of Mr.
Brownrigg, the late Harbors and Rivers engineer,
and it is understood that in a special report he
undertook, if six artillerymen were sent down and
placed under his command, to save this enormous
waste of public money, and still carry on the inter-
minable work of constructing the breakwater.
But the suggestion was not accepted, Mr. Brown-
rigg ultimately accepted another post in the Govt,
service, and the system of wasteful expenditure
under a divided control has been continued up to
the present time.

A Self-Made Man.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was his boast that he began
His life in squalid ignorance,
To learn he never had a chance
(He seemed to think it a romance),
This Self-Made Man.
Then when upon his chin the down
Commenced to show, across the seas
He came, his native land to please,
And landed in “ the colonies ”
With half-a-crown.
And after forty years or so
Of playing at the griping game
A full-blown Fatman he became ;
Yet still he had the very same
Half-crown to show.
And so he rose to wealth and power
On that half-crown—’twas ever thus—
While we, who have no overplus
Of coin—it would have lasted us,
Say, half-an-hour.
Vast-paunched, with pride-congested head,
He’d rise at banquets—chiefly free —
And tell this yarn unceasingly
Till all who heard it wished that he
Would drop down dead.
He walked about, as in a trance,
Self-hypnotised by his own worth—
His waistband girdled all on earth
He cared for—’twas a goodly girth
Of arrogance.
He died, as even Fatmen do
(Thank Heaven !) and heaved a husky sigh
As though to say, “ Poor world—good-bye !
I know that you will miss me—l
Feel sad for you ! ”
And when before the Judgment Seat
He came, no whit abashed was he,
But said to his Creator —“ We,
O Lord, I reckon, will agree—
Our views will meet.”
“How?’’said the Lord, “presumptuousclod?”
He answered—“ Thus are cases fit—
A self-made Man am I, to wit,
And you, Lord, are, you nrast admit,
A Self-made God.”
C. R.
Gr. 11. Sims tells two good English yarns in
a recent Referee. A young parson was called
upon to take the place of a sick chaplain at the
county gaol on the morning of an execution. The
condemned criminal, a notoriously hard case,
didn’t want to see the new chaplain, any more
than he had cared to listen to the old one, but at
last the novice managed to get alongside his spiri-
tual charge and ask him what he was thinking
about in this solemn hour of death. The mur-
derer objected to the question, on the ground that
his bloomin’ thoughts were his own business.
“My friend,” said the verdant shepherd, “as a
servant of God it is my duty, my business, to do
all I can for you.” This was playing right into
the hands of the impenitent sheep. He gazed
sternly at his tormentor and replied : “You’re a
servant of God, are you ? Then look’ee ’ere, young
feller, you’d better be blanky careful what you say
to me, for in half-an-hour I shall be seeing your
guv’nor.” Sims’s other anecdote came from the
lunatic-asylum, where jockey Giles—known as a
“chalk jockey ” in his time, because he invariably
rode stiff ’uns —was confined. One of the punting
warders (all warders are horsey-minded) came to
Giles for an opinion on the chance of Sprite for
the Chester Cup. “ What do you think about
Sprite?” asked the warder, “I think,” said the
insane jock., “ that if you’ve backed that to win
the Cup, you ought to be the patient in this
asylum, and I ought to be taking care of your
money.”

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS,
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-out No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ TIHZIELIEIE] CASTLES” and “ CAPSTAIT.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “ Three Castles” and (< Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. (Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)
