A Melbourne woman has been committed
for trial charged with an almost entirely new
crime. According to the evidence on which the
committal was made, she had had a disagreement
with a young man over “another girl,’’ and pro-
ceeded to get level with him by registering the
birth of an imaginary infant of which he was said
to be the father. She didn’t intend to blackmail
him, only to “ let people know what a bad man he
was.” If the case is proved this Melbourne
woman has opened up an interesting and hitherto
much-neglected department of crime. “Conceal-
ment of birth ” is old enough, but publication of
birth which didn’t take place is almost new.
Australian Anarchist Andrews, in Reason,
notes the curious trifles on which the introverted
mind of a gaol-prisoner feeds: —
The eagerness with which, at Biloela, I took every
opportunity of posting myself on an elevation for a smail
outlook upon the harbor and its shores ; the relish with
which, after familiarising myself almost with the indi-
vidual peculiarities of every grain of sand in the face of
the sandstone wall, I observed on one occasion the exis-
tence of a moth’s egg in one of the tool-marks; the magni-
fied contrast of colors which I strained my eyes to dis-
cover in the stones of Darlinghurst, dull and uniform-
tinted to casual view ; the critical scanning of a cell I had
not been in before, for a different distribution of the bug-
marks, a different scaling down in the whitewash, a differ-
ent brightening of the bolt and edges of the inner door by
friction; the delight afforded by the discovery of a red
featherwhichthewindhadcarriedoverthewall; the intense
interest with which I watched the behaviour of a sparrow’s
feather caught in an eddy, and the train of reflections to
which it gave rise on the subject of aerial navigation ; the
individual identification of every sparrow that came to
feed in our yard, and the naming of them after the
warders according to their bodily peculiarities; the excru-
ciating agony of interest with which the first turning on
of the electric-light in the gaol was watched ; the strange
sensations produced by a political meeting, and the play-
ing of a band near the gaol; then the growing indiffer-
ence to these things, as to any other impressions which
did not subserve the imposed routine ; the tendency to
“ brown study ” and passive vacuity—just as when, being
without food for four days, as happened several times to
me in the past, I would pass from the eager picking up of
individual crumbs of the size of a pin’s head, to a loss of
appetite and loathing of food, and, when I got the means
to buy some, could not bring myself to eat it except by
resorting to stimulants.

Sunshime and Hain.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was hot, with a dazzling, roasting heat
that baked the ground, scorched the grasses, and
dried, up the water-holes, and from one end of the
great West to the other there was not to be seen
a blade of green herbage, a running creek, or a fat
beast of any kind. The sun—red, wrathful—cast
a bloodshot eye everywhere: north, south, east
and west.
M‘Allister drove along the monotonous road
leading from Lambing Downs to his own home-
stead, Bumbarri. On each side ran for miles and
miles a dusty, straggling fence. It extended until
the dazzling heat-waves permitted the eye no
longer to follow it; as with the road, it was lost
in a misty simmering.
The two dust-grimmed horses with sweaty flanks
wearily tugged at the light buggy, and M‘Allister
gazed at the dry herbage upon the plains, thinking
of the mischief a stray spark would make; this
same thought had been his companion all the
journey, and had caused him to forego his pipe.
Suddenly he jerked the horses to a standstill. A
lone, ragged figure sat upon a swag in the road-
way. An old slouched hat was pulled over the
eyes, and the head was bowed upon two grimy
hands.
“ Hallo ! ” cried M‘Allis ter.
The head, lacking an eye, looked lazily up.
“ Going to camp here ? ” asked M'Allister.
“ I spec so, boss,” said the one-eyed man.
A vision of a fire boiling a billy flashed through
M'Allister’s mind with horrible vividness. Then
he said :
“ Better chuck your swag in the back and jump
up. I’ll drive you to the homestead. Rations
and the travellers’-hut will be better than here.”
The sundowner looked momentarily surprised.
His single optic wandered over the land reflec-
tively. Then he smiled as one who knows °nd
climbed into the buggy.
* * * •
Six months later. The string of water-
holes upon which Bumbarri was built had become
a rushing, roaring stream. The rain still poured
down continuously, and was likely to do so for
some time ; and M‘Allister, sitting on the veran-
dah, looked contentedly at the flood.
The sun had not been seen that day, and the
evening was gloomy before its time. A wet, be-
draggled figure carrying a bluey loomed through
the mist and stood on the edge of the opposite
bank.
“ Hello !” it hailed.
“ Well ? ” yelled M'Allister across the water.
“ Row us across in the dingy, boss.”
“It’s that old ‘whaler’ that’s always about,”
said M'Allister to himself. Then aloud—“ Go to
the devil !”
“ I don’t think he can do much harm this
weather,” he murmured, going inside to his tea.
The man with the swag (he had but one eye)
cursed to himself, then to his swag, and finally to
the universe. After which he got under a log and
made himself as comfortable as possible for the
night.
• * * *
Next summer M'Allister was burnt out—
lock, stock, and barrel. No one knew how the
fire originated. Some alleged, however, that a
man with one eye had camped in No. 4 paddock
the night before. But that was merely hearsay.
Nevertheless M'Allister, girthing up one of the
few horses remaining to him, prior to departing to
file his schedule, was heard to murmur—
“ Perhaps it would have paid better if I had
rowed that one-eyed devil across that night.”
Perhaps it would,
H. A. CONANT.
The recent diplomatic difficulty with Hol-
land has brought Canning’s couplet into common
use with loyal papers :
In matters commercial the fault of the Dutch
Is giving too little and asking too much.
And how true it is, too, the other way about:—
In matters commercial the British have much
The same little tricks as their neighbors, the Dutch.
Fireworks from the first issue of Hydera-
bad (India) Times :
We start this paper by the help of the Supreme Being,
who has illuminated the Roof over our heads with num-
bers of golden globes, which never require any cleaning
and shine equally upon everything without making any
distinction between them. In the same manner he has
decorated the Earthern thrones with sparkling jewellery
crowns, in whose light the two classes of animals live
comfortably and peacefully ; out of those crowns there is
only one which is most popular, valuable, and substantial,
the shadow of which is called universally a “ perfect light,”
the person who takes protection under it becomes very
lucky and remains safe for ever ; the well-known owner of
it is called Her Majesty the Empress of India.
It sounds like the pronouncement of a black Dan
O’Connor.
England has several times bitten off more
of South Africa than she could chew, and at
least once had recourse to prayer in order to
obviate lockjaw. Having got into an unholy
mess with the natives, the Cape Govt, proclaimed
Xmas Day, ’5l, as “a day of solemn humiliation
and prayer before God.” But things got no better
very fast. Then the Devil took a hand. Gov.
Smith was recalled, and Cathcart set to work.
Fifteen months of Kaffir-killing and Basuto
butchery ended, in ’53, in a re-establishment of
peace. And the result was claimed as a direct
answer to prayer.
A young Melbourne damsel got a severe
shock to her nerves, recently, from beholding
Undertaker Sleight for the first time. Just pre-
vious to the death of her aged uncle, which hap-
pened two or three years ago, she had a vivid,
gruesome dream, as follows She saw a short,
thick-set man, with his head under his arm, enter
her uncle’s room, tap the old gentleman on the
shoulder, and lead him downstairs into the garden.
In the garden there was a deep hole dug. The
dreamer saw her uncle dragged to the edge of the
Eit by the strange visitor, who said, from under
is arm, “ Dust to Dust!” in a sepulchral tone.
After which he toppled the venerable relative
down the cavity, and the young lady awoke
screaming. A fortnight later her uncle’s funeral
was transacted by Augustus Sleight, but the
dreamer, not being acquainted with the personality
of that famous undertaker, attached no psycho-
logical import to his connection with the melan-
choly event. It was not until the other day that
she saw, walking down Collins-street, the muti-
lated vision who had called for uncle in her dream.
Sleight was the aforesaid harbinger of death.
Augustus is evidently the sort of coming event
that casts a shadow before, and The Bulletin
will be glad to hear suggestions as to why his
spectre should unscrew its head. The great
original wears his head, and pours refreshments
down his neck, quite the same as every other
man.
Dear Bulletin, —A little W.A. experi-
ence. On Jan. 15 I and two mates making for
Roebourne struck a telegraph-station not 100 miles
from that city of pretty little Jap. women. Not a
soul about. Notice on the door: “ Gone to land-
ing ; back in half-an-hour,” Waited; no fresh
tracks coming or going ; camped and took posses-
sion. All doors open; keys in desks, drawers,
cupboards; telegrams, &c., open for anyone to
inspect; watches and chains lying about; clothes
hanging on line ; bread risen in tins ready for the
oven ; meat in bucket boiled to shreds. Obviously
a helter-skelter evacuation. We wondered why.
Overhauled things ; found last .wire:—“ 4J inches
rain ; river a banker; still raining ; leave for the
hills.” Now, we, who arrived, as stated, on Jan,
15th, had no rain since Jan. 11th. The telegraph
staff, we found out afterwards, retreated Jan. 12.
On Jan. 17 one man came back ; Jan. 18 brought
another; and on Jan. 20 the third and last turned
up. Now, if I and my mates and others can be
depended on, the river was never a banker, and
never came within 15 feet of the telegraph- station
level. Therefore, ? ? ? ? ? ?— Prospector.
A £30,000 museum and public library is
to be built in Perth (W.A.) on the site of an old-
time gaol. Thus the State now seeks to elevate
the mind on the spot where it used to elevate the
body.
Dear Bulletin, —Niggers’ “justice” in
Westralia. Time: last Dec. Place : inland from
Roebourne. White man has a native down on
the ground, thrashing him for some trifling affair.
Native manages to struggle free. The first
thing he sees is a tomahawk, which he picks
up and throws at his flogger, cutting him on the
leg. Native put on the chain and taken to town-
ship ; white taken to hospital. Native brought
before magistrate and sentenced to six months’
hard. Later in the day magistrate discovers
native will have to be sent to Roebourne for that
term. Native brought up again, and sentence
altered to one month and 25 lashes ! Flogging
duly comes off. Audience —his original assailant
(carried on a stretcher to the place of punishment);
magistrate ; publican, who brings a couple of “ his
natives ” to show them how bad boys are dealt
with ; the policeman who cried out the number of
strokes ; and alleged-white-man flogger who did
the job for 10s. Talk about British Chartered Co.
and Matabeles; Boers and Kaffirs. Bah ! — Crab.
JOE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I’ll ask ’im for a rise, blowed if I don’t,” said
Joe ; and he proceeded to his employer’s room.
“ Good mornin’, Mr. Dingle; I’ve come to ask
for a rise. Been ’ere nine months now, sir; think
I’ve given satisfaction, sir.”
“Rise, Joe?” said Mr. Dingle. “Nonsense,
my boy, times are really too bad, and, besides ”
but Joe was gone.
“ Damn ’im ! I knew it was no good.”
Joe did very little work that morning ; he was
thinking hard. When Mr. Dingle summoned
him to go to the bank, he started and blushed.
With £5O in gold, Joe set out, and, when he
had passed the bank, he glanced nervously right
and left.
“ It must be done,” he said ; “ I’ve got to clear
out. Damn the totes ! ” Joe went to the railway
station ; but when he found there was no train
for an hour he returned towards Circular Quay.
“I’ll get aboard one o’ them sailing vessels,” he
said.
Then a new idea struck him, and he set off for
Parramatta, where he would get a train without
being seen.
Nervousness again seized him and he became
very depressed. He began to wish he hadn’t
bolted, and the tears came to his eyes. A ’bus
went past, and Joe ran to catch it.
“ Stop thief ! ” cried a newsboy, and Joe nearly
fell with fright. He saw a policeman in the ’bus,
and ran back.
“ Damn’yer ! ” yelled out the driver.
“ It’s no good. I’ll give myself up,” said Joe,
and he hurried towards Mr. Dingle’s office. As
he approached the bank it occurred to him to pay
in the money, and he did so.
“ That you, Joe ? ” said Mr. Dingle.
“Yes, sir,” said Joe, as he entered his em-
ployer’s office with downcast eyes.
“ I’ve been thinking about that rise,” said Mr.
Dingle, good-humoredly; “7s. 6d. is rather small,
and I’ll give you 12s. 6d. from to-day. Pay in
that money, Joe? Ah, I see you have the receipt.
You’re crying, boy. What’s wrong? Not well?”
“ No, sir,” said Joe as he left the room.
Sydney.
EDWARD RAY.
Port Wine Mary .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Nor a pedigree girl. No one knew whence sht
came, but the boys saw that she was “all there,”
and, like Eve in Eden, the only woman on the
diggings. The impression she left upon the minds
of all beholders was that of a charming but incon-
gruous picture combination of black hair, great,
stag-like eyes, luscious lips, print dress, and man’s
coat and hard-hitter hat. The two last were once
the property of Fred, the bailiff of the diggings.
Nature has so constructed what is called the
weaker sex that even the courtesan has loving
predilections ; and the bailiff of the early diggings
is not the Uriah Heep-like sneak of more advanced
civilisation.
When Mary heard from one of the gang who
sought her favor that Burgess and Sullivan in-
tended to stick up Fred when on his way, coin-
laden, from Donnelly’s to Ross, she determined
that Fred should be absent and that she herself
would be there. So she lied to Fred and told him
that she had seen the man he sought going up
Jones’ Creek, and thither he went. And then in
the grey dawn of the morning, hatted and coated,
as the komako sang his matin to the coming day,
Port Wine Mary went to tell the gang that their
victim had escaped—went to jeer at them, in
fact. The gang cursed her not. They chaffed
her on the cut of her coat and offered her
a drink, which she scornfully refused, and started
back for home. Rounding the flange of a
giant pukatea tree-trunk, whose upper branches
were gaudy with the ruddy bloom of the rata, a
fiery stream shot out from the under-scrub.
Port Wine Mary was never seen again on
Donnelly’s.
The gang worked on the principle that dead
men or women tell no tales, and, although it is
known that they robbed and murdered many, of
whom over twenty are buried between the Grey
and Hokitika, there is no living witness to their
crimes

His Lucky Hay.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The man sat at the table, writing; the woman by
the wide fireplace grinding maize in an old coffee-
mill. It was a slab-hut with the crevices between
the unhewn planks clay-plugged ; above, the bare
rafters, the inner surface of the bark roof browned
with smoke.
Seated on the earthen floor, two ragged children
were playing jacks with dried peach-stones—they
werenoisy and quarrelled over theirgame. Themill
kept up a regular scrunching sound, but the man
heeded it not ; his face beamed, the rapid move-
ment of his pen was interrupted only while he
gazed with illuminated eyes into the grey bush
seen from the open door. At times he smiled—the
triumphant smile of one victorious.
“ Ann,” he said, “ I have got an inspiration—
it’s simply wonderful. No one has ever thought
of it before ; it will make my name.”
“Yes, John,” answered the woman wearily,
“but don’t you think you had better dig some
ground for potatoes? I could beg a little seed
from my brother.”
“We are all Christians, Ann —all love one
another and believe the Bible. Why not call
delegates from the rich and poor together in con-
ference ; and, coming to a mutual understanding,
abolish poverty ? I would take the chair and see
fair play. It is bound to succeed; every man,
unless he is a fool or a rogue, must agree. It will
be the greatest event in the world’s history.”
DIDN’T CARE TO BE INSIDE.
KANGAROO : “Hello, Leo! have you seen anything of the keeper?"
LEO: “No. Can’t you come in and wait for him'!”
KANGAROO : “ No, thanks. He’d be more apt to bind me here.”.
“ Or couldn’t you hoe a few hills for pumpkins,
John? We have nothing to eat now, but hominy,
and goodness knows what we shall do when that
is gone.”
“ And, after the conference, my dear, they can
surely do no less than make me a paid executive-
officer, to carry out the scheme.”
“You know I do not understand these things.
Remember how you were going to abolish standing
armies, and prevent war, by writing to the Em-
perors of Germany, France, and Russia, and I
don’t know who else—and you never heard any
more of it. And the postage on those letters
would have bought us a bit of meal for dinner this
day.”
“No doubt I was sanguine, Ann, in saying they
would send for me at once. But they are thinking
it over. My reasons cannot fail to strike them.
We may have a royal courier coming for me
shortly. ”
“ I want something to eat, mother ! ” the boy
on the floor cried out.
“You must wait till the pudding is cooked,”
said the woman.
“ I don’t like puggy, it’s nasty ; I want bread,”
shouted the boy.
“ With butter on it,” whispered the girl, who,
though not crying herself, was willing to add her
moral support to the agitation.
“ I want bread, with butter on it,” screamed the
boy, raising his terms with alacrity.
The man continued to write—he heard nothing.
The woman, having finished her grinding, went
out in the bush to gather sticks. The child, find-
ing his outcry
unheeded, re-
lapsed into
sobs and his
game of jacks
again.
Presently
the woman re-
turned to the
doorway, tears
in her eyes,
a strange man
by her side.
“ It has come
at last, John.
I always said
it would
come ! ”
“ What is
it, Ann ? ” re-
plied the hus-
band, jumping
up.
‘ ‘ It’s a bailiff
from Stone,
the st or e-
keeper, you
gave the mort-
gage to. He
is tired of wait-
ing, and has
sent to take
possession,
and—and—we
must go. Now
see what all
your fine ideas
have brought
us to ! ”
“Don’t wor-
ry, my dear.
I always
thought we
were buried here in the bush ; let us go into
town, where there is more scope. Yes, more
scope,” he repeated, as though the word pleased
him.
The woman, with an exclamation of despair, ran
off to a neighbor’s, the crying children clinging to
her skirts.
The stranger entered the house and sat down.
Later, the wife came back with Robson, the next
selector, driving his horse and dray. On this
vehicle the scanty furniture and personal effects
were packed, the family took their seats on top,
and the dray moved town wards.
“ This is most unfortunate,” said the neighbor,
sympathetically.
“Yes,” said the man, “I have been trying to
impress on that bailiff the advantages to society of
the co-operative principle, but I never met anyone
so dense. He does not seem to realise the first
principles of political economy. This bush-life is
accursed. If a man has brains he becomes a
crank—l might even get that way myself if I
stopped here much longer—and with poor intellect
he is little better than the beasts that perish.’
The neighbor did not answer, but called to his
horse. . .
The man continued: “While I was waiting
there for the wife to come back, I got hold of a
splendid new idea—this must be one of my lucky
days. The whole world is at loggerheads:
each people has a different religion from
the others. And all this division over mere
paltry details of no importance. Why not
have a conference of the Archbishop of Can-
terbury, the Pope, and the head of the Russian
church, and by mutual concessions arrange a com-
mon faith? I am sure they only want to meet
together and talk matters over to settle all
disputes once and for all. It is a splendid concep-
tion ! ”
Robson snapped his whip. The woman, un-
heeding the talk, was looking back, gazing at the
neglected clearing, overgrown with wattles and
saplings, at the mean hut that had been their
home, at the picture of her past. The children,
delighted with the ride, were imitating the driver
and calling to the horse.
The man looked forward, his eyes glowing. He
did not see the bush-track, the dog-rail fence, or
the squalid signs of poverty he was leaving; before
him was unfolded a gorgeous panorama, a regal
procession of monarches in an empire city, with
banners and music, and splendid pomp. He was
himself seated in the midst in a golden chariot ac-
claimed by a multitude, a God amongst men.
Entranced, he took out a soiled pocket-book, and
with a beaming face noted the day of the month.
It was his lucky day.
N.S.W.

The anti-German feeling aroused by the
Kaiser’s message to President Kruger cannot be
realised by any one not on the soot,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent. “Such an
~,1 . „ -
outburst has never been known in the memory of
man, and quite puts into the shade the scenes at
the time of the expected war with Russia. There
has not been such a fighting spirit abroad since
the time of George 111., when England went to
work to squelch the French, and succeeded (with
some assistance). The funny incident of the Mel-
bourne people making the German bands play
Rule Britannia has given great satisfaction,
and if Fitzgibbon were here now he would have
no difficulty in floating a big Board of Works
loan.”

A Gambler’s Gratitude.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
That claim of ours at Fox’s was, in the opinion
of our gold-surfeited party, nearly worked out. It
had dwindled to lOdwt. a day per man when
Mick, the younger brother of Jimmy the gambler,
came along.
Mick was not much of a spieler, but a hard-
working digger, down on his luck, and seeking for
a job to raise tucker for a prospecting tour.
I turned my share in the claim over to Mick,
and went to town for a spell. That spell lasted
seven months, easily.
* * * *
I got into a poker-school at Ross. My dart
was a big Californian, who held much utu. I
wasn’t short of cash at the start, but luck was
against me.
At last, I got a full-hand, and anted-up on it
till the well ran quite dry, and I hadn’t a sovereign
to buck against the ’Frisco-man’s bluff. As my
hands went into my empty pockets in a sort of
hopeless plunge, my face must have told of my
despair.
Then something touched my right hand, and the
rustle of crisp paper whispered a song of hope into
my ear.
I grasped the note —a fiver—and played on.
My chip was taken in change for a larger note,
and when at last I scooped the big pool 1 hardly
noticed that my fortune-bringing fiver was not in
the heap.
It was Jimmy the Gambler, the brother of the
man I had given my claim to over at Fox’s, who
had passed me the note under the table. As I
raked in the pool a Small voice, which made up in
import what it lacked in volume, said :
“ Sonny, it’s a flash ’un ! ”
V * * *
Sixteen hours of fear and trembling
ensued. If my opponent, my particular opponent,
should tire of the game, or if I should happen to
go stone-broke, it meant —well, you know what it
meant on the diggings. I prayed and played my
best, and played on and on.
Just as a new day, breaking into the shanty,
began to make the lamplight look sickly, the
“ flash ’un ” came back to me, and I suddenly lost
all interest in the game.
I had saved at least a ducking in the muddy
creek.
* * * *
Though Jimmy the Gambler was one of
the right sort, his kindness was embarassing.
Maoriland.
